not return to the farm, as my grandfather had hoped someone would;
instead he became a rural doctor and dedicated himself to providing
affordable health care to the community, often footing the bill for
patients who could not afford to pay. He raised the funds to build a new
middle school in town and mentored many generations of local youth.

Uncle Gerald's generosity was matched by his intolerance of deviations
from the social norms that structured our family. When, as children, we
scraped our knees while working on the farm or recreating in the
mountains, we knew never to cry or complain for fear of being mocked.
When, as an adult, I wrote an op-ed in the Salt Lake Tribune urging
Mormons to stop paying tithing to the church until its leadership
reversed a homophobic policy, I learned through a cousin that Uncle
Gerald was furious that I would presume to tell him what to do.

In December 2020 Uncle Gerald died of COVID-19. On one level, his death,
like every coronavirus death, was an avoidable consequence of the
capitalist system that produced the pandemic and the state's failure to
manage it. At the same time, his personality and ideological
orientation, including his recklessness, his libertarian leanings, and
his fierce loyalty to his community, made him particularly vulnerable to
the virus and to premature death. When his friend, the local dentist,
got COVID-19 and requested a home visit, Uncle Gerald
went---maskless---without a second thought. When he contracted the virus
and developed symptoms, he chose to treat himself at home rather than go
to the hospital, and the first treatment he tried was
hydroxychloroquine. By the time he finally allowed my aunt to drive him
to the hospital, he could barely breathe.

I often wonder what might have happened to my family if we had
encountered a different form of solidarity in the wake of internment,
one that was not so closely tethered to the American Dream. Would Uncle
Gerald have lived a longer life? Could he have been a participant in the
Black Lives Matter movement, rather than a skeptical onlooker, afraid of
identifying too closely with what he called "victim mentality"? What
kind of an organization might allow my family to identify racial
capitalism as the root cause of our internment and my uncle's premature
death, and to find common cause with others who have an interest in
transforming that system? Perhaps another answer to Henry's question is
that I organize because the same shit that killed George Floyd killed my
Uncle Gerald, however more softly and however more self-inflicted, and
because I, too, want my family to survive.

The vision of togetherness animating Mormonism is one of permanent
unity---the formation of an "eternal family," whose persistence depends
on rigid hierarchies, the suppression of internal conflict, and the
exclusion of those who might threaten the group's stability. In this
respect, Mormonism resembles what political theorist Nathan DuFord, in
their recent book Solidarity in Conflict (2022), calls "antisocial
solidarity": forms of solidarity that ultimately undermine the necessary
conditions for common life by cultivating domination and exclusion.

What does it look like to organize without aspiring to unity or
permanence? DuFord argues that [solidarity]{.underline}, when it is
democratic, [is **always** premised on **disunity**]{.underline},
[because conflict]{.underline} within solidarity organizations [serves
as a **crucial check** on domination and exclusion]{.underline}. On this
point, DuFord articulates a version of the paradox of divergent
interests: "Often, solidarity organizations reproduce the exclusions and
injustices from the situation in which they are formed. This is not a
condemnation, but simply evidence that these organizations do not exist
outside the material context in which they are formed." Given this
reality, the only way to make such organizations less exclusive is for
members to agitate for change from within.

While we tend to associate neoliberalism with individualism and,
therefore, with selfishness and conflict over scarce resources, DuFord
shows that [neoliberalism actually tends to produce]{.underline} a
certain sort of **[consensus]{.underline}**. [Good neoliberal subjects
assume **agreement**]{.underline} about existing economic and political
arrangements [and leave each other **alone**]{.underline}, [rather than
**confronting** each other and trying to **change each other's
minds**]{.underline}. In this way, [neoliberal society tends to drive us
**apart**]{.underline}. By contrast, DuFord suggests that conflict
within solidarity organizations transforms the neoliberal subject into
"a member of a society." For DuFord, internal conflict takes "what was
initially an individual goal a person perhaps thought many others
shared, and transforms the goal, the person, and that person's norms."
[Through **conflict**]{.underline}, [our understandings of our own
interests and the world we want to build can **change**]{.underline}. At
the same time, [we can become **more knowledgeable**]{.underline},
**[less dominating]{.underline}**, **[less judgmental]{.underline}**,
[and **more generous** in our interpretations of one
another]{.underline}---in a word, more cooperative.

Of course, even if conflict can be productively transformative, it
doesn't always have that effect. [Many conflicts simply make people
never want to go to another meeting again]{.underline}. If
[one]{.underline} product of neoliberalism is consensus, another seems
to be a particular [style of conflict]{.underline}: one that
**[atomizes]{.underline}**, [rather than **transforms**]{.underline},
[the people involved]{.underline}.

Recognizing this fact, DuFord attempts to distinguish between
constructive and destructive conflict. Following sociologists Lewis
Coser and George Simmel, DuFord identifies the former sort of conflict
with "realistic" disagreements, in the sense that "something real is at
stake"---strategic or "substantive" issues that are "generated in the
course of advancing a constitutive aim." As an example, we might think
of ongoing conflicts within the Democratic Socialists of America over
how socialists ought to engage in electoral politics. By contrast,
"unrealistic conflict" is "generated for the purely psychological
satisfaction of a fight"---it arises from "incompatible personalities or
petty quarrels" and "lacks any true goal." This distinction may be
plausible in principle, but it is less plausible in practice. Often what
seem like strategic disagreements are also personality-based conflicts.
Moreover, the latter can be constructive if we accept that our
personalities are shaped by our experiences of the social world and
allow even these types of disputes to work on us.

[A more promising way to facilitate solidarity]{.underline} through
conflict [might be to think about]{.underline} the norms and practices
that cultivate **[endurance]{.underline}**. [No conflict is productive
without a social context that **holds people together long enough** that
they have to try to **understand** where the other person is coming
from]{.underline}. [If we don't have to stay in the room
**together**]{.underline}, [we have **no reason** to do the difficult
work of identifying the **roots** of our disagreement]{.underline},
[considering the ways our experiences]{.underline} of privilege or
oppression [might be **shaping our perspective**]{.underline}, [and
exercising our judgment to **resolve the dispute**]{.underline}. [The
crucial question]{.underline}, then, [is not how to **categorize**
conflict as good or bad]{.underline}, [but how to cultivate the capacity
to **show up** when you **don't know** what kind of a fight it's going
to be]{.underline}.

What's missing from DuFord's account, in other words, is the temporal
dimension of solidarity. DuFord sometimes seems to suggest that
stability is irrelevant to the question of how constructive conflict can
be. They write, "These \[internal\] conflicts do threaten the groups'
stability, but solidarity organizations are not intended to be permanent
political institutions." Solidarity organizations are not meant to exist
forever, and the attempt to make them do so produces perverse results.
"Solidarity Forever" is a dangerous promise and a recipe for exclusion
and domination. But solidarity [organizations]{.underline} do [have to
**endure**]{.underline} for a while. [The challenge is to figure out how
to stick together long enough for conflict to **effect change** in the
people involved]{.underline}, [and in the world]{.underline}.

In this regard, Mormons have something that [most neoliberal subjects
lack]{.underline}---**[social endurance]{.underline}**, or [the capacity
to **keep showing up** even when doing so is unpleasant]{.underline}.
Growing up Mormon in the Boston area, I learned how to show up to three
hours of meetings on Sundays, two hours of youth group on Wednesday
night, and an hour of scripture study before school every morning that I
didn't sleep through my alarm, all with people who I mostly did not
like. Over time [I came to value the **frustration** I experienced
in]{.underline} these [meetings]{.underline}. Church was the one place
in my life where I regularly interacted [with people who were **very
different** from me]{.underline}. My friends at school were all
upper-middle class, Ivy League--bound, secular, and liberal. My brothers
and sisters at church were from diverse socioeconomic backgrounds. Some
were recent immigrants. Many were ideologically right-wing. [Most
believed things that I found **deeply implausible**]{.underline}. [The
process of learning to work with these people]{.underline} to do mutual
aid and organize our congregation [forced me out of myself]{.underline}
in ways that I found difficult to leave.

It should be easier to leave a democratic solidarity organization than
it was for me to leave Mormonism. But [some degree of social endurance
is **essential** to both personal and social
transformation]{.underline}, given how difficult it is to hold subjects
of neoliberalism together for any length of time.

[To cultivate social endurance is to enter every collective in
anticipation of **annoyance** and **heartbreak**]{.underline}. [We are
going to **irritate** and **disappoint** each other]{.underline},
despite our best intentions. Given the ways our society is segregated by
race, class, and other demographic categories, [we almost certainly will
find things to **dislike** when we get together]{.underline}, even if
only [because we **don't know how to act** around each
other]{.underline}. We might even think our comrades are stupid
motherfuckers, which---let's be honest---they often are. Fortunately, as
Dean argues, one of the distinctive features of comradeship is that [we
don't have to **like** the people with whom we are in
solidarity]{.underline}. Maybe [political relationships work best when
we **don't like each other**]{.underline}, [and when we express
our]{.underline} frustrations, [disagreements]{.underline}, and anger
[with the intent of moving toward a **shared goal**]{.underline}.

### Link---Decolonial Thought---1NC/2NC

#### Decolonial epistemologies [naively]{.underline} assume that epistemologies can be decolonized without [material structures]{.underline} changing---that [obscures]{.underline} political economy and [facilitates]{.underline} capital expansion.

**Kapoor, 24**---professor of Critical Development Studies at the
Faculty of Environmental and Urban Change at York University (Ilan,
"Intersectionality, Decoloniality, Indigenous Localism: A Critique,"
Theory, Culture & Society, OnlineFirst, doi:10.1177/02632764241303689,
dml)

There is much to learn from decoloniality -- from its multi-dimensional
analysis of coloniality, to its [[critique of Western modernity]{.mark}
and its]{.underline} prescriptions for decolonizing the colonial matrix.
But Mignolo's [analysis [inclines toward **romanticizing** decolonial
spaces]{.mark}]{.underline}. He has almost nothing critical to say about
the subaltern/Global South; it is [as though they are **somehow immune**
to contradiction]{.underline}, [failure]{.underline}, [or
disagreement]{.underline} (Kapoor and Zalloua, 2022: 96--9).

[This is evident in]{.underline} Mignolo's reliance on
[**[simplified]{.mark}** and **intransigent [binaries]{.mark}** such
as]{.underline} when he pits **[Western]{.underline}** 'imperial'
[universalism against **decolonial** pluriversality]{.underline}, which
for him is invariably 'convivial, dialogical, or plurilogical . . .
decolonial and communal personalities are driven by the search for love,
conviviality, and harmony' (Mignolo, 2018: xii, xiv; see also Mignolo,
2011: 313). It is also evident in the work of the
Modernity/Coloniality/Decoloniality [research]{.underline} programme, a
collective Latin America-focused research project of
[[which]{.underline}]{.mark} both Mignolo and Arturo Escobar are part.
The project [[prioritizes]{.underline}]{.mark}
'[**[non-Eurocentric]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[forms]{.mark} of
living]{.underline}/knowledge rooted in such communities as the
Zapatistas in Mexico, Indigenous groups in Ecuador and Bolivia, and
Afro-Latinx movements in Colombia (Asher, 2013: 838; see also Escobar,
2020: 84--96, 120--35; Mignolo, 2011: 11, 213--51). Yet when Mignolo
applauds, for example, the Zapatistas for their participatory politics
and collective consensus-making, he appears to ignore that
consensus-based politics can coerce single decisions on communities,
often suppressing subaltern voices (Kapoor, 2008: 107--8, 2020: 147--69;
see Rancière, 1999). When Mignolo praises Indigenous communities, he
**[[ignores]{.underline}]{.mark}**, for instance, [the [**cooptation**
of Indigenous leaders]{.mark}]{.underline} by the Ecuadorean state, the
Morales regime's [[**backtracking** on]{.mark} commitments to
[Indigenous self-determination]{.mark} to appease capitalist
extraction]{.underline}, [**[ideological divisions]{.mark}** between and
among Indigenous groups]{.underline} across North, South, and Central
America, [[or]{.underline}]{.mark} the anti-Muslim politics of sections
of adivasi (Aboriginal) communities in India in [[**collaboration** with
right-wing]{.underline}]{.mark} Hindu
[[supremacists]{.underline}]{.mark}: 'What is **[absent]{.underline}**
in Mignolo's representations of the subaltern [are]{.underline} the
[warnings]{.underline} by the likes of Freire and Fanon [about the
possibility of internalized subaltern oppression]{.underline},
[racism]{.underline}, [and co-optation]{.underline} (which is ironic,
given that Fanon is one of Mignolo's key decolonial exemplars)' (Kapoor
and Zalloua, 2022: 96; see also Baviskar, 2007; Merino, 2016).

The extent of such an indiscriminate politics of
authenticity/romanticization is to be gleaned in Mignolo's recent
endorsement of a book from a right-wing Hindu author (Deepak, 2021) who
deploys decolonial arguments to make the case for a return to a Hindu
(Brahmanical) epistemology. After a public outcry, Mignolo eventually
withdrew his endorsement,8 but his [[willingness to **uncritically
embrace** a]{.underline}]{.mark} Hindu nationalist [[politics]{.mark}
just [because it is **disparaging of Western
epistemology**]{.mark}]{.underline} and aims at retrieving an
'Indigenous' Hindu cosmology [is revealing of the decolonial tendency to
embrace putatively]{.underline} '**[authentic]{.underline}**' [Global
South spaces even if they are **elitist** or
**oppressive**]{.underline}.

What is conspicuously missing in Mignolo's decolonial position is the
dimension of the Real, the imminent blockage that prevents any
discursive formation -- whether capitalist, post-capitalist, or
pluriversal -- from actualizing its identity. As Crenshaw does with
intersectional identity, Mignolo appears to so revel in the jouissance
of the subaltern/Global South space that he is blind to -- disavows --
social contradictions. Hence his resort to an authentic pluriversality
devoid of imperfections: a space where, as Michaelsen and Shershow
(2007) underline in their analysis of his work (p. 53), there is de
facto consensus and agreement, indeed not even the need to criticize,
debate, or deliberate. [This [appears as]{.mark} the **[textbook]{.mark}
definition of [ideology]{.mark}**]{.underline} -- [the **erasure** of
the political]{.underline}, the covering over of social antagonism
(Žižek, 1989: 25).

The accompanying proclivity is that [the production of an authentic
Global South subaltern space ends up **militating against** the putative
political subject of decoloniality]{.underline} -- [the
subaltern]{.underline}. In this regard, echoing Spivak (1988), Asher
(2013: 838--9) suggests that Mignolo and his decoloniality peers are
'turning to subaltern knowledge to fulfill \[their\] desire to create a
just world for humans and non-humans'. Decolonial jouissance of the
pluriverse induces the absence of critical self-reflexivity on the part
of the decolonial analyst, with the latter portraying himself as
transparent relay able to accurately capture the subaltern voice. The
search for a subaltern pluriverse thus becomes a self-fulfilling
prophecy: you project a pluriversal platform on the subaltern and then
rejoice in miraculously finding it! But in this mêlée, the subaltern is
not heard (which is why my above critique of Zapatista participatory
politics says less about the Zapatistas than Mignolo's construction of
them).

Mignolo's [Manichaean [argumentation]{.mark} also tends to be **broadly
dismissive** of Western thought]{.underline}, [accusing it of complicity
in the colonial matrix of power]{.underline}, [thus appealing for it to
be superseded]{.underline} (Mignolo, 2002: 80, 2010: 346). He seems [to
[**conflate** hegemonic European]{.mark}]{.underline}
([colonial]{.underline}) [[thought with European thought writ
large]{.underline}]{.mark}. In so doing, he [**[fails to
acknowledge]{.mark}** the many [European]{.mark} intellectual
**[counter-discourses]{.mark}** to colonial rationality or binary
thinking]{.underline} (anti-imperialism, non-dualism, communitarianism,
etc.) [with [which]{.mark}]{.underline}, for all intents and purposes,
[much of [decolonial thought is **allied**]{.mark}]{.underline}. He
fails as well to account for his own debt to so-called Eurocentrism,
disavowing that his intellectual background, as well as the very
language (English) and critical tools he deploys (e.g. geopolitics,
difference, epistemology, gnoseology, aesthesis), are unavoidably
Eurocentric (Kapoor and Zalloua, 2022: 99--100). Worse still, his
[policing of the boundaries of what is]{.underline}
'**[Western]{.underline}**'/'non-Western' [ends up **eliding** the
centuries-old cultural and economic exchange between Europe and its
putative others]{.underline} (Africa, the Arab world, Indigenous
America, the Indian subcontinent, etc.); [or indeed the **substantial
history** of subaltern resistance]{.underline} -- [in Europe's
colonies]{.underline}, [and in Europe itself]{.underline} -- [that is
**integral** to the emergence of such notions as]{.underline}
'equality', '**[freedom]{.underline}**', and 'social justice' (see, for
example, Anghie, 2005; Buck-Morss, 2009; Cusicanqui, 2012).

Thus, Mignolo's [decolonial disavowal]{.underline} of the
unconscious/Real [is]{.underline} not only deeply ideological for
romanticizing subaltern pluriversality, but also **[deeply
depoliticizing]{.underline}**. Rather than seeing internal (community or
social movement) differences and contradictions as weaknesses/traumas to
be suppressed or avoided in order to produce a perfect subaltern 'hero',
why not try to face them head-on to invent new and creative (although
never harmonious) subaltern political spaces? [Rather than understanding
colonial violence and dispossession as a reason to **nostalgically
manufacture**]{.underline} past or present [glory or wallow
in]{.underline} victimhood and **[resentment]{.underline}** ([against
European thinkers]{.underline}), [[why not]{.mark} struggle to [**become
proficient** with]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[and
**use**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[European discourses of emancipation and
freedom]{.mark} to fight for subaltern equality and social justice
everywhere?]{.underline} [[The Western colonial legacy is]{.mark} indeed
[imperialist]{.mark} domination and plunder]{.underline}, [[but it is
**also**]{.underline},]{.mark} as Žižek (2009) underlines, 'that of [the
**[self-critical examination]{.mark}** of the violence and exploitation
the West itself brought to the Third World]{.underline} . . . the West
supplied the very standards by which it (and its critics) measures its
own criminal past' (p. 115). [The [antagonisms of European
hegemony]{.mark}]{.underline}, in this sense, [[can be]{.mark} viewed as
**[powerful]{.mark} subaltern political
[resources]{.mark}**]{.underline}. And such is the case not only for the
subaltern from the Global South, but also for the subaltern from the
Global North: their common predicaments could be the occasion to find
cause together in shared trauma (Kapoor, 2020: xv, 66; Kapoor and
Zalloua, 2022: 20). [This is something that]{.underline} Mignolo's
[demonization of the West **precludes**]{.underline}, failing as it does
to distinguish hegemonic from subaltern (counter-) discourses, whether
in the West or otherwise, thereby further depoliticizing the subaltern.

Finally, there is the issue of [the [exclusion of the
**state**]{.mark}]{.underline} and political economy in Mignolo's gloss.
In regard to the state, Mignolo stands firmly against it, predictably
seeing it as the political expression of (Western) coloniality. For him,
pluriversal spaces would 'exist independently of the state and
corporations' by delinking 'from state forms of governance, from the
economy of accumulation' (Mignolo, 2018: xii, xiv). Thus, 'the future
would be composed of "communal nodes" around the planet cooperating
rather than competing with each other . . . \[through\] the entanglement
of several cosmologies connected today in power differential' (Mignolo,
2011: 283, 2018: x).

But Mignolo's position [[appears]{.mark} politically
**[naive]{.mark}**]{.underline}[. [Without some]{.underline}]{.mark}
[form of **[coordinating authority]{.mark}** like a state]{.underline},
[it [remains **unclear**]{.mark} [how]{.mark}]{.underline} the
[[security and well-being]{.underline}]{.mark} of 'communal nodes'
[[would be realized]{.underline}]{.mark}; [[what would ensure
**cooperation**]{.underline},]{.mark} [rather than
conflict]{.underline}, [[among and between nodes]{.underline}]{.mark},
especially given 'power differential'; [[and how]{.mark} these nodes
would be able [to **thwart** the]{.mark} continuing [incursion
of]{.mark} global [capital]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[or threats
from]{.mark} both the [**Western** and]{.mark}]{.underline}
['**[de-Westernized]{.underline}**' [powers]{.underline}]{.mark}
envisaged by Mignolo. It seems, in fact, that his [pluriversal
politics]{.underline} [**unconsciously requires** a]{.underline}
national, regional, and/or transnational [state]{.underline} to ensure
[the [coexistence of]{.mark}]{.underline} '[**[many
worlds]{.underline}**']{.mark}. He [[appears to **count** on]{.mark}
such things as [peace enforcement]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark}
[cooperation]{.underline}, [provision of [universal education and health
care]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[regulation of
multinationals]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[campaigns against]{.mark} global
[viral infections]{.mark}]{.underline}, [and so forth]{.underline}, for
his communal nodes [to **thrive**]{.underline}.

As to **[[political economy]{.underline}]{.mark}**, it [[is]{.mark} all
but **[absent]{.mark}**]{.underline} in Mignolo's political viewpoint,
given his pluriversal privileging of epistemological, at the expense of
material, decolonization. He does sketchily mention imperial resource
extraction in his [critique of coloniality]{.underline}, but says almost
nothing about the material dimensions of pluriversality. This is because
(expectedly) he [assesses Marxist political economy as]{.underline}
integral to a '**[Eurocentered]{.underline}** critique' (Mignolo, 2011:
xi, xviii), [**glossing over** the many variants of Third World
Marxisms]{.underline} ([whether Nepalese]{.underline},
[Cuban]{.underline}, [Chinese]{.underline}, [South African]{.underline},
[Peruvian]{.underline}, [Indian]{.underline}, etc., [many of which have
been actualized as political projects]{.underline} with varying degrees
of success/failure, including democratic communism in Kerala and Bengal,
for example). Mignolo (2010) looks instead to 'delinking from
capitalism' epistemologically (p. 339), [[as though decolonized
knowledge production will undo not just]{.mark} **neoliberal
[ideology]{.mark}** [but]{.mark} the **[structures]{.mark}** of global
capitalism]{.underline}. [The **[overwhelming evidence]{.mark}** to date
[points to]{.mark} just [the **opposite**]{.mark}]{.underline}: as
mentioned earlier, [the [success of]{.mark} corporate [capitalism
lies]{.mark} in part [in its **co-optation** of]{.mark} the
[epistemological]{.mark}-cultural [claims]{.mark} of social
movements]{.underline} -- [because [not only do they not pose a
**threat** to accumulation]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[but]{.mark} they [can
also be **commodified** to advance accumulation]{.mark}]{.underline}.
This is all the more the case with a decolonial politics of authenticity
which, like intersectionality, fixes and essentializes subaltern
culture/identity, thereby enabling its commoditization.

Consequently, given the disavowal of any state-like authority or
meaningful materialist analysis or strategy, Mignolo's decolonial
pluriversality yields to little more than what decolonial Indigenous
scholar Cusicanqui (2012) sees as 'multiculturalism' (p. 104).
Decoloniality may well mount a [critique of the coloniality of
power]{.underline}, but in Mignolo's hands, just as in Crenshaw's, such
critique nonetheless [ends up **facilitating**]{.underline}, **[not
challenging]{.underline}**, [global capitalism]{.underline}.
[Localism]{.underline}/particularism [**nicely suits** capitalism as
much as coloniality]{.underline}, [since it can be **readily
accommodated** if not **commodified**]{.underline}. So as with Crenshaw,
my claim is that Mignolo's disavowal of state/political economy and his
accompanying advocacy of the local amounts to an unconscious acceptance
of capitalist multiculturalism. We are thus left with another case of
'fetishistic disavowal' -- a secret love for the System in spite of an
outwardly biting critique of it. The global capitalist order sets (and
is allowed to set) the boundary for decolonial counterhegemonic
critique.

### Link---Identity---1NC/2NC

#### The 1AC explains [material]{.underline} oppression through an [identitarian]{.underline} lens, which [culturalizes]{.underline} capitalism instead of [dismantling]{.underline} it.

**Kapoor, 24**---professor of Critical Development Studies at the
Faculty of Environmental and Urban Change at York University (Ilan,
"Intersectionality, Decoloniality, Indigenous Localism: A Critique,"
Theory, Culture & Society, OnlineFirst, doi:10.1177/02632764241303689,
dml)

Indeed, identity politics is integral to capitalist liberal
multiculturalism, allowing state and market to recognize and protect
minority group rights. Yet the latter development is revealing of the
very political limitations of liberal rights discourse (Jameson, 1991;
McGowan, 2020): while certainly empowering for social groups and
[focusing public attention on]{.underline} a host of issues created,
ignored, or suppressed by liberal democracies, the problem is that
[identity]{.underline}/multicultural politics, [**far** from effecting
structural changes]{.underline}, [has been **easily
mainstreamed**]{.underline} -- [incorporated into the
System]{.underline}. The reason is that [[cultural recognition poses
**little threat** to]{.mark} [capital]{.mark}ist
multiculturalism]{.underline}: the state's granting of minority rights
or the business sector's [recognition of]{.underline} group [identities
requires **no meaningful systemic changes**]{.underline}. On the
contrary, [[the corporate]{.mark} sector]{.underline}, for example,
[[has been]{.mark} **only too [happy]{.mark}** [to capitalize
on]{.mark}]{.underline} [']{.mark}**[cultural
capitalism]{.underline}**', niche marketing products to multicultural
groups, while 'proudly' sponsoring such events as Earth Day, Women's
Marches, Black History Month, or Gay Pride. [The political
weakness]{.underline} of identity politics [lies]{.underline}, in other
words, [in its tendency to **too narrowly** focus on identity
issues]{.underline}, [at the expense of **broader** and **more
collective** material and socioeconomic rights]{.underline} (land
rights, the protection of the environmental commons, socioeconomic
equality, etc.; more on that below).

[Implied in this]{.underline} '[[**culturalization** of
politics]{.underline}']{.mark} [is the **positivization** of
identity]{.underline} -- [essentializing it]{.underline}, [fixing its
parameters]{.underline} -- [[which]{.mark} is what allows
the]{.underline} liberal [state and]{.underline} corporate [capitalism
to anesthetize it]{.underline}, [domesticate it]{.underline},
[commoditize it]{.underline}. And [such positivization]{.underline} is
reflected in intersectionality, revealing of its compliance with a
liberal multicultural framework. Accordingly, Carastathis (2008: 25--6,
2016: 125--62) [[analyzes]{.underline}]{.mark} Crenshaw's [[subjects
as]{.mark} always already]{.underline}
'[**[having]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[identities]{.underline}]{.mark},
[on the basis of which their marginalization occurs]{.underline}:
'political marginalization follows . . . the formation of the identity
of the hyper-oppressed subject, who by virtue of her identity is
politically marginalized. Intersectional subjects come, so to speak,
already formed to the space of political contestation'.3 It is not the
negative space of the intersection that is upheld, since negativity
would be difficult to fix, accommodate, or commodify, thus threatening
the mobility of global capital. [What is countenanced]{.underline} by
intersectionality instead [is the **positivization** of
identity]{.underline}, [something the System **welcomes** for its
stability]{.underline}, [recognizability]{.underline}, [and
commodifiability]{.underline}. It is because [[the]{.underline}]{.mark}
hyphenated [[subject]{.underline}]{.mark} (e.g.
female-Black-disabled-queer) [[is **identifiable**]{.underline}]{.mark},
[because multiple subject-positions can be **integrated** into the
System]{.underline} -- [indeed [can help **advance** the System since
the more subject-positions]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[the more
opportunities for **commodification** and **product
differentiation**]{.underline}]{.mark} -- that intersectionality's
critical stance ultimately conforms with, and helps reproduce,
capitalist multicultural politics.

Further evidence of [such conformism]{.underline} is that intersectional
analysis [is now **integral** to mainstream]{.underline} -- including
private sector -- equity, diversity, and inclusion
(**[EDI]{.underline}**) [strategies]{.underline} (Ahmed, 2007; Mounk,
2023), [most often aimed at enhancing organizational
performance]{.underline}. Intersectional EDI training helps employees
acknowledge their implicit biases against the diversity of identities
and human experiences so as to improve hiring practices and ensure
inclusive workplaces. [It [tends to be
**agent-focused**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [only [addressing]{.mark}
questions of [oppression]{.mark}]{.underline} (misogyny, homophobia,
racism, ableism, etc.) [[at the level of the
**individual**]{.underline}]{.mark} employee or to the extent that they
help advance organizational inclusion, effectiveness, and profit-making,
[as opposed to tackling **broader social systems** of
oppression]{.underline}. In fact, the more intersectional and
multidimensional the organization's EDI strategy, the more it can
promote its brand as equitable and diverse (even if it does not
necessarily live up to such a claim). [This is not]{.underline} to
suggest that intersectionality is [inherently corporate]{.underline},
[but]{.underline} to point out that [its tendency to culturalize and
positivize identity]{.underline}, despite -- and because -- of its
multi-dimensionality, [leaves it **too easily open** to]{.underline}
domestication, **[commodification]{.underline}**, corporate branding.

Psychoanalysis adds another significant dimension here: the reason why
intersectional identity politics appeals in today's multicultural world
is because of the subject's enjoyment (jouissance) of it -- enjoyment in
Lacanese denoting the excessive pleasure derived from something to the
point of irrationality (Lacan, 1998: 183--4).4 Accordingly, under a
multicultural regime, [the subject is **seduced** by the prospect of
social and political recognition and recovery]{.underline} (Thakur,
2020: xx--xxi; Žižek, 2006: 309). Intersectionality's [[**libidinal
reward** can be seen in]{.mark} a range of current socio-political
occurrences]{.underline}: [the]{.underline} (liberal) [benevolence
of]{.underline} granting rights and [[**rescuing** and
**rehabilitating** oppressed voices]{.underline}]{.mark}; [the
corresponding thrill of [being **recognized**]{.mark}]{.underline} by
claiming identity [or]{.underline} minority rights; the
self-righteousness of [**asserting** one's oppression]{.underline} and
'victimhood'; [[and]{.mark}/or the narcissism of
**[acknowledging]{.mark}** one's personal [guilt and
complicity]{.mark}]{.underline} in social injustices. Of particular note
is 'privilege theory' (Applebaum, 2011; Kimmel and Ferber, 2016), which,
while [bringing attention to privileged subject positions and
complicity]{.underline} in homophobia, patriarchy, or racism,
[[can]{.mark} sometimes [create]{.mark} an atmosphere of
[**surveillance** and **disciplining**]{.mark}]{.underline} ('privilege
checking') [of both self and other]{.underline}. As Zalloua (2020)
notes, [there is]{.underline} 'a kind of illicit satisfaction -- an
unconscious enjoyment -- not only in exposing the blind spots \[and\] .
. . rhetorical disciplining of others, but in your own self-discipline,
in your perceived suffering. . . . At its base level, privilege theory
provides you with [[the fantasy of]{.mark} intervention and
action]{.underline}; [it offers]{.underline} you
**[[criticism]{.underline}]{.mark}** (attention to subjective violence)
[[without **critique**]{.underline}]{.mark} (occluding objective \[i.e.
systemic\] violence)' (pp. 36, 18). [So invested can people become in
identity]{.underline} (their own and that of others) [that they [can get
**bogged down** in battles over **recognition** and
**self-policing**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The libidinal complement to intersectional identity politics thus
reinforces the culturalization of liberal democratic politics. [Real
social problems]{.underline} (discrimination, marginalization,
racialization, inequality) [are pressingly raised]{.underline}, [but
only **superficially** or **partially** acted upon]{.underline}.
[[People enjoy and publicize]{.mark} their critical
[self-awareness]{.mark}]{.underline}, [their [empathy]{.mark} with the
oppressed]{.underline}, [and their seemingly progressive]{.underline}
and humanitarian [politics]{.underline}, [but **[without]{.mark} taking
any [meaningful action]{.mark}**]{.underline} (confronting the systemic
barriers to inequalities, or demanding collective and material rights,
not simply individual civil and cultural rights). [[We feel good about
**naming**]{.mark} **our [complicity]{.mark}**]{.underline} in global
inequality, [[or]{.underline}]{.mark} we get off on [[**surveilling**
others']{.mark} lack of]{.underline} '**[political
correctness]{.underline}**', [but this only ends up [**reinforcing**
the]{.mark} particularity]{.underline},
[[fragmentation]{.underline}]{.mark}, [and depoliticization tha[t
capital]{.mark}ist liberal democracies [count on]{.mark}]{.underline}.

But intersectionality's [most significant]{.underline} blind spot
[is]{.underline} its [**disavowal** of political economy]{.underline}.
Class as socioeconomic category, in particular, is all but absent in
Crenshaw's piece, as evidenced for example by the following statement:
'theories and strategies purporting to reflect the Black community's
needs must include an analysis of sexism and patriarchy. Similarly,
feminism must include an analysis of race if it hopes to express the
aspirations of non-white women' (Crenshaw, 1989: 166). Consistent with
her identity-focused claims, in the few spots that Crenshaw broaches
class, she does so as part of a series of categories -- e.g. 'race, sex,
class, sexual preference, age, and/or physical ability' (p. 151),5
thereby taking class as an identity (i.e. the identity of 'privileged
white women' or 'working-class Black women'). But [equating class with
identity **culturalizes** the term]{.underline}, [**reducing** the
problem of social oppression]{.underline} once again [to questions of
diversity and inclusion]{.underline}. [It **flattens** socioeconomic
inequalities]{.underline}, [implying the latter are simply a problem of
difference]{.underline}, so that, for instance, by recognizing and
including working-class Black women in the social field, they will
somehow be on a level playing field with 'privileged white women'.

Marx's (1847) insistence on class as a socioeconomic category (p. 195)
is meant precisely to bring out the socioeconomic inequality on which
the capitalist system is founded, and the resulting sociopolitical
antagonism that follows (as intimated by his notion of 'class
struggle'). By [focusing on]{.underline} social diversity and identity
[**recognition** to give voice to the marginalized]{.underline},
intersectionality [ends up **smoothing over** this key
antagonism]{.underline}, thereby [**covering over**
capitalism's]{.underline} '**[dirty secret]{.underline}**'
(exploitation, domination, socio-material hierarchies). As Foley (2019)
points out, the 'fact that the black women in question \[in Crenshaw's
article\] are workers who earn at best modest wages, but make the bosses
of GM very rich, is simply taken as a given . . . \[Crenshaw's\] model
for analysis and compensation \[is\] confined to the limits of the law'
(p. 11). [[Choosing **cultural** explanations to oppression over
**material** ones]{.underline}]{.mark}, investing in liberal civil
rights discourse to address socioeconomic wrongs, [ends up
[treat]{.mark}ing the [**symptom** as]{.mark} the
**[cause]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Foley perspicaciously points out in
this regard that [[it is not]{.underline}]{.mark}
'[**[race]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[that causes
racism]{.underline},]{.mark} [[or]{.underline}
'**[gender]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[that results in
misogyny]{.underline}]{.mark} (p. 11); rather, [race and gender are
[categories of **social differentiation**]{.mark} invented]{.underline}
at the dawn of capitalism [to [justify]{.mark} the exploitation and
[domination of labour]{.mark}]{.underline} (Lugones, 2016; Quijano,
2000; Robinson, 2019). By [[**omitting** this]{.mark}
historical-structuralist dimension]{.underline}, intersectionality
[[misses the **material roots** of]{.mark} marginalization and
[oppression]{.mark}]{.underline} (Aguilar, 2015; Foley, 2019). In this
regard, what Crenshaw's crossroads metaphor ignores is that the
velocity/power of some vehicles (i.e. representing class, revealingly
absent in her example) is more impactful than others. So [without
attending to socioeconomic inequalities]{.underline}, [it is
**difficult** to see how patriarchy and racism]{.underline}
([or]{.underline} indeed [homophobia and ableism]{.underline}) [can be
**meaningfully abated**]{.underline}, as indeed they haven't despite
(and dare I say it, because of) decades of liberal rights recognition
around the world (Fraser and Honneth, 2003).6

### Link---Oppositional Scholarship---1NC/2NC

#### The 1AC's focus on oppositional scholarship enables [neoliberal capture]{.underline} of the academy.

**Okoth, 23**---member of the Salvage editorial collective (Kevin
Ochieng, "Decolonisation and the Decline of the 'Bandung Spirit'," *Red
Africa: Reclaiming Revolutionary Black Politics*, Chapter 1, pp. 16-20,
dml)

[The recent [focus on **epistemic**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [**symbolic**
or **cultural** [concerns]{.mark}]{.underline} of decolonisation can
largely be attributed to the Decolonial Studies (DS) school spearheaded
by the Argentinian linguist Walter Mignolo, which [has
[re-conceptualised decolonisation as]{.mark} a **[purely
academic]{.mark}** and **intellectual** issue]{.underline}. While the
discourse encompasses multiple strands of writing on colonialism and
epistemology, DS [scholars share a common set of theoretical
positions]{.underline}, [including]{.underline} Enrique Dussel's
anti-universalism, Mignolo's geopolitics of knowledge, Immanuel
Wallerstein's world- systems theory, and Sandra Harding's standpoint
theory, [an epistemic [approach]{.mark} which [argues that research must
**begin** with]{.mark} the perspectives of [the **most
oppressed**]{.mark} and **marginalised**]{.underline}, [since they are
socially situated in a way that gives them an advantage in gaining
certain forms of knowledge]{.underline}. Most importantly, however, DS
builds on the concept of the coloniality of power first articulated by
Aníbal Quijano to describe a 'matrix of power that produces racial and
gender hierarchies on the global and local level, functioning alongside
capital to maintain a modern regime of exploitation and domination'.17

For Quijano, coloniality is a mode of power that has its roots in two
historical processes: the codification of difference (i.e., superiority/
inferiority) in the idea of race, and the establishment of new modes of
control which made labour and resources available for capitalist
accumulation. Such formulations are, of course, nothing new. But it is
Quijano's linking of modernity and coloniality in a singular matrix that
constitutes his major contribution. What this matrix enables us to see
is that coloniality is the other -- dark -- side of modernity, the
inevitable outcome of an epistemological frame that enables the colonial
project to survive in reconstituted forms. Mignolo and other DS scholars
supposedly offer a way out of the oppressive bind of Quijano's
modernity/coloniality matrix. As Mignolo explains in On Decoloniality:

If there is no modernity without coloniality, if coloniality is
constitutive of modernity, if the '/' \[in the triad
modernity/coloniality/decoloniality\] divides and connects, then
decoloniality proposes the undoing of modernity. That is, decoloniality
implies demodernity. At the same time, modernity/coloniality engender
decoloniality. So there would be no decoloniality -- and decoloniality
would not be necessary -- if modernity/ coloniality had not created the
need to delink from the rhetoric of modernity and the logic of
coloniality.18

DS proposes new ways of 'thinking, sensing, believing, doing, and
living' that delink from the Eurocentric knowledge production of
modernity. Its aim, then, is to enable a reconstitution of knowledge
based on 'epistemic pluriversality': the coexisting epistemologies and
practices of the multiple worlds that the cultural and linguistic Other
inhabits. In doing so, they are pushing back against modernity, which
relies on a philosophical anthropology that, to borrow a phrase from
Sylvia Wynter, overrepresents European man as if it were the Human
itself, and subjugates those who have been categorised as subhuman
according to colonial logics.

[There is a **tension** between the **emancipatory aims**]{.underline}
to which DS pledges allegiance, [the **theoretical
framework**]{.underline} it proposes, [and its proponents'
**positionality** in the international]{.underline} ([and
intellectual]{.underline}) [division of labour]{.underline}. [What are
the implications for anti-imperialist struggle in the global south if
[those at the **forefront** of challenging]{.mark} the Eurocentricity of
[knowledge]{.mark} production [are based in]{.mark} the
**[resource-hoarding universities]{.mark}** of the global
north]{.underline} (and especially in the United States)[?]{.underline}
[Is there not a **[danger]{.mark}** here [of
**reproducing**]{.mark}]{.underline} precisely the kind of **[[epistemic
coloniality]{.underline}]{.mark}** from which we are trying to
delink[?]{.underline} And [what are we to make of]{.underline} DS'
[reliance on a **[deferential]{.mark}** version of standpoint
theory]{.underline}, [whose **[narrow focus]{.mark}** on]{.underline}
'[spokespeople from marginalised groups]{.underline}' [could
[**prevent** us from centring those voices]{.mark} whose exclusion DS
claims to be fighting against?]{.underline} As Olúfémí O. Táíwò
explains, 'The concept of elite capture originated in the study of
developing countries to describe the way socially advantaged people tend
to gain control over financial benefits, especially foreign aid, meant
for others'. But '[**[elite capture]{.underline}**'
[can]{.underline}]{.mark} also be used to [describe]{.underline} '[how
political projects can be **hijacked** in **principle** or in **effect**
by the well positioned and resourced]{.underline}'.19 There is, perhaps,
an analogy to be made between certain types of [scholars in the
neoliberal university]{.underline} and the neo-colonial elites; both
[**[enable]{.mark}** the **reproduction** of a system that ensures the
**[continued exploitation]{.mark}** of the very people they claim to
represent]{.underline}.

Táíwò is not [[the]{.underline}]{.mark} only one concerned with elite
capture. In 'Reflection on the Practices and Discourses of
Decolonisation', a forcefully argued critique of DS, the Bolivian
Anarcho-feminist Silvia Rivera Cusicanqui takes aim at a Latin American

[[intelligentsia]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[strikes]{.mark}
**post-modern** and]{.underline} even **[[post-colonial
poses]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and to the US academy and its followers
who built pyramidal structures of power and symbolic capital -- baseless
pyramids that vertically blind certain Latin American universities --
[[and]{.mark} form **clientelist networks** with indigenous and black
intellectuals]{.underline}.20

[These intellectuals]{.underline}, she argues, [[reproduce]{.mark}
a]{.underline} '[**[conditional inclusion]{.underline}**']{.mark} -- a
sort of 'second-class citizenship [[that moulds subaltern]{.mark}
imaginaries and [identities into]{.mark} the role of
**[ornaments]{.mark}** through which the anonymous masses play out the
theatricality of their own identity]{.underline}'. By reducing
Indigenous peoples to the marginal status of minorities, 'noble savages'
or 'guardians of nature', scholars like Mignolo and Catherine Walsh
[effectively]{.underline} exclude them from modernity and the crucial
struggles that are waged within it. Cusicanqui accuses Mignolo of
[[building]{.mark} a]{.underline} '**[small [empire within an
empire]{.mark}]{.underline}**[']{.mark} -- a formation that perpetuates
coloniality in Latin America [[by]{.underline}]{.mark}
'[**[enthroning]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[decolonisation in the
academy]{.underline}]{.mark}.21 Through [the **appropriation** and
**depoliticisation** of]{.underline} the work of [radical]{.underline}
Indigenous [scholarship]{.underline}, like that of Cusicanqui herself,
Mignolo [**marginalises** these scholars and activists from the very
debates that they themselves started and are still engaged
in]{.underline}. [This]{.underline}, in turn, [**limits** the
**imaginative power** and **emancipatory potential** of their
discourse]{.underline} on 'decolonisation'.

Decolonial Studies' concept of delinking does not correspond to the
Bandung-era demand, elaborated theoretically by Amin, that nations in
the global south should economically and politically delink from
imperialism. Rather, [delinking]{.underline} here [takes place
exclusively on an epistemic level]{.underline}. This is because, Mignolo
and his frequent collaborator Walsh argue, decoloniality delinks from
the knowledge of political theory, political economy and the
corresponding subject formation these knowledges entail.22 DS makes a
sharp distinction between colonialism as it is commonly understood and
the more abstract phenomenon of coloniality. While colonialism simply
refers to a political and economic relation in which 'the sovereignty of
a nation or a people rests on the power of another nation', coloniality
refers instead to the underlying 'patterns of power' -- in the spheres
of knowledge production or culture, for example -- that have survived
beyond the official end of colonial domination.23 For Mignolo and Walsh,
the national liberation movements of the Bandung era were engaged in
'dewesternisation'; that is, they were simply trying to fight
coloniality on its own terms (those of the nation-state), thus failing
to challenge the 'epistemic empire' that colonialism created.
Decoloniality, on the other hand, is not a state-led project, but
emerges from 'people organizing themselves in their local histories'.24
Theirs, they argue, is the only true decolonising praxis.

In her 1993 essay 'Public Enemies and Private Intellectuals', Ruth
Wilson [Gilmore takes issue with]{.underline} three approaches to
scholarship that have shaped how marginalised academics present
themselves in the neoliberal university. Gilmore groups these approaches
together under the banner of '[**[oppositional
studies]{.underline}**']{.mark}, which includes overlapping approaches
such as 'individual careerism', 'romantic particularism' and 'luxury
production'. [She defines **romantic particularism** as the tendency to
**uncritically [celebrate]{.mark}** the essential]{.underline}
'[**[authenticity]{.underline}**']{.mark} [of epistemologies and
cultures that have been suppressed]{.underline} by colonialism and
imperialism. **[Luxury production]{.underline}**, on the other hand,
[refers to the **relatively comfortable site**]{.underline} (i.e., [the
university]{.underline}) [from which these scholars produce]{.underline}
'oppositional' knowledge. For Gilmore, the final tendency, **[individual
careerism]{.underline}**, '[assigns **primary importance** to the
fact]{.underline} -- [and survival]{.underline} -- [of oppositional
studies within the intellectual and social structures of the
university]{.underline}'.25 To this end, [[scholars become **obsessed**
wit]{.mark}h the idea of [being]{.mark} the **first to coin** a
theoretical concept or [the **most knowledgeable**]{.mark} about a new
