## Elite Capture Core

### K---1NC

#### Their method falls prey to [elite capture]{.underline} by prioritizing [theoretical cohesion]{.underline} over [diverse coalitional organizing]{.underline} against imperial structures.

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, "Introduction," *Elite Capture: How the Powerful
Took Over Identity Politics (and Everything Else)*, Introduction, 14-16,
dml)

Nigeria's Special Anti-robbery Squad, US police forces, and
[many]{.underline} other [[repressive bodies use **similar**
]{.mark}**ideological [structures]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **strategies of
[violence]{.mark}** because they are similar kinds of
institutions]{.underline}, [created to achieve similar
aims]{.underline}. Most of these forces have their roots in the colonial
era of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, when national-level
institutions functioned like franchises under the global racial empire's
logo, each territorial army, colonial government, and national stock
exchange linked together in a powerful cartel. [While individual
security forces were dedicated to different national interests under the
**[global racial empire]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [the cartel as a whole
served the interests of the **same elites**]{.underline}, [making sure
wealth and advantage flowed **south to north**]{.underline}, **[Black to
white]{.underline}**. [That system [has **never been
dismantled**]{.mark}]{.underline}. So, [while]{.underline}
"**[empire]{.underline}**" [is no longer a popular term]{.underline} in
global politics, [we're **still**]{.underline} basically **[living
it]{.underline}**: [nakedly imperial structures **live on**]{.underline}
in forms like France's management of currencies of many of its former
African colonies, and seemingly neutral international corporations and
institutions bully the poorer peoples and countries of the world in
"neocolonial" fashion.10

So, despite differences in local context, when people around the world
rose up against the police terror and violence to which they have been
subjected for hundreds of years, it was immediately clear that something
global was at stake. The response from governing elites was equally
immediate: the World Bank established a "Task Force on Racism," and the
United Nations, under pressure from the entire African Union bloc of
fifty-four countries, agreed to launch a yearlong inquiry into
anti-Black racism.11 Two [[strategic trends]{.underline}]{.mark} in the
response [**quickly** [became clear]{.mark}]{.underline}: [the
[elites']{.mark} tactic of [performing **symbolic identity
politics**]{.mark} to pacify protestors **[without]{.mark} enacting
[material reforms]{.mark}**]{.underline}; [[and]{.mark} their [efforts
to **rebrand**]{.mark}]{.underline} (**[[not
replace]{.underline}]{.mark}**) [existing
[institutions]{.mark}]{.underline}, also using elements of identity
politics.

In a stunningly clear summary of the first trend, the mayor of
Washington, DC, had "Black Lives Matter" painted on streets near the
White House, atop which protestors continued to be brutalized. The
following year, the Central Intelligence Agency rolled out the second
strategy, producing a dozen "Humans of CIA" recruitment videos reaching
out to multiple identity groups, including queer and Indigenous people.
Journalist Roberto Lovato cautioned readers about the resonance of this
moment in an aptly titled article, "**[The Age of Intersectional Empire
Is Upon Us]{.underline}**": "In the vast world that lives outside of
progressive circles, there are millions of people who have emotional
reactions to Army and Marine recruitment ads featuring proud Black and
Latinx soldiers."12

Formal political task forces, encouraging murals, and inspirational
commercials are serviceable carrots. But there's also, of course, the
stick. By June 2021, twenty-five state legislatures had introduced
legislation to ban the teaching of "critical race theory," as part of a
culture war backed by think tanks such as the Heritage Foundation and
Manhattan Institute, alongside well-connected individuals such as Mark
Meadows (a former White House chief of staff in the Trump
administration).13 In the United Kingdom, the British government formed
a Commission on Race and Ethnic Disparities, which released a report
exonerating the government of the institutional racism alleged by Black
Lives Matter protes-tors.14 Where co-optation fails, regular old
repression will do.

So what, then, are we to make of identity politics? Some expressions of
identity politics are twisted to rebrand old imperial projects, while
others are actively banned by the powers that be. Is it itself an
innocuously different version of left politics, separated from more
orthodox left politics mainly by "failures of communication" as
philosopher Ashley Bohrer suggests?15 Or, more ominously, is identity
politics "an essential tool utilized by the bourgeoisie to maintain its
class domination over the working class by keeping workers divided along
racial and gender lines," as Dominic Gustavo alleges at the World
Socialist Web Site?16 Or is identity politics, as embodied in critical
race theory, a dangerous ideology and threat to the established order
that the powers that be aim to stamp out?

The Combahee River Collective (and Why Identity Politics Isn't What You
Think It Is)

The term "**[identity politics]{.underline}**" [was]{.underline} first
[popularized by]{.underline} the 1977 manifesto of [the **Combahee River
Collective**]{.underline}, an organization of queer, Black feminist
socialists, [and it was supposed to be about fostering **solidarity**
and **collaboration**]{.underline}.

American studies scholar Duchess Harris recounts the collective's origin
story as follows: in 1961, President John F. Kennedy convened a
Commission on the Status of Women. It was split into four consultative
bodies, one of which was the Consultation on Negro Women. This event
inspired sequels, and the third National Conference of Commissions on
the Status of Women birthed the meeting that founded the National
Organization for Women, which founders hoped would serve as an "NAACP
for women." However, NOW failed to live up to this promise to treat race
seriously---and Black nationalist organizations failed equally to
address gender.17 As a result, in 1973, activists formed the National
Black Feminist Organization.18

In 1974, the young activist Barbara Smith met Demita Frazier after she
began organizing an NBFO chapter in Boston. The pair agreed with many
NBFO goals but also wanted an organization that would discuss "radical
economics" more freely and that would guarantee a voice for lesbians.
And so, from a meeting of four, began the Combahee River Collective.
From 1977 to 1980, they held seven retreats with fellow activists, which
were attended by like-minded Boston veteran activists, and even the
famed writer Audre Lorde.

The experiences that united these activists---the consistent sidelining
and devaluation of their political priorities within different political
organizations---were foundational to the stance they developed, which
they christened "identity politics."

"We, as [black women]{.underline}, we actually [had a right to create
**political priorities** and **agendas** and **actions** and
**solutions**]{.underline} based in our experiences,"
**[Smith]{.underline}** later [explained]{.underline}---a political
agenda based in their full experiences and interests, rather than
positioning them as white women's tokens or as Black men's secretaries,
and one that incorporated the full complexity of their values, rather
than a degraded and misshapen caricature of them. [As]{.underline}
Princeton professor Keeanga-Yamahtta [**Taylor** puts it]{.underline},
"One could not expect Black women to be wholly active in political
movements that neither represented nor advanced their interests";
therefore, the [[identity politics]{.underline}]{.mark} they developed
[[served as]{.underline}]{.mark} "[**[entry points]{.mark}** for Black
women [to engage in politics]{.mark}]{.underline}," [[**rather**
than]{.mark} a **[whole cloth withdrawal]{.mark}** from problematic
organizations and movements]{.underline}.19

As such, [they were in favor of **diverse coalitional
organizing**]{.underline}, an approach that Smith later saw exemplified
by the Bernie Sanders presidential campaign's grassroots approach and
its focus on social issues that people of many identities face,
especially "basic needs of food, housing and healthcare."20 Beverly
Smith, another of the group's founders, recalls the immediate political
effect of the group's statement among groups in the Boston left: "\[W\]e
also drew many women of color or who were not Black to us. We had
connections with Latinas. We had connections with Asian women. . . . And
they drew us too. Because [it **wasn't**]{.underline} just like **[one
way]{.underline}**. [When we'd find out about things that were
happening]{.underline}, [we would get ourselves there as
well]{.underline}."21 The collective's principled stance on identity
politics functioned as a principle of unity, rather than division.

But, [in the decades since]{.underline} the founding of the Combahee
River Collective, [[instead of **forging alliances across
difference**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[some have chosen to **close
ranks**]{.underline}]{.mark}---especially on social media---[around
**ever-narrower** conceptions of group interests]{.underline}. Smith
says, diplomatically, that many of today's common uses of the concept
are "very different than what we intended."22 Asad Haider puts it more
starkly in his book Mistaken Identity, where he acknowledges the radical
history of the concept while nevertheless describing identity politics
as "the ideology that emerged [to **appropriate** this emancipatory
legacy in service of the advancement of]{.underline} political and
economic **[elites]{.underline}**."23 While agreeing with these points,
I also agree with political theorist Marie Moran and philosopher Linda
Martín Alcoff who have both argued effectively that ideological
explanations that tie troubling political developments to the ideas
supposedly built into identity politics tend to miss the mark: many
criticisms target ideas that aren't essential to identity-based
movements or that misconstrue their basic goals entirely.24

The idea of "**[[elite capture]{.underline}]{.mark}**" helps reconcile
these two points with each other. It is true that recent developments in
the meaning and use of identity politics have not stopped police murders
or emptied prisons. Identity politics [has]{.underline}, however,
[[equipped]{.underline}]{.mark} people, organizations, and [institutions
with [a **new vocabulary** to describe]{.mark} their [**politics** and
**aesthetic**]{.mark}]{.underline}---[[even if]{.mark} the
**[substance]{.mark}** of those political decisions [are]{.mark}
**irrelevant** or even **[counter]{.mark}** to the interests
of]{.underline} the [marginalized people]{.underline} whose identities
are being deployed. But that is a feature of how identity politics is
being used, rather than what identity politics is at its core. It is
this "elite capture"---not identity politics itself---that stands
between us and a transformative, nonsectarian, coalitional politics.

Elite Capture: The Bigger Problem

The concept of [elite capture]{.underline} originated in the study of
developing countries to describe the way socially advantaged people tend
to gain control over financial benefits, especially foreign aid, meant
for others. But the concept has also been applied more generally to
[describe how political [projects can be **hijacked** in **principle**
or in **effect**]{.mark} by the well positioned and
resourced]{.underline}. And yet, the idea also helps to explain how
[public resources such as **[knowledge]{.mark}**]{.underline},
**[[attention]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [[and **values** become
**distorted**]{.mark} and **distributed** by power
structures]{.underline}.

Elite capture accounts for many of the common objections leveled against
identity politics, including that it requires uncritical support for
political figures based on their identities without regard for their
politics and that it often reflects social preoccupations that are
"really for rich white people." One commentator, Saagar Enjeti,
criticized "the identity politics obsessed elite wing of the Democratic
party," alleging that "the people who populate our newsrooms" and
"populate the professional managerial class . . . have far too much of
an impact on our contemporary political discourse."25 Despite having
identified the problem with mainstream popular uses of identity politics
today---the outsize impact of well-positioned people on our political
discourse---Enjeti nevertheless seems to think this is a special problem
of one wing of one political party. In fact, [the underlying dynamics
are as old as politics itself and are **not confined** to a particular
politics of social identity]{.underline}.

[Elite capture is]{.underline} not a conspiracy. It's [**bigger**
than]{.underline} cynical appropriations, opportunism, or [the **moral
successes** or **failures** of any individual or group]{.underline}. [It
is]{.underline} a kind of **[system behavior]{.underline}**---a
phenomenon articulated at the population level, [an
**observable**]{.underline} (**[predictable]{.underline}**) [**pattern**
of actions involving individuals]{.underline}, [groups]{.underline},
[and subgroups]{.underline}, [each of whom may be pursuing **any number
of different goals** from their own narrow point of view]{.underline}.
[Elite capture is **not limited** to the scope of their
intentions]{.underline}. [The constant dynamic]{.underline} of
individual and group interactions [makes up a social
system]{.underline}, [and elite capture emerges out of that
dynamic]{.underline}.

#### Vote negative for [non-alignment]{.underline}. Refusing to adjudicate the 1AC is key to [conceptual decolonization]{.underline}. 

**Bright, 23**---Associate Professor, Department of Philosophy, Logic,
and Scientific Method, London School of Economics (Liam Kofi, "White
Psychodrama," The Journal of Political Philosophy, Volume 31, Issue 2,
June 2023, Pages 198-221, dml)

[The Non-Aligned person represents a kind of **ideal**]{.underline}, a
**[character type]{.underline}** to which one could be reconciled even
amid a society as so described. The basic idea is borrowed and adapted
from key ideas in Wiredu\'s philosophy of conceptual decolonization,71
so I will explain the big-picture strategy before giving more concrete
illustrations. Broadly speaking, I have fulfilled half of the task I set
for political philosophy in the introduction. I have outlined a cause
which prevents people being able to reconcile themselves to their social
order---the fact that the [ideology]{.underline} they espouse
[and]{.underline} the [material reality]{.underline} they live within
**[fundamentally come apart]{.underline}**, [the one always generating
**problems** for the other]{.underline}. And, at a high level, this
immediately suggests a path to reconciliation---make the world better
resemble the ideology, or the ideology better resemble the world. I will
presume here that in some form or another a racially egalitarian
ideology is to be maintained, and thus consider the more specific
question: [how can one **earnestly work** towards a more egalitarian
social structure]{.underline} given the situation we now find ourselves
in[?]{.underline} As I will outline in a moment, epistemic features of
the status quo make this a non-trivially difficult task to even attempt,
and so it requires certain characterological virtues that I shall use to
construct the Non-Aligned person\'s archetype.

And this is where the central idea from Wiredu comes in. The situation
of the person of colour facing the contemporary culture war, recognizing
it as doomed to incoherence, yet unsure of how to do better, shares
certain features with the situation of any formerly colonized person
trying to understand their present situation. Passing familiarity with
the history leading up to now makes it clear that [**key concepts**
through which one understands the social world]{.underline}, the
institutions charged with gathering and marshalling evidence about that
world, [and the [**bodies empowered** to **set the agenda**]{.mark} in
social discussion]{.underline}, [[have]{.underline}]{.mark} all
[[been]{.underline}]{.mark} developed by powers [indifferent or
[hostile]{.mark}]{.underline} to one\'s interests. And [yet it would
**clearly be facile** to **simply reject** whatever one inherits from
this nexus]{.underline}.72 After all, [this too **lets those same forces
set the agenda**]{.underline}. [It simply **adds a negation sign**
before stating a preferred response to someone else\'s
agenda]{.underline}. [[One must]{.underline}]{.mark} thus [develop the
skills and capacities to **[sift through the discourse space
cautiously]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[**weigh** and **evaluate**]{.mark}
what one finds]{.underline}, [[and **synthesize** what is best]{.mark}
therein so that it serves ends one sets oneself]{.underline}. [[This
is]{.underline}]{.mark} what Wiredu calls **[[conceptual
decolonization]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and [I]{.underline} shall more or
less [contract the **Non-Aligned** ideal to be an **ideal practitioner**
of conceptual decolonization within the]{.underline} present [culture
war]{.underline}, working towards the end of a genuinely racially
egalitarian socio-political order.

Let us then work through that abstract story as applied to our more
concrete case. First, why is it that the Non-Aligned person is in a
situation wherein they cannot trust their concepts, agenda-setting
agencies, or evidence-gathering institutions? This follows from the way
in which the culture war is sustained by a material inequality that no
one is seriously trying to fix. Repenters and Repressers are both
responding to discontent generated by an ideology--reality mismatch, but
neither of them wishes to either ideologically justify the material
inequality or give away their property and superior opportunities. But
as long as such material inequalities exist, they can generate stable
patterns of inegalitarian interaction in line with salient identity
categories.73 These can compound one\'s disadvantage the more
minoritized one is.74 And [even if]{.underline}, per impossible, [we
could somehow persuade all the whites to adopt the right sort of **inner
attitude**]{.underline}, [the wrong sort of **institutional structure**
can perpetuate racial inequality **regardless**]{.underline}.75

What\'s more, due attention to colonial history makes it apparent that
[regimes of stark and brutal racial [inequality can **last**]{.mark} **a
long time** [despite]{.mark} a firm majority of [the population thinking
them]{.mark} entirely [illegitimate]{.mark}]{.underline}.76
[Participants in the culture war]{.underline}, [organized]{.underline}
as it is [around **responding** to this situation]{.underline}, thus
[find themselves with **endless new fodder** with which to fuel their
battles]{.underline}. [[The ideology--reality mismatch is]{.mark}
**forever asserting** itself]{.underline}, [[**ever directing** their
intellectual]{.mark} and practical [attention]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[The]{.underline} Du Boisian social [problem can be **temporarily**
forgotten or the guilt from failing to solve it meliorated for a
**moment**]{.underline}---[but it will **never stop generating new
instances** of itself]{.underline}, [never stop reaffirming its own
basic reality]{.underline}.

Hence as long as the material inequalities exist they will keep making
racial hierarchy salient whatever the Repressers want, and keep
generating reasons for guilt whatever the Repenters want. All of [the
institutions designed [to respond to]{.mark} this culture
war]{.underline}---which is essentially all of the epistemic
institutions controlled by the white bourgeois, which is to say all of
them---[are]{.underline} thus [fundamentally addressing the **wrong
questions**]{.underline} from the point of view of the Non-Aligned
person. [They are concerned with managing the results of [a tension they
can **never resolve**]{.mark}]{.underline}, which the nature of the
Repenter and Repressor conflict will not allow them to resolve. [They
[are not arranged to **produce info**]{.mark}**rmation**]{.underline},
[[or **set an agenda**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [that will aid in resolving
material inequality]{.underline}, [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} in fact
[[will **forever be supplied** with more]{.mark} culture war
[flashpoints]{.mark} on which to **focus** and with which [to
**distract**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Since that will no doubt strike many as a bleak situation, it is worth
pausing here to address a temptation many have felt, which may indeed be
the psychological basis of many becoming PoC intelligentsia. If this is
all our society has to offer, [[why not **give in**]{.mark} to the
overwhelming temptation]{.underline} for an educated person of colour
[[and **participate**]{.mark} in the culture war]{.underline}, usually
from a perspective much more friendly to the Repenters? [Could we not
win **serious material change** by persuading them to act as our
agents?]{.underline} [And even if we cannot]{.underline}, [maybe
**pessimism** is simply the proper response to this]{.underline}; [and
[one **may as well at least get paid**]{.mark} for blunting the worst
edges of oppression]{.underline}. It is easy to see why this tempts, not
only for reasons of material interest mentioned above, but also in light
of the sort of policies and social standing which the Repenters would
seem to offer us. But [earnestly trying to **win** the culture war in
the sense of achieving **[victory]{.mark}**]{.underline} for either
Repenter or Represser [[is a **fool\'s errand**]{.underline}]{.mark};
[if those are the teams]{.underline}, [then **[the only winning move is
not to play]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

For one thing, [it is **impossible** that we shall **ever** be so
persuasive]{.underline}. [[The matters]{.mark} disputed [are
**sufficiently complex**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} the
historical narrative **sufficiently
[contradictory]{.mark}**]{.underline}[, [that there will **always**
be]{.underline}]{.mark} [the possibility of **reasonable
[disagreement]{.mark}**]{.underline}. For another, [[many]{.mark} people
[**benefit** from the status quo]{.mark}]{.underline}. First, in the
obvious sense in which both Represser and Repenter strategies do not
involve surrendering white wealth and are thus relatively advantageous
to white elites when compared to seriously redistributive policies that
might actually advance the material welfare of black people. But even
beyond that there are [many]{.underline}, including both whites77 and
the PoC intelligentsia outlined above, who [materially benefit from the
perpetuation of the culture war as it now exists]{.underline}. [They
will **not be keen** to let go of it without a fight]{.underline}. [And
in a highly interconnected and ideologically diverse
society]{.underline}, [[everyone will always be able to find
**sympathetic intellectual spokespeople** to bolster]{.mark} their faith
in [their preferred narrative]{.mark}]{.underline}. Under these
conditions, we should not expect any coherent grand narrative to achieve
consensus.78 [**[Strategic participation]{.mark}** on one side [might
**seem**]{.mark} like the **[pragmatic]{.mark} hard-nosed response** of
a realist]{.underline}, [[but it is **doomed** and]{.underline}]{.mark}
in fact simply [[a **waste of time**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Instead, [we should bear in mind that [some situations are **simply
beyond our control** and **can only do us harm by involving
ourselves**]{.mark}]{.underline}.79 [As long as the **material
circumstances** underlying the culture war remain the same]{.underline},
[neither]{.underline} the Represser nor the Repenter [archetype will
reliably act for our good]{.underline}. [That is not to counsel
**inaction**]{.underline}, [but rather to stress that our actions should
be aimed at **other ends**]{.underline}. With that, let us return and
ask ourselves: if [the Non-Aligned person]{.underline} cannot trust the
politico-epistemic institutions of the participants in the culture war,
nor reasonably hope to turn one of their sides into an engine for
change, what ought they to do instead?

Well, at a high level they [ought to act in such a way that they can
earnestly see themselves as sensibly working towards the eradication of
the material inequalities between racial groups]{.underline}. While it
is not the only way of advancing this goal, as a placeholder let us
assume the Non-Aligned person seeks to remove the ideology--reality
mismatch by securing the physical and cultural conditions necessary for
non-white people to enjoy republican freedom.80 There is good reason to
think that [this **cannot** be achieved by **mere [change in]{.mark}
people\'s [attitudes]{.mark}** [or **shifting**]{.mark} **interpersonal
etiquette [norms]{.mark}**]{.underline}.81 For republican freedom to be
attained, [**far-reaching modifications** in the basic economic
structure may]{.underline} well [be required]{.underline}.82 The
Non-Aligned character is therefore moved to seek out avenues for
effective collective action that might realize the necessary changes.
The question is then: [what sort of **attitudes** and **dispositions**
do they need]{.underline} to have to be effective at this[?]{.underline}

The psychological virtues necessary to work towards this under the
present situation are difficult to attain. As noted, [most of the venues
wherein intellectual work]{.underline} and cultural media [are produced
[are]{.mark}]{.underline} controlled by either Repenters or Repressers,
with whom Non-Aligned people are, well, not aligned. In so far as people
of colour have found a voice there, they have been [highly [vulnerable
to **elite capture**]{.mark}]{.underline}.83 Yet, [at least until
**counter-institutions** have been built which have sufficient
reach]{.underline}, [these are the spaces many of us must exist
in]{.underline}, [and persons whose work we must draw from]{.underline},
[if we are to **plan** and **coordinate** working out how to
collectively build a better world]{.underline}. What is more, all the
while those institutions will be humming to the rhythm of the perpetual
Repenter versus Represser conflict, as the world we inhabit continues
inevitably to defy their preferred ideologies. [We will]{.underline}
thus [be **bombarded** with messaging which claims that]{.underline}
their [culture war flashpoints are **supremely important** and **worthy
of our immediate attention**]{.underline}. [The Non-Aligned person must
learn to **tune out**]{.underline} the culture war noise, [while being
attentive to what is of value given their distinctive
projects]{.underline}. What sort of projects would these be, and what
sort of psychological dispositions would aid their completion?

It is unfortunately rather difficult to say what those distinctive
projects will be at this point, precisely because the present author is
one subject to all the epistemic disadvantages outlined. But we can say
a bit. [Before we engage in the project of trying to **refine** and
**disseminate** racial etiquette codes]{.underline},84 [Non-Aligned
persons will want some **evidence** that improved]{.underline} civility
[norms would **actually generate material redistribution**]{.underline}.
[Where there is some reason to think they will not]{.underline}, [we
will **simply set aside** such research projects and **reallocate our
attention elsewhere**]{.underline}. That is simply an example of a
broader point; [[an immediate project for a non-aligned intellectual
would be the **re-evaluation** of projects which are]{.mark} ostensibly
for the good of black people and presently [taking up]{.mark} much
**[time]{.mark}**]{.underline}[, **[attention]{.underline}**, [and
**resources**]{.underline}]{.mark}---such as racial etiquette
training85---and see which of these projects are premised on in fact
serving Repenter or Represser goals. [Where examples of [misallocated
resources]{.mark} are identified]{.underline}, as is the case for
etiquette training,86 [we will **[siphon off]{.mark}** resources [and
**prevent** people sympathetic to our cause]{.mark} from casting their
pearls here]{.underline}.

For a more positive example of a project we could engage in that has
both intellectual and more directly material elements, Olúfẹ́mi Táíwò has
recently advocated for a forward-looking vision of climate reparations,
concerned with what sort of world we can make together, in a fashion
that very much mirrors the concerns of the Non-Aligned person.87 What is
more, he argues that, due to many of the factors discussed in my
narrative earlier, any hope for a decent world for the vast majority of
the world\'s non-white population requires (among other things) the
provision of resources and technology to counteract the ecological and
political effects of climate change. This is a form of climate
reparations. To this we would perhaps wish to add research on ways in
which such people could become independent of the generosity and good
behaviour of the world\'s wealthy, in light of our adopted republican
ideals. But in any case, redirecting the conversation, practices, and
ultimately resources, involved in how we deal with reparations towards
specific climate ends requires powers we do not presently have, and a
mixture of skills and dispositions that are rare. Indeed, even thinking
about things this way is often very contentious.88 However, it is
exactly the sort of shift in mindset and practice we will need if we are
to carry out the Non-Aligned project of striving to actually resolve our
material inequalities.

With our examples of Non-Aligned scholar-activist projects before us, we
can think about what psychological virtues they require. And since both
require shifting focus quite dramatically within already established
areas of enquiry, we have our first characterological requirement of the
Non-Aligned person. By whatever psychological means necessary we must
stop granting the white bourgeoisie agenda-setting power over our own
concerns. That is to say, [the Non-Aligned person must cultivate
**mental self-determination** in order to ensure we are pursuing lines
of thought and action pertinent to our projects and responsive to our
concerns]{.underline}.89 For guidance in this we can look to outsider
political traditions that have also had to develop within the confines
of social forms they could not control.90 [This requires]{.underline} of
us a kind of [**self-conscious autonomy** in formulating and setting
**sub-goals** towards our ultimate end]{.underline}; [and a stance of
**reflective irony** towards what is being proffered as
important]{.underline} by institutions we cannot trust [is
essential]{.underline} to remaining Non-Aligned.

Relatedly, [[we must cultivate **dispassion** towards culture war
flashpoints]{.underline}]{.mark}.91 Repenters, Repressers, and
[[many]{.underline}]{.mark} of the PoC intelligentsia, [[will **insist**
we ought to **care deeply**]{.mark} about these issues]{.underline}.
[And [there are]{.mark} **genuinely [good arguments]{.mark}** for
affective engagement with political injustices]{.underline}.92
[[But]{.underline}]{.mark}, where our own agenda of securing republican
freedom by changes to the material base does not independently confirm
their concerns to be of interest, [these [affective]{.mark}ly charged
[flashpoints are **nothing more than a
distraction**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Yet, Non-Aligned people are
psychologically formed in the environment of our narrative. Our
ill-considered passions are hence likely to align with the spurious
concerns of the Repressers and Repenters, and this can be very hard to
break free from.93 Hopefully, [[the]{.mark} **very [act of coming to
appreciate one\'s place]{.mark}** in the narrative [will help
**denaturalize**]{.mark} its concerns [and allow one to **better
liberate oneself** from]{.mark} being swept along by [the **affective
salience regime**]{.mark} it brings with it]{.underline}.94 [[This
allows one to **focus on what matters** to one\'s own project and
**better cooperate** with others]{.mark} to those ends]{.underline}.95

Of course, [this will not mean that we are **always
unconcerned**]{.underline} with whatever Repressers or Repenters care
about---the ability of the police to engage in extra-judicial killing
certainly affects poor black people\'s republican freedom! And [even
where our indifference is maintained]{.underline}, [[this should not
be]{.mark} understood as being **[simply
unfeeling]{.mark}**]{.underline}.96 [[But we must]{.mark} none the less
strive to **[maintain our own inner seat of judgement]{.mark}** apart
from the concerns the dominant culture attempts to foist upon
us]{.underline}.97 [[With our **own assessments** given
primacy]{.underline}]{.mark}, the superior wealth and access to media of
the Repenters and Repressers will be unable to cajole or bribe us away
from our task.98 In this way, [we can carry out]{.underline} Wireduite
**[[conceptual decolonization]{.underline}]{.mark}**, having put
ourselves in a position to sift through what our epistemic environment
offers us and select only what we need.

Cultivating [this]{.underline} inner space [[is]{.underline}]{.mark}
hence [**[no]{.mark} mere personal [retreat]{.mark}** from the
world]{.underline}. [[It is **vital** to]{.underline}]{.mark} achieving
[the Non-Aligned person\'s goal]{.underline}. [By [ensuring one is able
to]{.mark} **effectively [work towards real
change]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [one may help create a]{.underline}
political [**community** and **material circumstance** where
all]{.underline}, now properly inclusive of non-white people, [may
freely exercise and live according to their own considered
judgements]{.underline}.99 This, then, is the ideal of the Non-Aligned
character archetype. Someone who can see themselves as genuinely
pursuing a reasoned approach to creating a better society, dispassionate
enough and at enough ironic distance not to get torn away from their
tasks by the raging of the Repenters and the Repressors, reconciled to a
project of genuinely resolving social incoherences rather than just
eternally responding anew to each successive dramatic demonstration of
this incoherence.

V CONCLUSION

Confusion is characteristic of a transitional society. This is a society
that has undergone sufficiently rapid changes in its mode of life and
ideological superstructure that the two have not yet had time to
properly adjust to each other.100 The case of race relationships is
especially fraught, because one effect of all these changes has been to
call the concept itself into question. Since the close of the twentieth
century, we have not even really agreed whether or not there is a
'there' there to race at all101---and this is no consequence-free
metaphysical disagreement; our technological practices embody (often
confused) notions of race.102 It is hence no surprise, and no reason for
shame, that we as a culture have had a hard time thinking through this.
The USA and the broader postcolonial world have not yet found a way of
making sense of themselves after de jure racist regimes. It is natural
enough that the narratives we have available are unsatisfying, and the
character archetypes available are not appealing roles.

I have offered as a more attractive character archetype the Non-Aligned
person. They seek to eliminate the mismatch between ideological
aspiration and material reality. This they do by rendering material
circumstances more akin to what one might expect given a racially
egalitarian ideology. That is to say, rather than solve the Du Boisian
social problem by trying to better manage its fallout, they wish to
simply eliminate the circumstances that gave rise to it. Since they have
to operate amid the present society with all its confusions and
distractions, I argued that [the Non-Aligned person needs to develop a
habit of considering issues with]{.underline} Stoical
**[dispassion]{.underline}**, [while maintaining **ironic
detachment**]{.underline} from the concerns of the other character
archetypes. In this way, [they can focus on achieving their
goals]{.underline}, [rather than be **distracted** by the pervasive and
highly affectively charged]{.underline} white psychodramas that
constitute the mainstay of Repenters and Repressers battling it out in
the [culture war]{.underline}. I hope this also illustrates how the
ideal of reconciliation can still guide political philosophers even in
non-ideal societies; we can think about what sort of person and project
would constitute a rationally satisfying mode of working towards the
better world, and work out how to create conditions that facilitate
occupying such a role.

But [whether]{.underline} my vision of [the Non-Aligned]{.underline} as
character [archetype]{.underline} with which one could be reconciled [is
appealing or not]{.underline}, [it is **imperative** that we **cease
investing our psychic energy** in the]{.underline} white bourgeoisie\'s
[culture war]{.underline}. [It will **never get better**]{.underline},
[and **only makes us worse**]{.underline}.

#### Conceptual decolonization is both [necessary]{.underline} and [sufficient]{.underline} to counter the [epistemic]{.underline} and [material violence]{.underline} of elite capture at a [global scale]{.underline}.

**Gaztambide, et al, 24**---visiting assistant professor in the
Department of Psychology at the New School for Social Research (Daniel
José, with Patience Ojionuka, Sarah Simon, Jasmine Rename, Gabriella
Diaz, and Josh Stell, "Standing Against Racial Capitalism: Reconsidering
Psychology's Role in Dismantling Systemic Racism," American
Psychologist, 2024 Volume 79, Issue 4 (May-Jun), dml)

Psychology's [[emphasis on **individual**]{.mark} change]{.underline}
and restrained policy [[results]{.mark} from]{.underline} its
[**capture** by neoliberalism]{.underline} (Adams et al., 2019). By
neoliberalism, we refer to an ideology and economic agenda organized
around market deregulation, limiting government as an expression of the
public good while preserving its functions to protect property and
capital, and an understanding of society as a collection of
entrepreneurial individuals competing for their self-interest.
[This]{.underline} neoliberal bias [frames racism]{.underline} primarily
[as a problem to be managed primarily at the **individual**
level]{.underline}---through DEI programs---while preferring policy that
does not require "too much" government intervention (Adams et al., 2019;
Malherbe et al., 2021).

As a response to the civil rights and labor gains of post-New Deal
coalitions, neoliberalism emerged over the course of the 50s, 60s, and
70s in the form of economic policies, resulting in massive inequality
along with racism as a tool to divide and conquer by the right-wing
Republican party and center-left elements of the Democratic party
(Lopez, 2019; T. Reed, 2020). Racism as politics was the handmaiden of
neoliberalism, operating in the form of "dog whistle politics,"
political messages that on the surface do not appear as racial appeals
yet trigger unconscious biases. The politics of racial fear communicates
three core messages: (a) fear lazy (and dangerous) people of color, (b)
distrust government as "coddling" the undeserving, and (c) trust the
marketplace and elites as the solution to our problems (Lopez, 2019).

[Such racial politics]{.underline} suppress support for redistributive
policies and empower right-wing politicians into office, who then enact
neoliberal policies that [**[worsen]{.mark}** both racial and economic
[inequality]{.mark}]{.underline} (Engler & Weisstanner, 2021; Macdonald,
2021). Income inequality, in particular, is associated with a litany of
adverse outcomes, including worse medical and mental health, social
mobility, access to housing, health care, and education, among many
others---[**especially** for the most marginalized]{.underline}
(McCartney et al., 2019; Sami & Jeter, 2021; Wilkinson & Pickett, 2020).
Conversely, the fruits of racial capitalism are reaped by predominantly
white male elites who invest billions in campaigns mobilizing racism to
maintain the status quo (Freeman, 2021).

[The use of racism as a **divide** and **conquer** strategy to empower
elites is **endemic** to the history of colonialism]{.underline}, [with
roots in attempts by elites to **subvert** solidarity between enslaved
African peoples and indentured white servants]{.underline} from the
Caribbean to the New England colonies (Dabiri, 2021; Zinn, 2015). The
category "white," along with its mantle of privileges,
"**[[whiteness]{.underline}]{.mark}**," [[was created]{.mark} precisely
[to promote]{.mark} cross-class [solidarity between]{.mark} **working
class** and **elite** [whites]{.mark}]{.underline} (Battalora, 2021;
Horne, 2020). [The [history]{.mark} of the]{.underline}
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates, in tandem, [[is
not]{.mark} one of [linear progress]{.mark} and enlightenment
[but]{.mark} of multiracial [coalitions and]{.mark} civil [rights being
**corroded**]{.mark}]{.underline} time and again through the use of
racial politics [[by]{.underline}]{.mark} wealthy
**[[elites]{.underline}]{.mark}** (Gosse, 2021; T. Reed, 2020).

**[Du Bois]{.underline}** (1935) [grasped the essence of racism as a
tool of **[elite power]{.mark}**]{.underline}. White workers, "while
they received a low wage, were compensated in part by a sort of public
and psychological wage" (p. 700). By opting for the psychological wages
of whiteness, white workers became complicit in "the wages of both
classes \[being\] kept low" (p. 701). Years later,
**[Fanon]{.underline}**, (1952/2008) [[underscored
the]{.underline}]{.mark} "**[[sociogenic]{.underline}]{.mark}**"
[[origins of]{.underline}]{.mark} internalized inferiority among
colonized peoples, resulting from a "double process: primarily,
economic, subsequently, the
**[[internalization]{.underline}]{.mark}**---[[or]{.underline}]{.mark},
better the **[[epidermalization]{.underline}]{.mark}** \[emphasis
added\]---[of]{.underline} this [inferiority]{.underline}" (p. 4). He
cites Jean-Paul Sartre [in]{.underline} underscoring the function of
racism, "the rich for the most part exploit \[emphasis added\] this
passion for their own uses," seducing the white working class through
the belief that they too "belong to [the **elite**]{.underline}" (cited
in p. 64). In the end, a racial and economic hierarchy is enshrined,
depending on violence unleashed against people of color to maintain the
system's stability.

[The seduction of [**racial capitalism** positions anti-Blackness as a
**reference point** for desire]{.mark}]{.underline},
[uplift]{.underline}, [and pleasure for the white working
class]{.underline} (Roediger, 2022). As Harris (1993) articulated,
[whiteness as]{.underline} "**[property]{.underline}**" [serves as a
**material** and **symbolic** consolation prize under the brutality of
the market]{.underline}. While not all white people will "win" in the
market, whiteness ensures "they will not lose ... there is a limit to
how far they can fall" (pp. 1758--1759). As described above,
[[**patriarchy** fulfills similar functions]{.underline}]{.mark}. As
economic inequality dislocates familial and communal bonds, patriarchy
transforms the malaise and insecurity this produces into calls for women
to "return to the home" and claims that "white genocide" and "feminist
attacks" are destroying the white family and traditional gender norms
(Tebaldi, 2021, p. 80). [[Moral panic]{.mark} targeting trans and gender
nonconforming people also **[reinstates]{.mark}** [traditional]{.mark}
notions of [gender and sexuality]{.mark} [while **corroding**]{.mark}
the [social safety nets]{.mark} that would **protect**]{.underline} the
**[lives]{.underline}** of trans and cis people (Faye, 2022).

Drawing on D. A. Bell's (1992) work, we might say [the use of a
racial]{.underline}, [gender--sexual **scapegoat**]{.underline} "[for
failed economic or political policies **works every time**]{.underline}"
(p. 377). [Without the]{.underline} "**[deflective power]{.underline}**"
[of such]{.underline} "**[isms]{.underline}**," D. A. Bell (1993) wrote,
"[masses]{.underline} of whites [would]{.underline} likely **[wake
up]{.underline}** to [and **revolt**]{.underline} against the severe
disadvantage they suffer in income and opportunity when compared with
those whites at the top of our socio-economic heap" (p. 571).
[While]{.underline} he argued [this cross-class allegiance ensured
that]{.underline} "[[racism is **permanent**]{.underline}]{.mark},"
[this was [**not** an **internal pathology**]{.mark} of the white psyche
[but the result of **political economic arrangements**]{.mark} whose
purpose is to **maintain elite capital**]{.underline}. He did
**[not]{.underline}** see this [as a cause for **pessimism** but
a]{.underline} "[lifetime commitment to **fight**]{.underline} against
the racism that diminishes the lives of its supporters as well as its
victims" (p. 572).

This is [a sentiment we extend to our understanding of
**cisheterosexism**]{.underline}, in that a fight against patriarchy is
a fight against [the system that diminishes the lives of cisheterosexual
people as well as the queer and gender nonconforming people victimized
by it]{.underline}. Hence, we underscore, per McGhee and King, how [we
are part of a]{.underline} "**[network of mutuality]{.underline}**"
within which [[what]{.underline}]{.mark} "[[affects one
**directly**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[affects all
**indirectly**]{.underline}]{.mark}" (see above), revealing
[an]{.underline} "[**intersectionality** of suffering]{.underline}"
where [**despite** inhabiting different positionalities]{.underline} of
power and privilege, [we are all **connected** by a system that harms us
in different and distinct yet **related** ways]{.underline} (Gaztambide,
2024).

Against Racial Capitalist Realism: Critiquing the Elite Capture of
Idealism in Psychology

Despite [[the]{.mark} antiracist]{.underline},
[anticapitalist]{.underline}, [and anticolonial]{.underline} origins of
CRT, its idealist [**[permutation]{.underline}** [has been **captured**
by elite interests in ways that **preclude**]{.underline}]{.mark}
**[transformative [change]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Curry (2017a),
following Delgado (2004), tracked how [idealism is used to
[**de-emphasize** political economy through a **narrow focus**
on]{.mark} language and individual identity]{.underline}. At its
extreme, [this **idealist turn** insists that [**psychology** and
**beliefs**]{.mark} cause societal structures to arise]{.underline}
(Curry, 2007). Others like A. L. Reed and Chowkwanyun (2012) remarked
that [when]{.underline} the term "**[structural racism]{.underline}**"
[is invoked]{.underline}, [[the]{.underline}]{.mark}
"**[[structural]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [component]{.underline} often
**[[disappears]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [[giving way to
a]{.underline}]{.mark} "**[[psychologistic]{.underline}]{.mark}**
racism/anti-racism [political **[ontology]{.mark}**]{.underline}" (p.
167). [The **reification** of idealism]{.underline}---[**separating**
race from class and psychology from
**structure**]{.underline}---[is]{.underline} itself [reflective of a
particular class politics]{.underline} (p. 167).

Research on Western democracies suggests a political realignment took
place beginning in the 1950s, in which [the **center-left** and
**right-wing**]{.underline} across Western countries [reached a
**consensus** on [neoliberalism]{.mark} even as culture and identity
became **central** to their ideological conflicts]{.underline} (Gethin
et al., 2022). [While the right-wing adopts neoliberal economics
alongside anti-LGBTQ]{.underline}, [anti-immigrant]{.underline},
[antifeminist]{.underline}, [and anti-Black rhetoric]{.underline} (Faye,
2022; Lopez, 2019), [the center-left [offers
**palliatives**]{.mark}]{.underline} to injustice [[in]{.mark} the form
of symbolic]{.underline} "**[representation]{.underline}**" and
"diversity" [while leaving underlying economic conditions
**unchanged**]{.underline} (Táíwò, 2022a; Taylor, 2016; also Turner,
2010). Put differently, "**[culture wars]{.underline}**"
[reflect]{.underline} an ideological tension among center-left and
right-wing elites, [[a]{.underline}]{.mark} "**[[never-ending
struggle]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[against]{.mark} racism as [a
**psychospiritual** force that leaves the]{.mark} political
[actors]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[policies]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and
economic structures]{.mark} that thrive from racism **virtually
[untouched]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

In the process, [neoliberal multiculturalism **co-opts** calls for
justice by **refashioning** them through the lens of a
**free-market**]{.underline}, representational, [**entrepreneurial**
view]{.underline} of equity and inclusion [whose goal is upward mobility
for some even as it **violently dispossesses** the most
vulnerable]{.underline}---the darker skin, immigrant, queer, and poor
(Melamed, 2011). [Given how neoliberal policies and economic inequality
shape meritocracy beliefs at the individual level]{.underline} (Goudarzi
et al., 2022; Mijs, 2021), [discussions of racial equity can similarly
**reproduce** a neoliberal logic in which access]{.underline} to
education, health care, and material security [is **limited** to people
of color who demonstrate]{.underline} "**[deservingness]{.underline}**,"
[while **disciplining** those who fall
short]{.underline}---[a]{.underline} "**[meritocracy 2.0]{.underline}**"
(Au, 2016). This can also influence how upwardly mobile, professional
managerial people of color frame equity and justice arguments within the
logic of neoliberalism (Gosine, 2019), such that it becomes impossible
to imagine a reality outside of capitalism as such (Fisher, 2022).

Fisher (2022) defined "capitalist realism" as the unconscious belief
that "capitalism \[is\] the only viable political and economic system
... impossible even to imagine a coherent alternative" (p. 2). If the
current system is "all there is," and our best recourse for redress must
be in primarily idealist terms (e.g., individual changes in bias,
representation for the "select few"), then, as Weis and Fine (2012)
wrote, [[this **limits** us to]{.underline}]{.mark} "[advocate merely
for **[more]{.mark} sweet**]{.underline}, [**quiet [spots]{.mark}** [of
**refuge**]{.mark} rather than for **structural change**]{.underline}"
(p. 175). Psychology's emphasis on idealism paradoxically results in
what we might call, borrowing from Fisher, a racial capitalist realism.
Conversely, Táíwò (2022a) reminds us that the **[[elite
capture]{.underline}]{.mark}** of idealist CRT is not limited to
psychology but [reflects]{.underline} "[how elites capture **all**
aspects of life]{.underline}, [including intellectual
movements]{.underline}, [under racial capitalism]{.underline}" (p. 29).
As the Jamaican British sociologist Hall (1997) intuited, [ideology
works to]{.underline} "**[freeze]{.underline}**" [meaning and language
to **constrain** our political imagination]{.underline}.

As a "New Left" scholar, Hall (1997) witnessed the capture of identity
politics in real time, rendering calls for social justice "appropriated
to a softer version of itself. It requires 'sleeping with the enemy'"
(p. 37). Hall observed this dynamic throughout politics, giving the
example of how a "public/private" transport system was developed in
Britain over a nationalized system to accommodate business interests.
American psychology is not immune to these pressures, which is why as a
discipline it can advocate for diversity and inclusion even as it has
been complicit in the immigration and prison--industrial complex (see
Klukoff et al., 2021), alongside its documented participation in torture
through the military--industrial complex (Hoffman, 2015; Orford, 2017;
Soldz et al., 2011). The APA resolution's favoring of more conservative,
private/public approaches to policy reflects these tensions.

Again, [such an approach [offers]{.mark} the possibility of upward
[mobility for **some**]{.mark} people of color]{.underline}, [queer
folk]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} women]{.underline}, [while maintaining
[**precarity** for **all**]{.mark} under racial capitalism]{.underline}
(T. Reed, 2020). [This presents a]{.underline} "**[poisoned
apple]{.underline}**" [insofar as upward mobility confers **diminishing
returns**]{.underline} to people of color and specifically Black
people's well-being, [such that]{.underline} "**[moving
up]{.underline}**" [confers **limited** protection]{.underline} against
racism (Assari et al., 2018). [We advocate **transcending** a
representational politics that [presumes a]{.mark}]{.underline}
"**[[trickle-down]{.mark} social
[justice]{.mark}]{.underline}**"---[[that elevating
**some**]{.underline}]{.mark} people of color into power or middle-class

**[decolonial]{.mark}**]{.underline} realist [[perspective is the
**antidote**]{.mark} to the racial capitalist realism that one-sided

[[**expanding** our]{.mark} political [imagination to]{.mark} ensure

The Fusion of Universal and Race-Conscious Policy to Transform Society:
Toward a Decolonial Politics in Assemblage

Our position is that [the combination of universal and
population-specific policies]{.underline}---targeting racial
discrimination and disparities---[will lead to the **most salubrious**
outcomes]{.underline} (Bakhtiari et al., 2018) [and [represent a
**challenge to elite power**]{.mark} within racial
capitalism]{.underline}. Given the intersectionality of suffering
outlined above, we offer examples of these policies to illustrate how
[universal [policies can **close**]{.mark} racial
[inequalities]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [and]{.underline}]{.mark} how
race-conscious policies [[yield benefits]{.mark} to **society [at
large]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

As discussed earlier, income inequality worsens mental health outcomes
(Dev & Kim, 2020) that are also implicated in racial disparities
(Manduca, 2018). [Redistributive [**tax** policy]{.mark}]{.underline},
for example, [not only leads to **salubrious outcomes**]{.underline}
"[for **all**]{.underline}" [but exerts a **larger** effect for Black
households]{.underline} (Hardy et al., 2022). Similarly,
[[raising]{.mark} the **minimum [wage]{.mark}** []{.mark}improves
**mental health** outcomes]{.underline} (Kuroki, 2021) [and closes
racial wealth and health gaps]{.underline} (Derenoncourt & Montialoux,
2021; Mishel & Bivens, 2021; Williams & Cooper, 2019). [Given the burden
of student loan debt on Black borrowers]{.underline}, [**universal [debt
cancelation]{.mark}** would close the racial wealth gap]{.underline},
with even \$50,000 in cancelations alleviating debt for three in four
Black households and increasing Black wealth by a third (Banerjee,
2021). [Policies that reduce economic and [racial]{.mark} inequality
**curtail** elites' economic and political power]{.underline}, [given
that such policies [are correlated with lower]{.mark} corporate
**[profits]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[higher]{.mark} involvement in
**[unions]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} higher
**wages**]{.underline} (Reich, 2021).

We want to recognize the APA resolution's mention of labor unions and
"broad parental leave and daycare program\[s\]" (APA, 2021c). We extend
this to [[consider]{.mark} the positive
effects]{.underline}---[especially for working-class families of

(Miller et al., 2020) [[alongside **unions**]{.mark} as part of [a
**robust policy**]{.mark} **[set]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Given how
prolonged contact improves intergroup relations (Meleady et al., 2021),
[**research** shows unions **increase support** for redistributive
policies and policies specifically benefitting African
Americans]{.underline} (Franko & Witko, 2023; Frymer & Grumbach, 2021).
We suggest the goal of future DEI programs should be precisely to form
and encourage labor unions as an antiracist strategy that provides a
solidarity dividend (McGhee, 2021). Put differently,
[providing]{.underline} education and [**concrete examples** of how our
suffering is different and distinct yet structurally related [would
**foment the solidarity needed**]{.mark} to address racial and economic
inequality]{.underline}. For example, by supporting universal childcare
for all workers---Black, white, and Brown (Lopez, 2019).

The implementation of universal policies must not be color-blind but
targeted to address the specific disadvantages vulnerable populations
face (Rossi & Táíwò, 2020). Relatedly, [we also advocate for **targeted
policies** such as **[reparations]{.mark}**]{.underline} for descendants
of slavery in the United States. While a full account of the
philosophical complexities of reparations is beyond our scope, we focus
on their impact on racial wealth and health gaps. [**Data-driven

[reduce the **longevity gap**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Himmelstein et al.,
2022). Reparations, then, are as much economic as it is public health

[reparations]{.underline} on African Americans, it [would also
[exert]{.mark}]{.underline} "**[[bottom-up]{.underline}]{.mark}**"
[[effects throughout society as a **whole**]{.underline}]{.mark}. For
example, recent research sheds light on how improvements in the social
conditions and well-being of African Americans will translate into
economic and public health gains for white people as well (see Bowser &
Austin, 2021).

Given the relationship between income inequality and COVID-19 infections
and mortality (Tan et al., 2021), [reparations have the potential for
**[reducing transmission risk]{.mark}** for African Americans [while
**mitigating infections**]{.mark} for the population **at
large**]{.underline} (Richardson et al., 2021). As stated above, [racial
capitalism was a **fundamental cause** of the]{.underline} COVID-19
**[pandemic]{.underline}** (Laster Pirtle, 2020). For example, rather
than advocating for closures that could have saved lives, elites pushed
for the U.S. economy to stay open as long as possible, rendering Black
and Brown workers simultaneously "essential" and expendable, producing
billions in wealth for elites who siphoned that wealth from the populace

Although we cannot cover the pragmatics of reparations here, we point to
Darity et al. (2022) and Attorney General's Office (2023) who present
actionable methodologies for what reparations might look like. We also
acknowledge there are other policies we did not explore at length, such
as the return of land to Indigenous people (see Chin, 2021). Relatedly,
we do not imply the only vehicles to challenge [[elite
power]{.underline}]{.mark} are through, say, progressive taxation, as
the construction of the "taxpayer" has complicated relations to settler
colonialism (Willmott, 2022). Although we focus on the United States, we
caution against a redistribution of resources that merely repurposes
wealth extracted through militarism and neocolonialism from the global
south (Andrews, 2021). In discussing these policies, we hope to start a
conversation that sheds light on how suffering in the United States [[is
connected to]{.underline}]{.mark} its structural instantiations and
expands to consider the implications of its foreign policy abroad,
especially for immigrants and others dislocated by
[**[war]{.underline}**, **[economic crisis]{.underline}**, [and
**ecological catastrophe**]{.underline}]{.mark} (Massey, 2020).

As Hall (1997) observed, social democratic policies like those reviewed
above are also not immune to elite capture insofar as they can be used
to redirect away from revolutionary change. Thus, [we make no claim to a
politics **untarnished** by our racial capitalist world]{.underline}.
[We are]{.underline}, as Wayne Yang wrote via his persona Lapaperson
(2017), [[an]{.underline}]{.mark}
"**[[assemblage]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[of]{.underline}]{.mark}
"**[[decolonizing dreamers]{.underline}]{.mark}** ... part of the
\[colonial\] machinery and part machine themselves" (p. 9). Yang
provocatively explored how [we [can be **impacted** by

**agency** as people who have]{.underline} "[[**reassembled** them to
**decolonizing purposes**]{.underline}]{.mark}" (p. 9). In exploring
[[this]{.mark} assemblage of policies]{.underline}, we seek to **[[shake
