(White, 2021), [**reorganizing** our **world** and **[placing a check on
elite power]{.mark}**]{.underline}, which entails [**dismantling** the
very tools the white]{.underline}, [male]{.underline}, [and wealthy have
used to **build** this system]{.underline}. In framing the intersecting
nature of "imperialist white supremacist capitalist patriarchy" (hooks,
2000, p. 46), [our policy recommendations are attempts not merely
to]{.underline} "**[repair]{.underline}**" [the world but to **imagine**
and **assemble** a new one]{.underline} (Táíwò, 2022b).

A Psychology for Liberation Without Equivocation: Toward Antiracist
Anticapitalism

In a recent contribution introducing the public psychology for
liberation training model, Neville et al. (2021) discussed the
resistance to a fully decolonial, liberationist model for training
psychologists, including disagreement "with the social justice
orientation stance to improving society and the lives of people through
an antioppression lens, including anticapitalism \[emphasis added\]" (p.
1263). We echo this concern in our field and with respect to APA's
initiatives on health equity and racial justice (APA, 2021a, 2021b,
2021c). We reiterate our appreciation of our task force colleagues,
council members, and staff who contributed to a transformative moment in
our discipline's history. But we still have far to go. [We [cannot stand
against]{.mark}]{.underline} racism as [[an
**amorphous**]{.underline}]{.mark}, **[individualized]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} "**[[psychologized]{.underline}]{.mark}**"
[[entity]{.underline}]{.mark}. [We [must combat]{.mark}]{.underline}
racism as [[a]{.mark} political]{.underline},
[[material]{.underline}]{.mark}, [and sociocultural
[force]{.mark}]{.underline} embedded within capitalism as such.

[This [requires a **paradigm shift** in]{.mark}]{.underline} how we
conceive of training within the helping professions, as well as in [our
conceptualization of **[suffering]{.mark}**]{.underline} (Gaztambide,
2024; Gaztambide et al., 2022). It also requires a shift from how we
conceive of justice as such. Neoliberal ideology tends to reify the
politics of recognition, emphasizing difference while erasing the
importance of redistribution. In retrieving the importance of a
dialectic of recognition and redistribution, we also imagine how
redistribution destabilizes difference and promotes solidarity (see
Fraser, 2020). As Fanon (1952/2008) analyzed long ago, recognition also
depends on forms of charity that reinscribe colonial structures of white
supremacy. As Dabiri (2021) wrote,

[It seems a [**huge tactical error** to frame]{.mark} antiracism as [a
**petition** to]{.mark}]{.underline} the kindness or **[[better
nature]{.underline}]{.mark}** of the "good," "selfless" individual. As
such, [allyship appeals to a desire to help a]{.underline}
"**[victim]{.underline}**," [constituting a reification of the power
imbalance]{.underline}. **[[Coalition]{.underline}]{.mark}**, on the
other hand, [[is about **mutuality**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [It reframes
the task as [identifying **common ground**]{.mark}]{.underline}---[while
[attending to]{.mark}]{.underline} the
**[[specificities]{.underline}]{.mark}** of racism---[[that **all** can
strive for and]{.mark} that **all** will [benefit]{.mark}
from]{.underline}. (p. 85)

In seeking to assemble a society that works for "the sum of us" (McGhee,
2021), [we invite our]{.underline} white and non-white
[colleagues]{.underline} to engage in this work not as allies but as
accomplices (Indigenous Action Center, 2014), willing [to [**get messy**
and **explore how much of our work has been
captured**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [and what we need to do to **free**
ourselves and our communities]{.underline}.

What we call the [[elite capture]{.underline}]{.mark} of CRT idealism
[**[distorts]{.mark}** not only the [**revolutionary insights**
of]{.mark} our [traditions and ancestors but]{.mark} also [**elides**
the knowledge of]{.mark} antiracist and Indigenous [activists on the
**ground**]{.mark} across the global north and south]{.underline}, many
of whom are resoundingly anticapitalist (see Movement for Black Lives,
2024; People's Agreement of Cochabamba, 2010). [This]{.underline}
"**[[epistemological violence]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[occurs]{.mark}
when we **erase** diverse ways of knowing and interpret phenomena in
ways that **distort** the **realities** of marginalized
communities]{.underline} when there are viable and important alternate
interpretations (Teo, 2010; cf. Escobar et al., 2020). [The]{.underline}
reification and [**elite capture** of the idealist approach enacts
precisely such an erasure in ways that **maintain** systems of
oppression]{.underline} within terms legible to neoliberal ideology.

### Link---Academy---2NC

#### Spill-out is [reverse goldilocks]{.underline}---their [mindset shift]{.underline} doesn't, but their [capture by elites]{.underline} does.

**Thomas, 25**---Professor of Sociology at the State University of New
York at Oneonta (Alexander, "Not Religion," *The Sociological
Predicament: Academia and the Contradictions of Working Class
Consciousness*, Chapter 8, pp. 178-179, dml)

[Academia is a **subsystem** within the larger social
system]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} like all complex adaptive
systems, [it is composed of **individual actors**]{.underline}---you
know, **[people]{.underline}**! Although a system-level logic of
academia is to formulate and promote ideas that promote social stability
(but not necessarily integration), [individual people have a diversity
of ideas]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} diversity **[filters out to
society at large]{.underline}**, [and]{.underline} the [**debates**
within the **academy** become part of the **wider societal
debate**]{.underline}. Not surprisingly, the [debates are
frequently]{.underline} "**[watered down]{.underline}**," and the debate
around, for example, evolution is dissimilar in the public eye from the
(largely settled) debate on campus. [These processes lend themselves to
**factionalization**]{.underline}, and at times, [the **clash** over the
nature of reality develops a logic **divorced** from the **original
debate**]{.underline}. When academics align with a faction, they show
themselves to be another political actor like any other and undeserving
of any more or less respect than other political actors. Those not in
the favored tribe will see the institution as hostile, and the goal of
positive social change is out of reach.

So why didn't the left decry Michelle Obama's callous comparison of
living in Trump's America to children visiting their possibly suicidal
divorced father? Because she's "one of us" and deserves forgiveness, but
"they" do not. One would hope that a goal of sociology would be to
illuminate the process and circumstances that lead to human suffering.
In a hierarchical society, however, suffering itself must be
hierarchical. In a society in which there is poverty, the middle class
obsession of discerning the deserving poor from those not so respectable
occurs so the "system" can continue to function without redistribution
of resources. Ultimately, the primary difference between American
political parties is how they define the deserving poor: Republicans see
them as people who lost their jobs due to "unfair" competition, whether
by government agencies or foreign companies or recent immigrants. For
Democrats, it is discrimination based on unchangeable characteristics.
Neither is an effective explanation because they are based on
individuals acting badly rather than the system as a whole maintaining
itself as a system. Social structure is not an aggregation of
individuals; it is a complex system with emergent characteristics that
has a logic of its own. Both "sides" choose to ignore the system
effects.

Red Washing

The deemphasis of sociology as a scientific endeavor and the discipline
[that]{.underline} requires (as opposed to the fantasy of machinelike
detachment) [enables a subculture that **claims** to fight the system
while]{.underline} performing the traditional sagely role of
[constructing ideologies that in actuality **support** the social
order]{.underline}. [A capitalist]{.underline} (and probably any)
[society requires a mechanism for **channeling** radical ideas into
forms **acceptable** for social order]{.underline}.11 In the
twenty-first century, [**simplified** versions of radical thought have
worked their ideas into popular discourse and become **opaque
deflections** of the original]{.underline}. This is perhaps nowhere more
obvious than in Critical Race Theory, bugaboo of the right who, for the
most part, fail to recognize their favorite targets are the versions of
CRT recuperated to be friendly to the social order.

The works so feared by the right are sacred texts aimed at deflecting
PMC attention from the knowledge of the sociological predicament. [Most
academics want to believe they are promoting **positive social change**
rather than **benefiting** from inequalities based]{.underline}, in
part, [on the **credentialism** academia requires]{.underline}. These
texts perform this function by being true but not complete, [thereby
**deflecting** attention away from information that **conflicts** with
the subcultural desire to be agents of good]{.underline}. [The texts
form the liturgical basis for **rituals** that **reaffirm** the moral
rectitude of the subculture and promote the **self-esteem** of the
individuals within]{.underline}; [the **high ritual** is the **act of
protest itself**]{.underline}. [The **elite capture** of protest is most
often evident in the selection of **reasons**]{.underline}:
[causes]{.underline} from around the planet [that **do**]{.underline},
in fact, **[deserve]{.underline}** the [**attention** but]{.underline}
also serve to [**narrow** the focus of activism **away** from matters
that **expose**]{.underline} the [class relations]{.underline} and, in
particular, the privilege of the PMC practitioners. [The system operates
by promoting works that are **superficially radical** but **narrow** the
focus of animus from the system as a **whole** to benefit of **specific
categories** of oppressed]{.underline}. The authors and readers of such
works frequently benefit from the very social arrangements about which
they write and paradoxically have an interest in maintaining them. Their
dependence upon the conditions they decry is the basis of a cognitive
dissonance that must be repressed and is the basis for the shape of the
rituals themselves.

### Link---Deference---2NC

#### The aff focuses on the elites within [\[x scholarship/x marginalized group\]]{.mark} and ignores everyone else -- that only results in them gaining more power while simultaneously placing the rest of the group in a worse position. 

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, Elite Capture: How the Powerful Took Over Identity
Politics (and Everything Else), Deference Politics, 23-24, ZMR

[A prime example of deference politics is the call to "listen to the
most affected" or "center the most marginalized,"]{.underline} now
ubiquitous in many academic and activist circles. These calls have never
sat well with me. In my experience as an academic and organizer, when
people have said they needed to "[listen to the most affected," it
wasn't usually because they intended to set up Skype calls to refugee
camps]{.underline} or to collaborate with houseless people. Acting on
this conception of "centering the most marginalized" would require a
different approach entirely, in a world where 1.6 billion people live in
inadequate housing (slum conditions) and 100 million are unhoused, a
full third of the human population does not have reliable drinking
water, and the intersections of food, energy, and water insecurity with
the climate crisis have already displaced 8.5 million people in South
Asia alone, while threatening to displace tens of millions more.17 [Such
a stance would require, at a minimum, that one leave the
room]{.underline}. Instead, "[centering the most
marginalized]{.underline}" in my experience has usually meant [handing
conversational authority and attentional goods to whoever is already in
the room and appears to fit a social category associated with some form
of oppression]{.underline}---regardless of what they have or have not
actually experienced, or what they do or do not actually know about the
matter at hand. Even in rooms where stakes have been high---where
potential researchers were discussing how to understand a social
phenomenon, where activists were deciding what to target---the [rules of
deference have often meant that the conversation stayed in the room,
while the people most affected by it stayed outside]{.underline}. This
particular politics of deference emerged out of a theoretical
orientation called standpoint epistemology, which became popular in
feminist circles in the 1970s and has continued to contribute to the
thinking of many activists and academics since.18 Standpoint
epistemology comprises three seemingly innocuous ideas: 1) knowledge is
socially situated, 2) marginalized people have some advantages in
gaining some forms of knowledge, and 3) research programs (and other
areas of human activity) ought to reflect these facts. These ideas
should go down easy. As Liam Kofi Bright argues, any serious empiricist
philosophy would entail all three of these points.19 Moreover, they are
politically important: they point to the value of lived experience and
the knowledge that comes from it. At face value, a c[ommitment to these
ideas should help us resist and contain elite capture]{.underline}. They
should provide a basis for respecting knowledge that the institutions of
the world otherwise want to discredit. But the devil is in the details.
The [common approaches to putting these abstract ideas into practice
emphasize deference to others in conversational contexts]{.underline},
in an effort to fix the distribution of attention: [they ask that we
pass the mic]{.underline}, believe marginalized people, and give
offerings. The motivation is admirable, and these actions themselves are
often good ideas, as far as they go. But [aside from involving attitudes
and interpersonal dynamics, oppression---racism, ableism, xenophobia,
patriarchy, and so forth---also have serious material
consequences]{.underline}. These structures of injustice decide who has
reliable access to basic interpersonal security, housing, health care,
water, and energy. All of these consequences of bigotry, from the
attitudinal to the material, have to be dealt with if we are to address
oppression. The [politics of deference focuses on the consequences that
are likeliest to show up in the rooms where elites do most of their
interacting: classrooms, boardrooms, political parties]{.underline}. As
a result, we seem to end up with far more, and more specific, practical
advice about how to, say, allocate tasks at a committee meeting than how
to keep people alive. [Deference as a default political orientation can
work counter to marginalized groups' interests.]{.underline} We are
surrounded by [a discourse that locates attentional injustice in the
selection of spokespeople and book lists taken to represent the
marginalized, rather than focusing on the actions of the corporations
and algorithms that much more powerfully distribute
attention]{.underline}. This discourse ultimately participates in the
weaponization of attention in the service of marginalization. It directs
what little attentional power we can control at symbolic sites of power
rather than at the root political issues that explain why everything is
so fucked up. A trip down memory lane provides a powerful example of
both the opportunities and limitations of the deferential approach. In
2007, Barack Obama was on the campaign trail for the US presidency.
Obama had just lost two of the first three primary contests to Hillary
Clinton. He gave a speech to a small crowd in Greenwood, South Carolina,
looking exhausted and disheartened. Suddenly, an attendee named Edith S.
Childs called out words of encouragement: "Fired up, ready to go!"
People around her repeated the chant, and the energy in the crowd
crescendoed. After a newly energized Barack Obama crushed Clinton in the
South Carolina primary, those five words became a slogan of the campaign
that carried the young upstart into the White House.20 Two years later,
President Barack Obama went back out on the speaking trail---this time
to Minneapolis, Minnesota, in defense of his fight to expand access to
health care. President Obama explained that he always believed that
change doesn't come from the top down; it comes from the bottom up. . .
. It "begins with you sharing your stories, fighting for something
better."21 But what does change coming from the "bottom up" mean, in
this context? The president was remarkably explicit: "\[I\]t goes to
show you how one voice can change a room. And if it changes a room, it
can change a city. And if it can change a city, it can change a state.
And if it can change a state, it can change a nation. If it change\[s\]
the nation, it can change the world." In other words, the president held
forth a model of change flowing through approved channels and
hierarchies atop which, ultimately, he stood. We [tend to be on our
guard for this kind of cynical use of "bottom up" thinking by elites
when we deal with politicians and formal, electoral
politics]{.underline}. But, as political theorist Jo Freeman has argued,
our own rooms are not free of this phenomenon.22 According to Freeman,
[any group of people]{.underline} [interacting with each other will
structure itself]{.underline} in some way or other, whether consciously
or unconsciously, leaving only the question of how that resulting
structure distributes resources, responsibilities, attention, and power.
[Elites from marginalized groups can benefit from deference in ways that
are at least compatible with social progress,]{.underline} especially if
we take the right actions afterward. But treating such elites' interests
as necessarily or even presumptively aligned with the broader group's
interests involves a political naivete we cannot afford. In this
context, [confusion about elite interests functions as a form of racial
Reaganomics]{.underline}: a strategy reliant on fantasies about the
exchange rate between the attention economy and the material economy. We
need to [fix the social structure itself---the rooms we interact in, and
the house they make up. Deference, as a strategy, bears at best a
tenuous relationship to this goal.]{.underline}

#### Deference politics only serves to forward the agenda of the elites.

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, Elite Capture: How the Powerful Took Over Identity
Politics (and Everything Else), A View from Inside the Room, 24-26, ZMR
(edited for ableist language)

Unlike structurelessness, [deference politics doesn't mask its
distributive consequences]{.underline}. Visible performance of a
deferential act of "[passing the mic" or "stepping back]{.underline}" in
order to give attention or space to another person does [tend to
redistribute short-term attention, as promised]{.underline}. But
[deference politics can still mask essential power
relation]{.underline}s, especially when we consider the performance in
the context of the people who aren't in the room at all. For instance,
[one white person giving the mic to the specific person of color in the
room can obscure both the overall power dynamics]{.underline} of the
room and the whole room's relationship to the broader category of
"people of color" that a particular comrade is taken to represent. It
would be reasonable to assume that most of those who practice standpoint
epistemology deferentially do so for the right reasons, and that they
trust the people they share the room with to help them find the proper
practical expression of their joint moral commitments. [Indeed, we don't
need to attribute bad faith to all or even most of those who practice
deferential politics to]{.underline} [explain the
phenomenon.]{.underline} Bad roommates aren\'t the problem, for the same
reason that being a good roommate isn\'t the solution: the problem is
that we are still trapped in the room. [If we want better politics, we
have to challenge how those rooms are put together]{.underline}, the
security system that controls access to them, and the rules that dictate
what happens in them. For illustration, we can return to the question of
how you came to read this book-how it is that you and I are interacting
through this text, right now. To do so, we have to consider the layers
of history, politics, and geography that made its writing possible. Many
aspects of our social system serve as filtering mechanisms, determining
which interactions happen and between whom, and thus, what social
patterns people are in a position to observe. For the majority of the
twentieth century, the US immigration quota system made legal
immigration with a path to citizenship available almost exclusively to
Europeans (earning Adolf Hitler\'s regard as the world \"leader in
developing explicitly racist policies of nationality and immigration,\"
in the words of legal scholar James Q. Whitman),24 But the 1965
Immigration and Nationality Act opened up immigration possibilities to
more people, with a preference for \"skilled labor.\" My family migrated
from Nigeria to the United States under these auspices, becoming part of
the Nigerian American community that makes up one of the country\'s most
successful immigrant populations. What no one mentions, of course, is
that the 112,000 or so Nigerian Americans with advanced degrees are
utterly ~~dwarfed~~ \[outweighed\] by the 82 million Nigerians who live
on less than a dollar a day. The selectivity of US immigration law helps
explain the rates of educational attainment in the Nigerian diasporic
community that raised me, which in turn helps explain the wealth, class
advantages, and cultural expectations that fueled my own educational
development.25 The class advantages I grew up with help explain which
rooms I was educated and socialized in during elementary and middle
school, which in turn help explain my entry into the exclusive Advanced
Placement and honors classes in high school, while others from more
disadvantaged backgrounds were routed through remedial courses. This in
turn helps explain my access to higher education, which involved
admission to schools from which others were rejected, and so on. Indeed,
the education system is a ready and uncommonly explicit example of
selection processes. T[his is the trajectory that explains why my
thoughts on elite capture were originally accepted]{.underline} and
published as an article in The Philosopher, 26 and why I have the
resources now to write a whole book that even nonphilosophers might
read. It\'s a case in point of what [sociological researchers call
\"cumulative advantage\"]{.underline} or the \"Matthew effect\": the
[people who were successful yesterday are likeliest to get today\'s
rewards]{.underline}, which makes them yet more likely to get
tomorrow\'s as well.7 With these selections in view, it is [easy to see
how this deferential form of standpoint epistemology contributes to
elite capture at scale]{.underline}. The [higher the form of education,
the narrower the social experience.]{.underline} Some s[tudents are
pipelined to PhDs, while others are pipelined to prisons-and the very
oppressive structures we aim to challenge largely explain who goes
where]{.underline}. Deferential ways of dealing with identity can easily
inherit the distortions caused by these selection processes. But it\'s
equally easy to see locally---in this room, in this social space, in
this conversation-why deference seems to make sense. It may be an
improvement on the epistemic procedure that preceded it. The [Black
person in the elite room may well be better positioned than non-Black
people in this space to think about policing and
incarceration]{.underline}. So, if we have to listen to one person,
[perhaps it\'s better that it be a Black person]{.underline}, even an
affluent and privileged Black person, [than the affluent and privileged
white person who would otherwise have dominated the
discussion.]{.underline} Put another way, deference can often seem like
the best we can do in the face of what we take to be the fixed facts
about the room and its purpose, and who\'s in it. [But these are the

w[hen the conversation is about social justice, the social mechanisms

The [deference interpretation of standpoint epistemology thus has an

[intervenes directly in morally consequential practices]{.underline} of

[since it focuses us on the interactions inside the rooms we occupy,

those who defer, the habit can supercharge moral cowardice, as the norms
of deference provide social cover for the abdication of responsibility.
It displaces onto individual heroes, a hero class, or a mythicized past
the work that is ours to do in the present. Their perspective may be
clearer on this or that specific matter, but their overall point of view
isn\'t any less particular or constrained by history than ours. [More

thoroughly fictional caricature of them. Deference to

[as identities become more and more fine grained]{.underline} and

than interdependence, [may soothe short-term psychological

the goals that motivated the project---and it entrenches a politics that

for collective liberation over mere parochial advantage.

#### The 1AC just reaffirms the status quo -- which is worse for [\[x marginalized group\]]{.mark} since voting aff will feel like it did something when it really did nothing.

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, Elite Capture: How the Powerful Took Over Identity
Politics (and Everything Else), Better Blueprints, 26, ZMR

[Deference politics is right about the what]{.underline}: it does in
fact matter that we pay attention to lived experiences, and it is
politically important that we pay attention to difference. But it is
[wrong about the how]{.underline}, because the [more we focus on
changing our norms of interactions]{.underline} to ones that locally and
cosmetically elevate the voices and perspectives in the room, the
[harder it becomes to change the world outside of the room]{.underline}.
As philosopher C. Thi Nguyen reminded us in the last chapter, the power
of the system is that of the game designer. It builds our social,
economic, cultural, and even attentional environment in ways that get us
to follow its game plan. In the speech I quoted at the beginning of this
chapter, Amílcar Cabral explains another important aspect of this
systemic control: \"\[[Imperialist domination\... for its own security,
requires cultural oppression]{.underline} and the attempt at direct or
indirect liquidation of the essential elements of the culture of the
dominated people.\" Culture, for Cabral, is our [collective ability to
design and organize our own lives]{.underline}, and be the engines of
our own history-an ability that [conflicts directly with the aims of
imperialists to be the ones doing the designing and
controlling]{.underline}. This is why, \"whatever may be the material
aspects of this domination,\" [imperialist domination can survive \"only
by the permanent, organized repression of the cultural life]{.underline}
of the people concerned. »29 This, above all, [illustrates the key
problem with deference: it focuses the very capacity]{.underline} that
we have to reconstruct the whole house to the specific rooms that have
already been built for us. It [advertises itself as deferring to
marginalized voices and perspectives, but in conceding so much creative
space to the blueprint of societ]{.underline}y, it is perhaps better
understood as deference to the built structure of society. I am arguing
here for another approach-one that concedes that we have to start with
the interactions that we have most control over, but that keeps in view
the point of changing how those interactions go: to rebuild the whole of
society, not just our interactions. [Rooting ourselves here thus gives

pursues specific goals or end results,]{.underline} [rather than aiming
to avoid \"complicity\"]{.underline} in injustices that we assume will
mostly persist anyway. I[f it\'s \"epistemology\" or knowledge practices
we\'re concerned about,]{.underline} then a [constructive politics
focuses on institutions]{.underline} and practices of [information
gathering that are strategically useful for challenging social
injustices themselves]{.underline}, not just the symptoms manifest in
the room we happen to be in today. [In general, a constructive politics
is one that engages directly]{.underline} in the task of redistributing

cashed out in symbols. [This is a demanding approach]{.underline}. It
asks that we swim upstream, that we be accountable and responsive to
people who aren\'t yet in the room, and that we build the kinds of rooms
in which we can sit together, rather than merely seek to navigate more
gracefully the rooms history has built for us. The [task of rebuilding
the world is demanding--- and it\'s constructive politics, not
deferential politics, that brings these demands together.]{.underline}

#### Turns exhaustion. Their politics pedestalizes the oppressed and causes them to bear trauma by themselves -- that causes their struggles to become performances for the benefit of elites.

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, Elite Capture: How the Powerful Took Over Identity
Politics (and Everything Else), What the Constructive Approach Asks of
Us, 35, ZMR

The [constructive approach is, however, extremely
demanding]{.underline}. It asks us to be planners and designers, to be
accountable and responsive to people who aren't yet in the room. In
addition to being architects, it asks us to become builders and
construction workers: to actually build the kinds of rooms we could sit
in together, rather than idly speculate about which rooms would be nice.
But it's [important to]{.underline} acknowledge, in closing this book,
that the constructive approach has implicit moral and emotional demands,
as well: we can neither plan nor build a better world without
collectively cultivating diverse kinds of moral and emotional
discipline. "[The deferential approach to politics is worth praising
because of its concern and attention to the importance of lived
experience]{.underline}---especially traumatic experiences. But just as
this [virtue becomes a vice when being in the room" effects are
ignored,]{.underline} this [virtue also becomes a vice when trauma's
importance]{.underline} and prevalence are framed as positive bases for
social credentials and deference behaviors, rather than primarily as
problems to deal with collectively. Here, scholarly analysis and
argument fail me. The remainder of what I have to say skews more toward
conviction than contention. But life has taught me that conviction has
much to teach, however differently posed or processed, and so I press
on. I take concerns about trauma especially seriously. I grew up in the
United States, a nation structured by settler colonialism, racial
slavery, and their aftermath, with enough collective and historical
trauma to go around. I also grew up in a Nigerian diasporic community,
populated by many who had genocide in their living memory. At the
national and community level, I have seen personality traits, quirks of
habit and action, that I've suspected were born of these grim parts of
history. Like most people, I have not been spared. I've watched and felt
myself change in reaction to fearing for my dignity or life, to crushing
pain and humiliation. I [reflect on these traumatic moments often, and
very seldom do I think, "That was educational]{.underline}." These
[experiences can be, if we are very fortunate, building
blocks]{.underline}. What comes of them depends on how the blocks are
put together. Those who study the politics of knowledge call this the
"achievement thesis." As philosopher Briana Toole clarifies, by itself,
one's social location only puts a person in a position to know;
"epistemic privilege" or advantage, on the other hand, is achieved only
through deliberate, concerted struggle from that position.8 Humiliation,
deprivation, and suffering can build---especially in the context of the
deliberate, structured effort of "consciousness raising" that Toole
specifically highlights. But these same experiences can also destroy,
and if I had to bet on which effect would win most often, it would be
the latter. Contra the old expression, pain, whether born of oppression
or not, is a poor teacher. [Suffering is partial, shortsighted, and
self-absorbed. We shouldn't have a politics that expects
different]{.underline}. Oppression is not a prep school. [Demanding as
the constructive approach may be, the deferential approach is far more
so, and in a far more unfair way]{.underline}. As philosopher Agnes
Callard rightly notes, trauma (and even the righteous, well-deserved
anger that often accompanies it) [can corrupt as readily as it can
ennoble]{.underline}.9 Perhaps more so. [When it comes down to it, the
thing I believe most deeply about deference politics is that it
asks]{.underline} something of trauma that it cannot give. It asks the
[traumatized to shoulder burdens alone that we ought to share
collectively, lifting them up onto a pedestal in order to hide below
them]{.underline}. When I think about my trauma, I don't think about
life lessons. I think about the quiet nobility of survival. The very
fact that those chapters weren't the final ones of my story is powerful
enough all on its own. It is enough to ask of those experiences that I
am still here to remember them. I also believe that deference politics
asks us to be less than we are---and not even for our own benefit. As
scholar-activist Nick Estes explains in the context of Indigenous
politics, "The [cunning of trauma politics is that it turns actual
people and struggles]{.underline}, whether racial or Indigenous
citizenship and belonging[, into matters of injury]{.underline}. It
[defines an entire people mostly on their trauma and not by their
aspirations or sheer humanity]{.underline}." [This
performance]{.underline} is not for the benefit of Indigenous people;
rather, "[it's for white audiences or institutions of
power]{.underline}."10 When I think about my trauma, I also think about
the great writer James Baldwin's realization that the things that most
tormented him "were the very things that connected me with all the
people who were alive, or who had ever been alive."11That I have
experienced my share of [traumatic experiences]{.underline}, have
survived abuse of various kinds, have faced near death from accidental
circumstance and from violence (different as the particulars of these
may be from those around me) [is not a card to play in gamified social
interaction or a weapon to wield in battles over prestig]{.underline}e.
It [is not what gives]{.underline} me [a special right to speak, to
evaluate, or to decide for a group]{.underline}. It is a concrete,
experiential manifestation of the vulnerability that connects me to most
of the people on this earth. It comes between me and other people not as
a wall, but as a bridge. Going together---[the politics of
solidarity,]{.underline} which deference provides one, flawed model of
doing---is a good start. But [on its own, it's not enough]{.underline}.
We also have to decide collectively where we're going, and then we have
to do what it takes to get there. Though we start from different levels
of privilege or advantage, this journey is not a matter of figuring out
who should bow to whom, but simply one of figuring out how best to join
forces. As Paulo Freire showed us in theory, and the African
anti-colonial and Portuguese Carnation revolutions showed us in
practice[, we will need each other to get where we're going. And getting
there, after all, is the point.]{.underline}

#### Deference epistemologies are [worse]{.underline} for community formation, [reify]{.underline} trauma, and actively [strengthen]{.underline} hegemonic structures by marginalizing the people who [were never here to defer to in the first place]{.underline}.

**Táíwò, 20**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, "Being-in-the-Room Privilege: Elite Capture and
Epistemic Deference," The Philosopher, vol. 108, no. 4, dml)

I think it's less about the core ideas and more about the prevailing
norms that convert them into practice. The call to "listen to the most
affected" or "centre the most marginalized" is ubiquitous in many
academic and activist circles. But it's never sat well with me. In my
experience, [[when people say]{.mark} they need to ["**listen to the
most affected**", it]{.mark} isn't because they intend to set up **Skype
calls to refugee camps** or to **collaborate with houseless
people**]{.underline}. Instead, [it has more [often meant]{.mark}
handing **[conversational authority]{.mark}** and **attentional goods**
to those who **most snugly fit** into the social categories associated
with these ills -- **regardless** of what they actually **do** o0r **do
not know**, or what they **have** or **have not personally
experienced**]{.underline}. In the case of my conversation with Helen,
my racial category tied me more "authentically" to an experience that
neither of us had had. She was called to defer to me by the rules of the
game as we understood it. Even where stakes are high -- where potential
researchers are discussing how to understand a social phenomenon, where
activists are deciding what to target -- these rules often prevail.

The trap wasn't that standpoint epistemology was affecting the
conversation, but how. Broadly, [the norms]{.underline} of putting
standpoint epistemology into practice [[call for]{.mark} practices of
**[deference]{.mark}**: **giving offerings**, **passing the mic**,
**believing**]{.underline}. These are good ideas in many cases, and the
norms that ask us to be ready to do them stem from admirable
motivations: a desire to increase the social power of marginalized
people identified as sources of knowledge and rightful targets of
deferential behaviour. But [deferring in this way [as a]{.mark} **rule**
or [**default political orientation**]{.mark} can actually work
**[counter]{.mark}** to [marginalized groups' interests,]{.mark}
especially [in **elite spaces**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[[Some rooms have **outsize**]{.mark} **power** and
**[influence]{.mark}**]{.underline}: the Situation Room, the newsroom,
the bargaining table, the conference room. [[Being in these rooms means
being in a position to **affect institutions**]{.mark} and **broader
social dynamics** by way of deciding what one is to say and do.
[Access]{.mark} to these rooms [is itself a]{.mark} kind of **[social
advantage]{.mark}**]{.underline}, and one often gained through some
prior social advantage. From a societal standpoint, [the "**most
affected**" by]{.underline} the [social injustices]{.underline} we
associate with politically important identities like gender, class,
race, and nationality [are **disproportionately likely** to
be]{.underline} incarcerated, underemployed, or part of the 44 percent
of the world's population without internet access -- and thus [both
**left out** of the rooms of power and **largely ignored** by the people
in the rooms of power]{.underline}. [[Individuals]{.mark} who make it
past]{.underline} the various **[social selection
pressures]{.underline}** that filter out those social identities
associated with these negative outcomes are most likely to be in the
room. That is, [they [are most likely to be in the]{.mark} [room]{.mark}
precisely [because of ways]{.mark} in which [they are]{.mark}
[**systematically different** from]{.mark} (and thus potentially
**unrepresentative** of) [the]{.mark} very [people they]{.mark} are then
**asked to [represent]{.mark}** in the room]{.underline}.

I suspected that Helen's offer was a trap. She was not the one who set
it, but it threatened to ensnare us both all the same. Broader cultural
norms -- the sort set in motion by prefacing statements with "As a Black
man..." -- cued up a set of standpoint-respecting practices that many of
us know consciously or unconsciously by rote. However, [the forms of
[deference]{.mark}]{.underline} that often follow [are ultimately
**self-undermining** and only reliably [serve "**elite
capture**]{.mark}": the control over **political agendas** and
**resources** by a group's **most advantaged** people. If we want
[to]{.mark}]{.underline} use standpoint epistemology to
[**[challenge]{.mark}** [unjust]{.mark} power [arrangements, it's **hard
to imagine** how we could do **worse**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

To say what's wrong with the popular, deferential applications of
standpoint epistemology, we need to understand what makes it popular. A
number of cynical answers present themselves: some (especially the more
socially advantaged) don't genuinely want social change -- they just
want the appearance of it. Alternatively, deference to figures from
oppressed communities is a performance that sanitizes, apologizes for,
or simply distracts from the fact that the deferrer has enough "in the
room" privilege for their "lifting up" of a perspective to be of
consequence.

I suspect there is some truth to these views, but I am unsatisfied. Many
of the people who support and enact these deferential norms are rather
like Helen: motivated by the right reasons, but trusting people they
share such rooms with to help them find the proper practical expression
of their joint moral commitments. We don't need to attribute bad faith
to all or even most of those who interpret standpoint epistemology
deferentially to explain the phenomenon, and it's not even clear it
would help. Bad "roommates" aren't the problem for the same reason that
Helen being a good roommate wasn't the solution: the problem emerges
from how the rooms themselves are constructed and managed.

To return to the initial example with Helen, the issue wasn't merely
that I hadn't grown up in the kind of low-income, redlined community she
was imagining. The epistemic situation was much worse than this. Many of
the facts about me that made my life chances different from those of the
people she was imagining were the very same facts that made me likely to
be offered things on their behalf. If I had grown up in such a
community, we probably wouldn't have been on the phone together.

Many aspects of our social system serve as filtering mechanisms,
determining which interactions happen and between whom, and thus which
social patterns people are in a position to observe. For the majority of
the 20th century, the U.S. quota system of immigration made legal
immigration with a path to citizenship almost exclusively available to
Europeans (earning Hitler's regard as the obvious "leader in developing
explicitly racist policies of nationality and immigration"). But the
1965 Immigration and Nationality Act opened up immigration
possibilities, with a preference for "skilled labour".

My parents' qualification as skilled labourers does much to explain
their entry into the country and the subsequent class advantages and
monetary resources (such as wealth) that I was born into. We are not
atypical: the Nigerian-American population is one of the country's most
successful immigrant populations (what no one mentions, of course, is
that the 112,000 or so Nigerian-Americans with advanced degrees is
utterly dwarfed by the 82 million Nigerians who live on less than a
dollar a day, or how the former fact intersects with the latter). The
selectivity of immigration law helps explain the rates of educational
attainment of the Nigerian diasporic community that raised me, which in
turn helps explain my entry into the exclusive Advanced Placement and
Honours classes in high school, which in turn helps explain my access to
higher education\...and so on, and so on.

It is easy, then, to see how [this deferential form]{.underline} of
standpoint epistemology [contributes to elite capture at **scale**. The
rooms of power and influence are at the end of causal chains that have
**selection effects**. [As you get]{.mark} higher and [higher]{.mark}
forms of [education]{.mark}, [social experiences **narrow**]{.mark} --
[some students are pipelined to **PhDs** and others to
**prisons**]{.mark}. Deferential ways of dealing with identity can
**inherit** the distortions caused by these selection
processes]{.underline}.

​[But it's equally easy to see locally]{.underline} -- in this room, in
this academic literature or field, in this conversation -- why [this
deference]{.underline} seems to make sense. It is often an improvement
on the epistemic procedure that preceded it: the person deferred to may
well be better epistemically positioned than the others in the room. It
[may well be the **best we can do** while holding **fixed** most of the
facts about the rooms themselves: what power resides in them, who is
admitted]{.underline}.

[But these are the **last** facts we should want to hold
fixed]{.underline}. Doing better than the epistemic norms we've
inherited from a history of explicit global apartheid is an awfully low
bar to set. [The facts that explain [who ends up in which room]{.mark}
[shape our world]{.mark} **much [more powerfully]{.mark}** [than]{.mark}
the squabbles for **[comparative prestige]{.mark}** between people who
have already made it into the rooms. And when the conversation is about
social justice, the mechanisms of the social system that determine who
gets into which room often just are the parts of society we aim to
address]{.underline}. For example, the fact that incarcerated people
cannot participate in academic discussions about freedom that physically
take place on campus is intimately related to the fact that they are
locked in cages.

[[Deference epistemology]{.mark} marks itself as a **solution** to an
epistemic and political problem. But not only does it [**fail** to solve
these problems,]{.mark} it **adds new ones**]{.underline}. One might
think [questions of justice ought to be primarily concerned with fixing
disparities around **health care**, **working conditions**, and **basic
material** and **interpersonal security**. Yet

**distribution of attention** and **conversational power**.
[Deference]{.mark} practices that serve **attention-focused
[campaigns]{.mark}**]{.underline} (e.g. we've read too many white men,
let's now read some people of colour) [can **fail** on their own
**highly questionable** terms: attention to **spokespeople** from
marginalized groups could]{.underline}, for example, [**[direct
attention]{.mark} [away]{.mark}** [from]{.mark} the need to change
[the]{.mark} **social [system]{.mark}** []{.mark}that marginalizes
them.]{.underline}

[Elites from marginalized groups can **benefit** from this arrangement
in ways that are **compatible** with social progress. But treating group
elites' interests as **necessarily** or even **presumptively aligned**
with full group interests involves a **political naiveté** we cannot
afford. [Such treatment of elite interests functions as a **racial

[about]{.mark} the [exchange]{.mark} rate [between]{.mark} the
[attention]{.mark} economy [and]{.mark} the [material

[[Perhaps the lucky few]{.mark} who get jobs finding the **most
culturally authentic** and **cosmetically radical description** of the

[workers who **clean up after our conferences**]{.mark}, to **slums of

**[[But probably not]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

A fuller and fairer assessment of what is going on with deference and
standpoint epistemology would go beyond technical argument, and contend
with the emotional appeals of this strategy of deference. Those in
powerful rooms may be "elites" relative to the larger group they
represent, but this guarantees nothing about how they are treated in the
rooms they are in. After all, a person privileged in an absolute sense
(a person belonging to, say, the half of the world that has secure
access to "basic needs") may nevertheless feel themselves to be
consistently on the low end of the power dynamics they actually
experience. [Deference epistemology]{.underline} responds to real,
morally weighty experiences of being put down, ignored, sidelined, or
silenced. It thus [has an important non-epistemic appeal to members
of]{.underline} stigmatized [or marginalized groups: it **intervenes
directly** in morally consequential practices of giving attention and
respect]{.underline}.

The social dynamics we experience have an outsize role in developing and
refining our political subjectivity, and our sense of ourselves. But
[this]{.underline} very [strength]{.underline} of standpoint
epistemology -- [its recognition of the importance of perspective --
becomes its **weakness** when combined with **deferential practical
norms**. Emphasis on the ways we are marginalized often matches the
world as we have experienced it. But, from a structural perspective, the
rooms we **never needed to enter** (and the explanations of why we can
**avoid** these rooms) might have **more to teach us** about the world
and our place in it. If so, [the deferential approach]{.mark} to
standpoint epistemology actually [**prevents** "**centring**]{.mark}" or
even **hearing** from [the most marginalized; it]{.mark}
[**focuses**]{.mark} us [on the]{.mark} interaction of the [rooms we
**occupy**]{.mark}, [rather than]{.mark} calling us to account for the
[interactions we **don't experience**]{.mark}]{.underline}. This fact
about who is in the room, combined with the fact that speaking for
others generates its own set of important problems (particularly when
they are not there to advocate for themselves), eliminates pressures
that might otherwise trouble the centrality of our own suffering -- and
of the suffering of the marginalized people that do happen to make it
into rooms with us.

[The dangers with this feature of deference politics are **grave**, as
are the risks for those outside of the most powerful rooms. For those
who are **deferred to**, it can supercharge **group-undermining
norms**]{.underline}. In Conflict is Not Abuse, Sarah Schulman makes a
provocative observation about the psychological effects of [both trauma
and felt superiority]{.underline}: [while these]{.underline} often [come
about for different reasons]{.underline} and have very different moral
statuses, [they result in similar behavioural patterns. Chief among
these are [**misrepresenting** the]{.mark} stakes of [conflict]{.mark}
(often by **overstating harm**) or representing others' independence as
a **hostile threat** ([such as failures to "**centre**" the **right
topics**]{.mark} or **people**). These behaviours, whatever their causal
history, [have **corrosive effects**]{.mark} on individuals who perform
them as well as the groups around them, **[especially when]{.mark} a
[community]{.mark}'s [norms magnify]{.mark}** or **multiply** [these
behaviours]{.mark} rather than **constraining** or **metabolizing**
them]{.underline}.

[For those who **defer**, the habit can supercharge **moral cowardice**.
The norms [provide]{.mark} social [cover for the **abdication of
responsibility**]{.mark}: it displaces onto **individual
heroes**]{.underline}, a hero class, or a mythicized past [the work that
is **ours** to do **now** in the present. Their perspective may be
clearer on **this** or **that specific matter**, but their **overall**
point of view isn't **any less particular** or **constrained** by
history than ours]{.underline}. More importantly, [deference places the
accountability that is **all of ours** to bear onto **select people** --
[and]{.mark}, more often than not, [a **hyper-sanitized**]{.mark} and
**thoroughly fictional [caricature]{.mark}** of them]{.underline}.

The same [tactics of deference]{.underline} that insulate us from
criticism also [**insulate** us from **connection** and
**transformation**. They **prevent** us from engaging **empathetically**
and **authentically** with the struggles of other people]{.underline} --
prerequisites of coalitional politics. [As identities become **more**
and **more fine-grained** and **disagreements sharper**, we come to
realize that "**coalitional politics**"]{.underline} (understood as
**[struggle across difference]{.underline}**) [is, simply, **politics**.
Thus, the deferential orientation, like that **fragmentation** of
political collectivity it enables, is ultimately
**anti-political**]{.underline}.

[[Deference]{.mark} rather than interdependence [may **soothe short-term
psychological wounds**]{.mark}. [But]{.mark} it does so [at a **steep
cost**]{.mark}: [it can **undermine**]{.mark} the [epistemic goals that
**motivate**]{.mark} the project, and it entrenches a politics
**unbefitting** of anyone [fighting for **freedom**]{.mark} [rather
than]{.mark} for **[privilege]{.mark}**, for **collective liberation**
rather than mere **parochial advantage**]{.underline}.

How would a constructive approach to putting standpoint epistemology
into practice differ from a deferential approach? [A constructive
approach would [focus on]{.mark} the pursuit of **[specific
goals]{.mark}** or **end results** [rather than avoiding
"**complicity**]{.mark}" in injustice [or]{.mark} adhering to **[moral
principles]{.mark}**. It would be concerned **primarily with building
institutions** and cultivating practices of **information-gathering**
rather than **helping**]{.underline}. It would focus on accountability
rather than conformity. [It would calibrate itself **directly** to the
task of **redistributing social resources** and **power** rather than to
**intermediary goals** cashed out in terms [of **pedestals** or
**symbolism**]{.mark}]{.underline}. It would focus on building and
rebuilding rooms, not regulating traffic within and between them -- it
would be a world-making project: aimed at building and rebuilding actual
structures of social connection and movement, rather than mere critique
of the ones we already have.

The water crisis in Flint, Michigan presents a clear example of both the
possibilities and limitations of refining our epistemic politics in this
way. Michigan's Department of Environmental Quality (MDEQ), a government
body tasked with the support of "healthy communities", with a team of
fifty trained scientists at its disposal, was complicit in covering up
the scale and gravity of the public health crisis from the beginning of
the crisis in 2014 until it garnered national attention in 2015.

The MDEQ, speaking from a position of epistemic and political authority,
defended the status quo in Flint. They claimed that "Flint water is safe
to drink", and were cited in Flint Mayor Dayne Walling's statement
aiming to "dispel myths and promote the truth about the Flint River"
during the April 2014 transition to the Flint River water source. That
transition was spearheaded under the tenure of the city's emergency
manager Darnell Earley (an African-American, like many of the city
residents he helped to poison). After the American Civil Liberties Union
(ACLU) circulated a leaked internal memo from the federal Environmental
Protection Agency (EPA) in July of 2014 expressing concern about lead in
Flint water, the MDEQ produced a doctored report that put the overall
measure of lead levels within federally mandated levels by mysteriously
failing to count two contaminated samples.

The reaction from residents was immediate. The month after the switch in
water source, residents reported that their tap water was discoloured
and gave off an alarming odour. They didn't need their oppression to be
"celebrated", "centred", or narrated in the newest academic parlance.
They didn't need someone to understand what it felt like to be poisoned.
What they needed was the lead out of their water. So they got to work.

The first step was to develop epistemic authority. To achieve this they
built a new room: one that put Flint residents and activists in active
collaboration with scientists who had the laboratories that could run
the relevant tests and prove the MDEQ's report to be fraudulent. Flint
residents' outcry recruited scientists to their cause and led a "citizen
science" campaign, further raising the alarm about the water quality and
distributing sample kits to neighbours to submit for testing. In this
stage, the alliance of residents and scientists won, and the poisoning
of the children of Flint emerged as a national scandal.

But this was not enough. The second step -- cleaning the water --
required more than state acknowledgement: it required apportioning
labour and resources to fix the water and address the continuing health
concerns. What Flint residents received, initially, was a mix of
platitudes and mockery from the ruling elite (some of this personally
committed by a President that shared a racial identity with many of
them). This year, however, it looks as though the tireless activism of
Flint residents and their expanding list of teammates has won additional
and more meaningful victories: the ongoing campaign is pushing the
replacements of the problematic service lines to their final stage and
is forcing the state of Michigan to agree to a settlement of \$600
million for affected families.

This outcome is in no way a wholesale victory: not only will attorney
fees cut a substantial portion of payouts, but the settlement cannot
undo the damage that was caused to the residents. A constructive
epistemology cannot guarantee full victory over an oppressive system by
itself. No epistemic orientation can by itself undo the various power
asymmetries between the people and the imperial state system. But it can
help make the game a little more competitive -- and deference
epistemology isn't even playing.

[The **biggest threats** to social justice attention and informational
economies are **not the absence of yet more jargon** to describe, ever
more precisely or incisively, the]{.underline} epistemic, attentional,
or interpersonal [**afflictions** of the disempowered. The biggest
threats are the **erosion** of the **practical** and **material bases**
for popular power]{.underline} over knowledge production and
distribution, particularly that [which could **aid effective political
action** and **constrain** or **eliminate predation** by elites. The
**capture** and **corruption** of these bases by well-positioned
elites]{.underline}, especially tech corporations, [goes on **unabated**
and largely **unchallenged**]{.underline}, including: the corporate
monopolization of local news, the ongoing destruction and looting of the
journalistic profession, the interference of corporations and
governments in key democratic processes, and the domination of elite
interests in the production of knowledge by research universities and
the circulation of the output of these distorted processes by
established media organizations.

Confronting these threats requires leaving some rooms -- and building
new ones.

[[The constructive approach]{.underline}]{.mark} to standpoint
epistemology is demanding. It [[asks]{.mark} that [we]{.mark} **swim
upstream**: to [be]{.mark} **[accountable]{.mark}** and **responsive**
[to people who **aren't yet in the room**]{.mark}, to build the kinds of
rooms we could **sit in together**]{.underline}, rather than merely
judiciously navigating the rooms history has built for us. But this
weighty demand is par for the course when it comes to the politics of
knowledge: the American philosopher Sandra Harding famously pointed out
that standpoint epistemology, properly understood, demands more rigour
from science and knowledge production processes generally, not less.

But one important topic stands unaddressed. [[The deferential
approach]{.underline}]{.mark} to standpoint epistemology [[often comes
packaged]{.mark} [with]{.mark} concern and attention to the importance
of **lived experience**]{.underline}. Among these, [**[traumatic
experiences]{.mark}** are especially foregrounded]{.underline}.

At this juncture, scholarly analysis and argument fail me. The remainder
of what I have to say skews more towards conviction than contention. But
the life of books has taught me that conviction has just as much to
teach, however differently posed or processed, and so I press on.

[[I take]{.mark} concerns about trauma **especially
[seriously]{.mark}**. I grew up in the United States, a nation
structured by [**settler colonialism**, **racial slavery**, and their
aftermath]{.mark}]{.underline}, with enough collective and historical
trauma to go round. [I also grew up in a Nigerian diasporic community,
populated by many who had **genocide in living memory**]{.underline}. At
the national and community level, I have seen a lot of traits of norms,
personality, quirks of habit and action that I've suspected were
downstream of these facts. At the level of individual experience, [I've
watched and felt myself change in reaction to fearing for my dignity or
life, to crushing pain and humiliation. [I reflect on]{.mark} these
[traumatic]{.mark} [moments]{.mark} **often**, [and **very seldom
think**: "**That was educational**]{.mark}".]{.underline}

These experiences can be, if we are very fortunate, building blocks.
What comes of them depends on how the blocks are put together: what
standpoint epistemologists call the "achievement thesis". Briana Toole
clarifies that, by itself, one's social location only puts a person in a
position to know. "Epistemic privilege" or advantage is achieved only
through deliberate, concerted struggle from that position.

I concede outright that this is certainly one possible result of the
experience of oppression: have no doubt that humiliation, deprivation,
and suffering can build (especially in the context of the deliberate,
structured effort of "consciousness raising", as Toole specifically
highlights). But these same experiences can also destroy, and if I had
to bet on which effect would win most often, it would be the latter. As
Agnes Callard rightly notes, trauma (and even the righteous,
well-deserved anger that often accompanies it) can corrupt as readily as
it can ennoble. Perhaps more so.

Contra the old expression, pain -- whether borne of oppression or not --
is a poor teacher. Suffering is partial, short-sighted, and
self-absorbed. We shouldn't have a politics that expects different:
oppression is not a prep school.

When it comes down to it, the thing I believe most deeply about
[[deference epistemology]{.underline}]{.mark} is that it [asks something
of trauma that it **cannot give**]{.underline}. Demanding as the
constructive approach may be, [the deferential approach]{.underline} is
far more demanding and in a far more unfair way: it [asks the
traumatized to shoulder burdens **alone** that we ought to share
**collectively**]{.underline}. When I think about my trauma, I don't
think about grand lessons. I think about the quiet nobility of survival.
The very fact that those chapters weren't the final ones of my story is
powerful enough writing all on its own. It is enough to ask of those
experiences that I am still here to remember them.

[Deference epistemology asks us to be **less** than we are -- and not
even for our **own** benefit]{.underline}. As Nick Estes explains in the
context of Indigenous politics: "[The **cunning** of trauma politics is
that it turns **actual people** and **struggles**]{.underline}, whether
racial or Indigenous citizenship and belonging, [into matters of
**injury**. It [**defines an entire people**]{.mark} mostly [on their
**trauma**]{.mark} and not by their **aspirations** or **sheer
humanity**]{.underline}". [This performance is]{.underline} not for the
benefit of Indigenous people, but "[[for **white audiences**]{.mark} or
**institutions of power**]{.underline}".

I also think about James Baldwin's realization that the things that
tormented him the most were "the very things that connected me with all
the people who were alive, who had ever been alive". [That I have
**survived abuse** of various kinds, have faced **near-death** from both
accidental circumstance and violence]{.underline} (different as the
particulars of these may be from those around me) [is **[not a card to
play]{.mark} in gamified social interaction** or a **weapon to wield in
[battles over prestige]{.mark}**. It is **not** what gives me a
**special right to speak**, to **evaluate**, or to **decide for a
group**. It is a concrete, experiential manifestation of the
**vulnerability** that connects me to most of the people on this Earth.
It comes between me and other people not as a **wall**, but as a
**bridge**]{.underline}.

### Link---Fiat---2NC

#### Fiat and adherence to the resolution is key to actualizing [\[their method\]]{.mark} -- anything else results in the same structures they critique. 

**Táíwò, 22**---assistant professor of philosophy at Georgetown
University (Olúfémi, Elite Capture: How the Powerful Took Over Identity

We are [seeing the corporate monopolization]{.underline} of local news
and social media, the ongoing destruction and looting [of the
journalistic profession]{.underline}, and the [domination of elite

universities and think tanks. But, as the long history of muckraking,
abolitionist newspapers, consciousness raising, and political education

[building strong networks for movement journalism,]{.underline}

think more comprehensively. In a speech describing social movements like
the anti-imperialist struggles he was then fighting against the

necessarily an act of culture."70 By "culture," Cabral did not mean that
a carefully curated list of customary greetings, traditional foods, and
styles of dress were themselves going to bring down the military forces
of a fascist empire. Culture is "the vigorous manifestation on the
ideological or idealist plane" of people and "a product of their
history," Cabral observed. But he also insisted that culture is not just
an idle ideological force or set of fashions and preferences that
results from past and present trends; it is also "a determinant of
history, by the positive or negative influence which it exerts on the
evolution of relationships between man and his environment." This is why
he claimed that imperialist domination "can be maintained only by the
permanent, organized repression of the cultural life of the people

are currently allowed to, cunning [imperialists understand that the

undertaking the struggle."71 We should [put our cultural norms to the

"The important thing is to [proceed to critical analysis]{.underline} of
African cultures in relation to the liberation movement and [to the
exigencies of progress.]{.underline}" That is, [we should evaluate our
culture instrumentally, by how well it helps us build what we are trying
to build.]{.underline}72 A [constructive political culture would focus
on outcome]{.underline} over process---the [pursuit of specific goals or
end results rather than]{.underline} avoiding [complicity in injustice
or promoting purely moral or aesthetic principles]{.underline}. When it

primarily [with building institutions]{.underline} and campaign-relevant

specific groups of people]{.underline} or spokespeople who stand in for

rebuilding actual structures of social connection and movement, not mere

[when they are planetary in scope, make it possible to totally revamp

### AT: Accountability

**[Anti-Leadership Attitudes]{.underline}**

Definition

[Hold]{.underline}ing [[skepticism of leadership as a
**rule**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Questioning the authority, legitimacy,
and competence of those with positional, perceived, or other forms of
power. Therefore, [all decisions made by leadership are subject to
**broad-based skepticism** and **mistrust**]{.underline}. Valuing
expertise and experience is challenged as potentially elitist.
Professionalization is cited as a problematic aspect of how leadership
and power are meted out. Anti-intellectualism is promoted as an
egalitarian appreciation of more informal forms of skill or knowledge.
This is not to be confused with a healthy skepticism of authority and
leadership, including within movement spaces.

Fallacies

To be clear, [abuse of authority]{.underline}, [corruption]{.underline},
[and the arbitrary concentration of power are real problems]{.underline}
that have gone unexamined in years past. We should credit those
people---many of them newer and younger to the work---who are shining a
light on systemic issues and the deep harm they cause to individuals and
the power we seek. However, [a reflexively anti-leadership orientation
[is an **overcorrection** that doesn't make room for]{.mark} the
existence of **[principled]{.mark}**]{.underline}[,
**[responsive]{.underline}**, **[accountable]{.underline}**]{.mark},
[and **democratic** [leadership]{.mark} or for the ability of anyone in
a leadership position to build strong movements]{.underline},
organizations, or workplaces.

Social change work requires experience, rigor, and study. [It can take
**years** of development to grow into a skillful organizer]{.underline},
strategist, communicator, campaign manager, or facilitator. Although
talented people can rapidly develop skills, [many capacities only
develop over time]{.underline}. For example, judgment, relationship
building, emotional maturity, and landscape awareness (including the
knowledge of what you don't know) deepen with experience.

"**[[Professionalization]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[and experience
are]{.underline}]{.mark} two **[[different]{.underline}]{.mark}** things
that should not be conflated. [[Most organizers]{.mark} historically and
globally [have not been developed through professional
pipelines]{.mark}]{.underline}. [However]{.underline}, [**[all]{.mark}**
skilled organizers]{.underline}---professional or otherwise---[[have
**rigorously honed**]{.mark} those [skills over time]{.mark} through
study and practice]{.underline}.

[[Pretending]{.mark} formal [leadership doesn't exist can **obscure
hierarchies** and create]{.mark} centers of **[informal
power]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[Formal leadership]{.underline}]{.mark},
when healthy, [[provides **clarity** and **transparency**]{.underline},
[which leads to **greater accountability**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[This]{.underline} in turn [fosters **more avenues for support** to
develop new leadership]{.underline}.

Finally, [there is a very real **intellectual** component to this
work]{.underline}. [[The idea that working people do not or cannot
engage in intellectual work is **truly elitist**]{.underline}]{.mark}
and has not been my experience.

Anti-Institutional Sentiment

Definition

Reflexively disdaining institutions and organizations as inherently
oppressive and antiquated, including the institution one may be
associated with. This point of view casts institutions themselves as the
problem, even those with a social change mission.

Fallacies

We all have examples of bloated, aimless, top-heavy, or simply
irrelevant institutions that take space, hoard power, and demonstrate
little impact. Even institutions that begin as disruptors of the status
quo can slow-walk toward conservatism, disconnection, bureaucracy,
irrelevance, and inefficiency. In fact, given the system of capitalism
our institutions are navigating, if we are not mindful of these
realities, we risk falling into them.

Despite those common pitfalls, [we need **institutions** for a
**powerful** and **durable** movement]{.underline}. [Organizations and
[institutions are **political vehicles**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [They are
also spaces [where individuals develop **skills**]{.mark}]{.underline}[,
**[connection]{.underline}**, [and **ideological
alignment**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Institutions transmit
**knowledge**]{.underline}, [hold **strategy**]{.underline}, [and
cultivate **power**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Atomized individuals that
loosely assemble **cannot do this at the scale needed**]{.mark} to take
on entrenched power]{.underline}.

[Arguing for doing away with political vehicles without offering a
**viable replacement** demonstrates a lack of a **power
analysis**]{.underline}. [The most [organized forces can **leverage
crisis**]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [so how can our movements
**win**]{.underline}]{.mark} [in moments of crisis [if we]{.mark}
**relinquish** our organized vehicles?]{.underline} [When our opposition
possesses disciplined and organized institutions at the ready to **fill
power vacuums**]{.underline}, [what does it mean to [**unilaterally
disarm**?]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### White chauvinism is a [political]{.underline} problem and assuming it [zeroes]{.underline} alt solvency is [proof]{.underline} of the link.

**Ford, 24**---Associate Professor of Education Studies, DePauw
University (Derek, "What's so Marxist about Marxist Educational

At the height of the CPUSA's influence, Black comrades were leading
theoreticians and organizers, standing united with white workers and the
global proletariat against a common enemy. Jones was largely responding
to [the **[right-wing shift]{.mark}** in the Party's

[[ranks]{.underline}]{.mark}" (Haywood 1978: 588). [Such [idealism
