### AT: Aff Key To Energize Movements

#### Solidarity link---voting aff [displaces]{.underline} political energies necessary to build organizations that can [endure]{.underline} through exhaustion.

**Dean, 23**---Professor of Politics at Hobart and William Smith
Colleges (Jodi, "Politics is Missing," Boston Review, Summer 2023, dml)

[[Enduring over the long haul is]{.underline}]{.mark} particularly
**[[challenging]{.underline}]{.mark}** for movements and radical
political associations. [Movements **ebb** and **flow**]{.underline};
[the spontaneity that generates enthusiasm **resists** channeling into
routines and institutions]{.underline}. [Radical politics demands
**enormous commitment**]{.underline}; [[even the most dedicated]{.mark}
organizers **[burn out]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Revolutionaries face
repression from the state, police harassment, and legal persecution.

To these challenges we should add the **[unique problems]{.underline}**
that [arise [from]{.mark}]{.underline} social media: [[argument without
**accountability**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [the seduction of [the **viral
hot take**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} the brutal [pleasures
that accrue from **demonstrating**]{.mark} **others'
[weaknesses]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [The reflexes]{.underline} honed
online [are **ill-suited** to the patient]{.underline},
[focused]{.underline}, [and mundane work of
politics]{.underline}---making placards, doing follow-up calls,
arranging events, finding a meeting time and location, distributing
fliers, and so on. [People who are intellectually or affectively
attracted by revolutionary ideals may **lack the disposition** for
sustained organizational labor]{.underline}.

[When left politics looks like]{.underline} movement without results,
whether as **[fiercely ineffectual]{.underline}** online
**[debates]{.underline}** or as inspiring mass demonstrations that end
with the election and reelection of the same old incumbents, [it's
**hard** to feel that showing up **makes a difference**]{.underline}. In
some of the 2020 protests I joined, after hours of marches, chants, and
standoffs with the police, organizers would thank the crowd for coming
out and remind us to vote. [If **voting** was the answer]{.underline},
[why did we spend]{.underline} the past seven **[hours]{.underline}**
pressed against barricades [in front of the **police
station**?]{.underline}

[Real **material** factors]{.underline} such as working two jobs, caring
for friends and family, trying to get through school, wariness of
encountering the police, and basic transportation [make a
difference]{.underline} in a large country with sprawling cities and
decaying infrastructure. [Time is **precious**]{.underline}. [Part of
the challenge of political endurance is providing people with the sense
that organizing is **worth their time**]{.underline}. [People show up
when they think showing up **matters**]{.underline}.

In her indispensable The Romance of American Communism (1977), Vivian
Gornick recounts a story that a woman named Belle told about her
seventy-year-old father. One snowy night, although bone tired, he got
ready to go back out into the cold to attend a rally at Madison Square
Garden. Belle tried to convince him to stay home. Her father's response:
"If I don't go, who will be there?" A committed member of the Communist
Party, he believed in showing up. He didn't think, "Oh well, there will
be so many people there, one more won't make a difference," or "It's
just another rally, so what." His party, his comrades, expected him to
show up, and he expected this of himself.

Political [[endurance is **strengthened** by purpose and
practice]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Strong]{.mark} political [organizations
give people a **reason** to show up]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[They explain
what they are fighting **against** and]{.mark} what they are fighting
**[for]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[They **clarify** why the fight
matters]{.underline}]{.mark}. The Communist Party in its heyday was
enormously skilled at drawing the connections between specific issues
and the long-term fight for socialism. Claudia Jones's famous
International Women's Day speech from 1950 is a great example. She
linked present struggles of progressive and Communist women with the
traditions of the great antislavery fighters Harriet Tubman and
Sojourner Truth, the militant textile workers of 1848, and women's
rights pioneers Susan B. Anthony and Elizabeth Cady Stanton. Jones
didn't worry about the differences between them. She drew a
centuries-long arc of courage and commitment. [The immediate political
goal was **unity**]{.underline}. The fight was for peace, equality, and
liberation which depended on organizing Black and white women in the
struggle for socialism. Jones embraced her listeners with
world-historical purpose. [Everyone counts in the battle]{.underline}
against the H-bomb, fascism, and imperialism.

The challenge of political endurance on the left today is that [people
**don't know** what they are fighting for]{.underline}. In recent
decades, **[millions]{.underline}** of people in the United States [have
come out to protest what they're **against**]{.underline}---the World
Trade Organization, the Iraq War, Wall Street, racist police murder.
[The movements **weaken** as they **fail** to give shape to a vision of
what they're **for**]{.underline}---[what does **winning** look
like?]{.underline} The advantage of organizations like the old Communist
Party is that they articulated such a vision: every local struggle and
specific issue gets its energy and purpose from the broader communist
horizon.

[[Correct practice is **essential** to political
endurance]{.underline}]{.mark}. These days [the jargon in [left
organizing spaces is **indistinguishable** from]{.mark}]{.underline}
corporate **[[h]{.underline}]{.mark}**uman
**[[r]{.underline}]{.mark}**esource [**[departments]{.underline}**.
[Disagreements are treated as **identity
differences**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Preoccupation with]{.mark}
interpersonal conflicts and [individual discomfort **displaces
attention**]{.mark} **away** [from political work]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[Political [organizations that orient their practices around their
**goals**]{.mark}]{.underline}---[[that make clear]{.mark} that people
have joined [a]{.mark} political [struggle]{.mark} that [requires
**discipline** and **unity**]{.mark}]{.underline} ([[not a **social
club**]{.underline}]{.mark})---[[navigate]{.mark} interpersonal [issues
by linking action to **purpose**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The old Communist
parties called this practice democratic centralism. Comrades debate,
vote, and accept the decision of the majority. Because keeping everyone
together is so important, [**debate** is conducted with an eye to
generating **unity**]{.underline}---[not scoring points]{.underline},
[blowing off steam]{.underline}, [or demonstrating one's knowledge of
theoretical arcana]{.underline}.

### AT: Alt Homogenizes/Excludes Individual Experience

#### Wrong---collective struggle doesn't require homogeneity, just similarity.

**Mokre, 24**---Institute of Culture Studies, Austrian Academy of
Sciences (Monika, "Negotiating identities. On identity politics and
deliberation," Identities, 2024, DOI: 10.1080/1070289X.2024.2393041,
dml)

There are some similarities between this understanding of the production
of 'the will of the people' and Hardt and Negri's considerations on the
question how the multitude can govern itself: Based again on Spinoza,
Negri postulates that individual desires can converge so that they 'are
displaced from the individual good to the collective good'. And,
according to Spinoza, this convergence happens when individual passions
are rejected in favour of reason (Field Citation2012, 25). [To direct
oneself towards the collective good **does not mean**]{.underline} for
Hardt and Negri to discuss issues of public interest in order [to
improve the existing political order]{.underline} but to struggle
against this 'alienating' political order. [The struggles of the
multitude are]{.underline}, [at the same time]{.underline}, [**local**
and **concrete**]{.underline} struggles, [and parts of transnational
struggles]{.underline}. In this way, [they **differ** from unified
struggles]{.underline} ([**subordinating** all other
struggles]{.underline}, e.g. [to]{.underline} the [class]{.underline}
struggles) [and **identity-based** struggles]{.underline} (Hardt and
Negri Citation2004, 217). '[Identity politics leads the struggle towards
emancipation of a certain group united based on the shared
identity]{.underline}, [but at the same time it **imposes** this
identity]{.underline}. Because of this imposition [the
struggle]{.underline} for the emancipation of a certain class, race,
gender, sexual identity [can serve as a means for rebellion and
emancipation]{.underline}, [but ultimately it **contradicts** the social
metamorphosis necessary for **truly revolutionary**
changes]{.underline}' (Reshe Citation2018). The hopes of Hardt and Negri
(or, rather, their expectations) for a revolution and a better society)
lie on the chaotic, dangerous multitude of which Hobbes was afraid, not
on stable group identities.

Even if one does not share Hardt and Negri's expectation and/or desire
for a revolutionary change their reflections on identity politics, on
the one hand, and general societal goals, on the other one, are of
relevance as they show a possibility to position identity politics
within the striving for the common good. [The starting
point]{.underline} of such an endeavour [would **not** be the assumption
that there is some **universal feature** such as rationality enabling
commonality but rather that one has to start from the **fiction** of the
possibility to understand each other in order to]{.underline} (in the
best case) [really understand each other after
deliberation]{.underline}. Thus, [deliberation]{.underline} in this
sense [does not start from **universalism** or **generalization** but
from the **specific position** of the speakers]{.underline}.Footnote1
'If cooperation or integration takes place without a place from which
one's own interests can be formulated, the disappearance of these
interests and adaptation to the dominant norms almost inevitably occurs'
(Meulenbelt Citation1993, 275, translation MM). [The driver]{.underline}
of such a deliberation [is not the assumption of a common
**base**]{.underline}, [a common **communicative
understanding**]{.underline}, [or common **goals** but the dispute about
**differences** and **fault lines** and the search for possible
commonalities or similarities]{.underline}. Thus, [striving for
**temporary alliances** and **common goals** is the aim]{.underline} of
such a deliberative procedure, [**not a generalized consensus** but
neither the re-affirmation of identities]{.underline}, [and
differences]{.underline}. '[Separatism as an **end** instead of a
**means** towards an end]{.underline} creates new ghettos and
[**excludes** possible allies]{.underline}' (Meulenbelt Citation1993,
275, translation MM). Bhatti's considerations on
[similarity]{.underline} as a dynamic concept rejecting homogeneity as
well as essentialised differences [can form an **analytical** as well as
**practical tool**]{.underline} here: 'The idea of similarity \[...\]
\[is\] suitable to dissolve rigid dichotomies and cultural hierarchies.
\[...\] As a cultural practice, the significance \[...\] of similarity
lies in the fact that it marks social processes. It is about a concept
of movement that represents a countermovement to the dominant
hermeneutics of the own and the foreign' (Bhatti and Kimmich
Citation2015, 18--19, translation MM).

### AT: Ballot=Symbolic Validation, Currency, Etc

#### Conflates [symbolic]{.underline} action of the ballot with [material differences]{.underline}, which [enables]{.underline} elite capture.

**Ludwig 25** \[David; Transdisciplinary researcher and philosopher
focused on questions of social and environmental change, associate
professor in the Knowledge, Technology, and Innovation Group of
Wageningen University; "It's a Shame That You Can't Afford Rent, But We
Can Offer Epistemic Compensation. On Relating Epistemic and Social
Justice"; Social Epistemology; Vol. 39; EC\]

Instead, [the issue is how **symbolic** and **material concerns** become
related within **struggles for reparation**]{.underline}. While
[symbolic reparations matter, they can also be mobilized to **sideline
questions of material reparations**:]{.underline} As Táíwò (2022a, 18)
puts the concern: '[elites can then exploit \[the distinction between
**symbolic** and **material reparations**\] choosing the version of
reparations that is **cheapest for them: the symbolic'**.]{.underline}
As Táíwò's (2022b) work on elite capture clarifies, [contestations of
symbolic reparations reflect wider political concerns about symbolic
inclusion that is not merely insufficient but can be **strategically
mobilized** to **legitimize institutions** and actors who materially
benefit from **pervasive inequality**.]{.underline}

[Concerns about misalignment between symbolic and material reparations
clearly extend to the epistemic domain, as 'symbolic reparations often
involve **distinctively epistemic goods** without identifying them as
such']{.underline} (Lackey 2022, 70). Again, [the concern should not be
misunderstood as the claim that epistemic recognition **does not
matter**.]{.underline} For example, Lackey builds her case for the
'right to be known' around the story of Lucas Baba Sikwepere who was
interviewed by the South African Truth and Reconciliation Commission
about being shot by a police officer of the apartheid regime and
survived with permanent blindness. Lackey (2022, 54) quotes from
Sikwepere's testimony: 'I feel what has been making me sick all the time
is the fact that I couldn't tell my story. But now I -- it feels like I
got my sight back by coming here and telling you the story'. Lackey
comments that the 'act of telling and being heard, especially after
suffering gross violations or injustice, reflects a deep human desire
for many' (2022, 61) and therefore argues that '[hearing the testimony
of victims is an end itself']{.underline} (2022, 61).

But even if we agree with Lackey's diagnosis, the issue of reparations
in South Africa also brings up difficult questions about the relation
between epistemic and social justice -- [how does the **right to be
known** relate to the right to be **materially
compensated**]{.underline}? In the case of South Africa, [it is hard to
ignore that progress regarding]{.underline} Lackey's (2022) [three main
categories of **epistemic reparations** -- public apologies,
memorialization and education -- contrasts starkly with **material
conditions** that continue to be structured by **colonial and apartheid
histories**]{.underline}. As Ramose (2012, 20) has argued 'from the
standpoint of African philosophy \[. . .\] reconciliation as a
legislative Act was a stillborn because of its objective reinforcement
of the poverty and wealth divide'. To illustrate this assessment with at
least one example and data point: In the Western Cape, the
agriculturally most productive region of the country, 'only 3% of
agricultural land \[. . .\] is owned by black farmers (African,
Coloured, and Indian)' (Villette 2018) despite constituting more than
80% of the population. In the South African context, [the challenge of
distorting priorities]{.underline} may therefore be
[expressed]{.underline} [through]{.underline} a variation of the
article's polemic title: [It's a shame that **you can't have your land
back**, but **we can offer epistemic reparations.**]{.underline}

The case of South Africa highlights the [importance of **addressing the
complex relations** between epistemic and social justice.]{.underline}
[Epistemic reparations have not served as **reliable tools** in
struggles for material reparations and the challenge of distorted
priorities]{.underline} [highlights that an isolated focus on the former
can actually become **part of the problem** rather than **part of the
solution**]{.underline}. The challenge here is not merely that 'other
issues also matter' but rather [how **foregrounding** of symbolic (or in
this case: epistemic) reparations can contribute to **sidelining**
material justice that affects livelihoods **more directly** but is also
**politically far more costly** to achieve. Even if ideal theories can
**integrate symbolic and material reparations** as **complementing**
rather than competing goals, the non-ideal reality of political
struggles often focuses on the **former** while **discrediting the
latter** as unreasonable radicalism.]{.underline}

While I have developed [the challenge]{.underline} of distorting
priorities through the example of 'epistemic reparations', it very much
[applies to the]{.underline} epistemic injustice [literature **at
large** and its tendency to approach]{.underline} '[epistemic justice as
a problem of recognition]{.underline}' (McConkey Citation2004). Although
much of the epistemic injustice scholarship embraces recognition theory
(Fricker Citation2018; Giladi Citation2018) and its critique of purely
distributive approaches to justice, it also points to more recent
frustrations with a superficial 'politics of recognition' and its
tendency to sideline concerns about the material conditions of
inequality.

[Indigenous scholar-activists]{.underline} like Coulthard (2014), for
example, have [argued that recognition of Indigenous culture **matters**
but has also become **misused** as a **substitute for redistribution**
of **economic resources** and **political power** to Indigenous
people.]{.underline} Similar [concerns appear in the **Black radical
tradition** that challenges **hollowed-out identity politics** and its
focus on **symbolic recognition** of **Black culture or Black
excellence** instead of resisting **material exploitation and
dispossession** of Black communities]{.underline} (James 2022; Táíwò
2022b). For example, James argues that [mainstreamed identity politics
has **betrayed** the **material demands of the Civil Rights Movement**
and become 'intoxicated with that **politics of symbolism** and less
willing to demand any kind of truly **redistributive materialist
agenda** for working-class and poor Black folk, who are the majority of
Black folk']{.underline} (2022, 221). Finally, consider [transnational
feminism]{.underline} (Mohanty 2003) [and its 'critique of white
feminists' pernicious mischaracterization of Third World women's
oppression as merely a worse case of gender oppression']{.underline}
(Herr 2014, 2), [focusing on issues such as recognition of sexual
identities or sexual violence in isolation of their entanglement with
the material conditions of class- and racebased oppression.]{.underline}

[All three traditions challenge a **narrow focus** on recognition while
also carefully avoiding accusations of 'economic reductionism' or 'class
reductionism']{.underline} that Spiegel (2022, 86) has [aptly described
as 'one of the **great anxieties**]{.underline} of 20th and 21st
\[century\] social philosophy'. Coulthard, for example, argues that the
[relations between Indigenous peoples and the]{.underline} Canadian
[**state** have become structured by a **superficial politics of
recognition** that puts heavy emphasis on the valuation of Indigenous
culture without undoing material dispossession and
exploitation]{.underline}. At the same time, Coulthard emphasizes that
Indigenous struggles do not reduce to struggles about the distribution
of economic resources (2014, 14). Instead, Coulthard's Red Skin, White
Masks follows Fanon's Black Skin, White Masks in [acknowledging the
importance of recognition to challenge framings of the colonized as
'uncivilized' and 'primitive' **while situating recognition** in
struggles for **material redistribution**]{.underline}: As Fanon argues,
[there is a '**double process**: primarily, economic; subsequently, the
internalization \[. . .\] of this inferiority.]{.underline} \[. . .\]
The black man must wage his war on both levels: Since [historically they
**influence each other**, any **unilateral liberation is incomplete**,
and the gravest mistake would be to believe in their automatic
interdependence']{.underline} (Fanon 1986, 13). Fanon's analysis
resonates with Coulthard's as [Indigenous struggles are indeed
**partially about recognition** while simultaneously **threatened by a
liberal 'politics of recognition'** that treats the **celebration** of
Indigenous culture as a **substitute for changing material
conditions.**]{.underline}

### AT: Ballot Fails

#### Ballot's both [necessary]{.underline} and [sufficient]{.underline}---solidarity is a [practice]{.underline} that starts at the most [quotidian]{.underline} levels like this debate.

**Taylor and Hunt-Hendrix, 24**---co-founder of The Debt Collective;
senior advisor at the American Economic Liberties Project, founder of
Way to Win and Solidaire (Astra and Leah, "Solidarity and the Sacred,"
*Solidarity: The Past, Present, and Future of a World-Changing Idea*,
Chapter 8, pp. 262-264, dml)

Where the previous two chapters focused on the larger domestic and
international structures that can help cultivate and sustain solidarity,
here we are focused on the actions that can be taken by individuals and
by smaller groups. [Transformative solidarity]{.underline} should aim to
transform policy, but this transformation [**begins** with
people]{.underline}---[with our **personal choices**]{.underline}. [We
all exist embedded in structures and systems that shape how we are able
to live out our lives]{.underline}, [but the way we **live** and
**decisions** we make can in turn **impact** those structures and
**alter** our societies]{.underline}, [for better and
worse]{.underline}. In Graeber's words, [human beings and the social
worlds we inhabit are]{.underline} "[projects of **mutual
creation**]{.underline}"---[our actions can either **reinforce the
status quo** or help **bring about**]{.underline} much-needed
**[change]{.underline}**.\[7\] On a fundamental level, solidarity comes
down to relationships.

When Durkheim observed that solidarity is created and sustained through
a shared sense of the sacred, he was saying that [solidarity is a
**practice**]{.underline}: [it must be **enacted** or else it **does not
exist**]{.underline}. Where [transformative solidarity]{.underline} is
concerned, this practice might entail attending a protest, sharing a
petition, or joining a political organization. But it also [manifests in
**smaller quotidian ways** through our **daily actions** and
**encounters**]{.underline}. [It can be]{.underline} useful to consider
solidarity as [a **habit of mind**]{.underline}, [or a particular kind
of **virtuous behavior**]{.underline}, [that orients us toward the
common good]{.underline}. Transformative solidarity, we argue, doesn't
require the creation of new religious or political dogma, but
[it]{.underline} does require a baseline of reverence for the fragile
ecosystems and communities on which we depend. Solidarity [asks each of
us to attend to those around us]{.underline}.

Consecrating the sacred, in Durkheim's sense, takes time. In the wake of
the French Revolution, the insurgent lawyer Maximilien Robespierre took
a universalist and authoritarian approach to the problem of [forging
common values]{.underline}. As the revolution shook the foundations of
the Church and the monarchy, Robespierre sought to replace these
institutions with a new religion: the Cult of the Supreme Being. It
lasted for around eight weeks before he was sent to the guillotine.\[8\]
Robespierre's absurd and tragic attempt to impose a new theological
doctrine [by **fiat** failed]{.underline}, [but over time the **French
Revolution**]{.underline}---[as a **collective practice** involving
hundreds of thousands of people and inspiring more across the
world]{.underline}, from Europe to the Caribbean---[did help **elevate**
and **spread** ideas that]{.underline} now fit Durkheim's definition of
sacred, including the tripartite motto of liberté, égalité, fraternité
that [helped pave the way for the modern emergence of
solidarity]{.underline}. As decades and centuries passed, these
principles and others---liberal democratic ideals of consent of the
governed, equality under the law, minority rights, religious tolerance,
freedom of expression, and so on---have become, in Durkheim's words,
"obligatory in a certain sense, because of the very fact of their being
held in common...they are to some extent indistinguishable from
religious beliefs proper."\[9\] [These principles have become part of
the **social fabric**]{.underline}. [Though we often fail to live up to
them in **practice**]{.underline}, [they serve as both a **baseline**
and a **beacon**]{.underline}. They are, in Durkheim's sense, sacred.

### AT: Don't Make Them Responsible

#### This proves our link args about individualization---it's not about [individual responsibility]{.underline}, but [collective liability]{.underline}.

**Taylor and Hunt-Hendrix, 24**---co-founder of The Debt Collective;

[But]{.underline}, the common retort goes, [[if we are not the

**directly**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[what]{.mark} kind of
[**responsibility** do we have?]{.mark}]{.underline} [What do I have to

Nigerian-American philosopher Olúfẹ́mi Táíwò empathizes with this

[[this]{.underline}]{.mark} way, Táíwò [[**rejects** the idea that we

majority of human beings are not **purely guilty** or **purely
innocent**]{.underline}, neither solely perpetrator nor solely victim.
Inspired by Martin Luther King Jr., Táíwò insists that "we are all
morally 'in the red.' " Furthermore, while it is clearly the case that
the governments of the Global North perpetrated severe violence on the
people of the Global South while also exploiting their labor, as we have
noted, the economic rewards have been unevenly distributed.
Working-class white Americans saw their jobs evaporate and livelihoods
diminish, even as they enjoyed more privileges than their Black and

were likely better off than the individuals working in the maquiladoras
and sweatshops of the Global South. These complexities are part of the

culpability]{.underline}---[[sometimes we are **liable** for things that

**created**]{.underline} the [abject living and working
conditions]{.underline} of the factory workers on the southern side of
the U.S. border, [but we are **bound** to them
nevertheless]{.underline}. [To improve their lot is also to improve our
own]{.underline}.\[110\]

### AT: Multiple Strategies Good

#### [Do not abandon the line struggle]{.underline} -- principled disagreement over specific lines between parallel movements creates the conditions for the emergence of a unified party, [but]{.underline} it requires [attention to and debate around]{.underline} difference rather than meaningless calls for "left unity." Holding each other's politics [accountable]{.underline} is good and [necessary]{.underline} to build a better world.

**Moufawad-Paul 13** -- Joshua Moufawad-Paul is a Canadian academic and
writer from Toronto, Canada, and a professor of philosophy at York
University. "Unity in Struggle and Struggle in Unity", M-L-M Mayhem,
July 30, 2013,
<http://moufawad-paul.blogspot.com/2013/07/unity-in-struggle-and-struggle-in-unity.html>
// rose

Thus, while there are admittedly internecine squabbles amongst the
general left that can be classified as \"sectarian\", there are also
principled lines of demarcation that are drawn for very good reasons. In
a first world context that is not yet revolutionary (even if the
objective circumstances are ripe, the subjective circumstances are
horribly underdeveloped), it is impossible for the movement to narrow
into a focused point where a vanguard can clearly emerge and, in this
emergence, settle these squabbles by winning over the masses while, at
the same time, isolating and eradicating those factions that refuse, out
of sectarian pride and a practical divorcement from the revolutionary
instance, to recognize an emergent vanguard. We are not at this point,
and some anti-capitalist groups will even argue that we should never be
at this point because they deny the necessity of a revolutionary
vanguard. Point being: there are multiple left-wing organizations and
factions, many of which exist for good reason, that are either
struggling to emerge as the revolutionary vanguard or struggling for a
movement that lacks a vanguard altogether.

[Here it needs to be said that in the current conjuncture at the centres
of capitalism there is nothing wrong with this multiplicity. Although I
believe that [it is erroneous to make]{.mark} the fact of [multiplicity
a]{.mark} principle of revolutionary [strategy]{.mark} (hence my
rejection of \"movementism\"), I do not think we should see the concrete
reality of multiple left tendencies as something to be feared. [It is
just a fact]{.mark}, and a fact we have inherited from a history of
revisionism and failure, that can only be overcome by engaging with this
multiplicity and, in the process, winning over the masses to whatever
organization succeeds in emerging as the party of the advanced
guard.]{.underline} Until then, the fact of leftist multiplicity is
something we have to engage with because it is a fact. And though I
realize that some groups operate in denial of this fact, it is my
contention that this willful obliviousness will only harm their
development. [For even if we believe that there needs to come a time
when [a revolutionary organization unified in theory and practice
becomes the]{.mark} revolutionary [organization in a given]{.mark}
social [context]{.mark}, such an organization will grow into this
organization through its engagement with other left-wing organizations.
That is, we can learn from different tendencies; we can grow by
interacting with them]{.underline}.

Herein lies the actual distinction between a sectarian and a
non-sectarian approach to anti-capitalism: whereas the sectarian will
loathe these multiple trajectories, and thus fear the dilution of a pure
politics that results from multiplicity, the non-sectarian will accept
such a multiplicity as a fact and try to work with this fact. For this
fact is the fact of line struggle, and it is nothing to be feared;
struggling with and sometimes against different leftist factions is only
a problem if one fears political pollution----and only the cultist fears
such pollution[. And yet it is worth repeating, as I have argued over
and over, that to adhere to [a principled political position is
not]{.mark} the same as [being sectarian:]{.mark} while [we should not
fear line struggle]{.mark}, and need to accept that there are currently
multiple political positions within the left, it is a matter of
principle to argue, especially if one adheres to a line that treats the
concept of a party vanguard as significant, that one theoretically
unified organization can and should emerge as hegemonic.]{.underline}

The multiplicity of trajectories, then, is a fact that cannot be denied;
the hope that these trajectories will be subsumed by a unified
organization that can account for interior multiplicity is something
some of us believe is worth considering. [To]{.underline} briefly
demystify this jargon-laced set of sentences: there was a multiplicity
of political organizations in Russia at the turn of the 20th Century
that provided the crucible for the emergence of a single vanguard
organization that was forced to prove itself in this context; there was
a multiplicity of political lines and factions in China in the early to
mid 20th century that produced a splintering revolutionary party in the
countryside that, by 1949, proved it was the vanguard. [[Neither]{.mark}
of these [world historical revolution]{.mark}s [would have been possible
without a context]{.mark} of revolutionary ferment [in which a
multiplicity of political lines vied for dominance and,]{.mark} in this
vying, produced the circumstances that could narrow into a revolutionary
event where [one line]{.mark}, that had learned from the others, would
[arise as revolutionary]{.mark}.]{.underline}

2: theoretical difference as unavoidable and necessary

[We are stuck with this contradiction: [we have to work together
and]{.mark} we have to [struggle against each
other.]{.mark}]{.underline} Work together because we all believe that
capitalism needs to be overcome. [Struggle against each other because
t[here are real differences that will]{.mark} necessarily [be
encountered]{.mark} in any attempt to supersede capitalism]{.underline}.
These differences are not simply the result of sectarian differences but
are visceral realities... Take, for example, my anecdote about
Palestinian self-determination mentioned earlier----this was not a
sectarian difference but a matter of principle that could only result in
a different method of theory and practice. [Other examples abound:
questions of [race and gender during]{.mark} the US revolutionary
[struggles in the]{.mark} 196[0s and]{.mark} 19[70s were significant
dividing lines]{.mark}----and for good reason!]{.underline}

To be fair, there are theoretical differences amongst marxist
organizations that are quite silly, and that nobody these days probably
cares about, that result from theoretical hair-splitting that is about
as useful as theological debates in medieval Catholicism. If an
organization cannot get past an arcane difference that has nothing to do
with concrete struggle, and might only exist in order to define oneself
as a more faithful (in the religious sense) marxist than another sect,
then they probably cannot work with other members of the left. Again:
this is the hallmark of sectarianism.

But we need to recognize the fact, noted in the section above, that
there are principled lines of demarcation that are important and cannot
simply be misconstrued as \"sectarian\" simply because they maintain
principled points of difference. [Principled difference is not
sectarianism, and those who would like the entire left to believe the
same thing and get along as a single organization are often those who,
in their attempt to obliterate difference and multiplicity, are guilty
of, implicitly or explicitly, attempting to subordinate the movement to
their own ideological position. That is, the political position that
often masquerades as \"anti-sectarian\" is itself a form of sectarianism
in that it is actually arguing, even if it is in denial, \"why can\'t
everyone get along according to my principles of unity?\"]{.underline}

[If we should not fear multiple anti-capitalist tendencies, then we
should not fear the fact that [difference]{.mark} between tendencies
sometimes [exists for a good reason.]{.mark} Moreover, [we should be
prepared to accept]{.mark} that a [rejection and]{.mark} furious [line
struggle against a given tendency]{.mark} is necessary for the
development of a movement, [especially if]{.mark} this line struggle is
intrinsically [wed to praxis rather than]{.mark} simply an exchange of
[polemics]{.mark}.]{.underline}

What I mean here is that in the course of revolutionary practice there
often develops a theoretical line, based on what encounters in the
concrete world, that sets one organization apart from another. Take, for
example, the development of the political line of the Bolshevik party
led by Lenin and how it set Lenin\'s faction apart from other communist
factions in Russia and in the International Communist Movement. Take,
for example, the development of the political line in the communist
faction in China led by Mao that set it apart from the original
Communist Party of China. [These differences mattered because they had
to do with making revolution; to ignore these differences for the sake
of some \"[let\'s-just-all-get-along\" socialism would be tantamount to
abandoning revolutionary struggle.]{.mark}]{.underline}

For there are indeed political lines that need to be struggled against
and marxist theory has been enriched because of this line struggle. We
cannot forget that Marx and Engels established the foundations of
historical materialism and its primacy as the revolutionary science of
the working class by waging line struggle against other tendencies in
the movement of their time. The hope, as I argued in the Party as
Process, is that line struggles between multiple organizations----which
we should not necessarily see as sectarian, nor as something to be
loathed----will be won by an emergent vanguard that proves its line in
the concrete class struggle of its social context.

### AT: No Solidarity With Racists

#### The alt doesn't require solidarity with bigots, but [does]{.underline} allow for the possibility of [renouncing]{.underline} bigotry. If the aff rejects that, we're [impact turning]{.underline} it.

**Taylor and Hunt-Hendrix, 24**---co-founder of The Debt Collective;
senior advisor at the American Economic Liberties Project, founder of
Way to Win and Solidaire (Astra and Leah, "Us vs. Them," *Solidarity:
The Past, Present, and Future of a World-Changing Idea*, Chapter 2, pp.
65-69, dml)

American conservatives, for example, increasingly seem dead set on
antagonistic conflict; they frame immigrants and leftists as an
existential threat. As Kevin Phillips, the infamous Nixon advisor, said
to a journalist during the campaign in 1968, "The whole secret of
politics is knowing who hates who."\[28\] This is an orientation that
stands in stark contrast to [the nonviolent tradition]{.underline}
progressives admire, to the teachings of Mahatma Gandhi and Martin
Luther King Jr., who called on their followers to love their enemies.
Both King and Gandhi had sharp critiques of those who create and sustain
injustice, yet they nevertheless [believed that nonviolent action should
seek **redemption** of political enemies rather than total
**defeat**]{.underline}. **[King]{.underline}** movingly [envisioned
a]{.underline} "**[beloved community]{.underline}**," [where even former
opponents could **make amends** and in so doing become part of a
**stronger** and **larger** Us]{.underline}. In a similar vein,
[**Hayes** and **Kaba** talk about the need to foster]{.underline} what
they call [a]{.underline} "**[counterculture of rebellious
care]{.underline}**," [which **refuses** to see people as **unworthy of
compassion** or **incapable of change**]{.underline}. \[29\]

We, too, believe that [we must not forget the **human capacity for
change**]{.underline}, and that [the ultimate goal is
**transformation**]{.underline}, [not **annihilation**]{.underline}, [of
our adversaries]{.underline}. [But this conviction is **not
incompatible** with the recognition that certain structural positions in
our society should **cease to exist**]{.underline}, [and that we should
**oppose proponents** of an unjust and violent society with **all our
might**]{.underline}. A democratic socialist, King understood that [our
economy must be **transformed** so that **no dominant class stands to
profit** from other people's exploitation and degradation]{.underline}.
[The point isn't to **vanquish individuals**]{.underline}; [it's to
**remake unjust systems**]{.underline}. This is why it is so absurd when
financiers compare hostility toward the wealthy to the Nazis'
fanaticism, as Blackstone CEO Stephen Schwarzman did in an overwrought
response to efforts to close the carried-interest loophole: "It's like
when Hitler invaded Poland in 1939."\[30\] Obviously the effort to make
the tax code more equitable is in no way equivalent to extinguishing a
human life or carrying out genocide. In fact, a more equitable tax code
is one way to save quite a few lives through expanded health and social
services.

Addressing these structural injustices, and disempowering those who
profit from and perpetuate them, requires polarization, not
de-escalation. To build a transformative majority capable of taking on
an increasingly greedy elite and lethal far right, we need to clearly
define an adversary. The process of polarization ---identifying two
sides in a struggle and naming the source of oppression and
exploitation---is a necessary component of building transformative
solidarity, and of any successful social movement. Importantly, though,
[the boundary between Us and Them need not be **unyielding** or
**eternally hostile**]{.underline}. [It's **preferable**]{.underline},
[and **more strategic**]{.underline}, [for the border to be
**porous**]{.underline}, allowing members to come in and out.
[Parishioners of a **Catholic congregation**]{.underline}, for example,
[might join with a community organization to petition for a **living
wage** for city employees]{.underline}, [but might be on the other side
of]{.underline} the table during [a debate about **reproductive
justice**]{.underline}. Or [consider what happened in
**2023**]{.underline}, [when the three women **Republican
representatives** in the South Carolina state senate reached across
party lines in an attempt to block a proposed **abortion
ban**]{.underline}.\[31\] **[Saul Alinsky]{.underline}**, the legendary
political activist who brought the model of labor organizing into
neighborhoods and communities like the Chicago stockyards and wrote
about his model in Rules for Radicals, was known for his maxim "[no
permanent **friends** and no permanent **enemies**]{.underline}."\[32\]
[There should]{.underline}, instead, [be permanent
**principles**]{.underline}, [and in pursuit of those
principles]{.underline}, [coalition partners will **change** depending
on the specific campaigns and circumstances]{.underline}.

There are a number of different ways polarization, or a distinction
between Us and Them, can be visualized spatially. Nationalism, for
example, functions on an inside/outside basis, where borders---and the
state and private-sector apparatuses protecting those borders---keep
outsiders at bay, giving the nation- state its cohesion and citizenship
its exclusive status. Class struggles have historically been referred to
as top versus bottom, while political debates are typically drawn as
Left versus Right. The challenge, for progressives, is acknowledging
multiple sources of oppression at once. Given all the different ways
that exploitation happens, the lines between allies and adversaries can
quickly become muddled.

We need to draw our lines with care, aware that some kinds of
polarization conceal others. To return to nationalism as a prime
example: many socialists and communists refused to take a side during
World War I, fearing that it would stoke nationalist sympathies and
dampen the prospects for international working-class solidarity. Voicing
this sentiment is what got the celebrated American socialist organizer
and five-time presidential candidate Eugene Debs convicted of sedition.
In 1918, Debs denounced the First World War; the only war in which he
would enlist, he proclaimed, was what he called "the holy war of the
workers of the world against the exploiters of the world."\[33\]
Nationalism, Debs believed, was a ruling-class ideology that covered up
class exploitation with patriotism, obscuring the fact that rich and
poor have opposing interests despite sharing a homeland. Nationalism
distracts from domestic inequities---from the fact that some citizens
own multiple vacation homes while others sleep in their cars---by
focusing on an external threat, including distant governments or
vulnerable immigrants and refugees. This is true of [many forms of
reactionary solidarity]{.underline} that [serve to **set us against each
other** and **hide the true enemy**]{.underline}: [pitting]{.underline}
citizens against foreigners, [white people against racialized
people]{.underline}, and immigrant workers against unionized workers
**[lets the ruling class off the hook]{.underline}**.

As the authors of the Combahee River Collective Statement understood,
[any analysis limited to a **single form** of
oppression]{.underline}---whether based on national origin, race,
gender, sexuality, or class---[will **inevitably leave people out** and
work **against** solidarity rather than build a bigger Us]{.underline}.
[Efforts focused only on the problem of **patriarchy**]{.underline}
without thinking about race or class, for example, [risk perpetuating
inequality and devolving into a **hollow corporate
feminism**]{.underline}, pushing for women to serve as high-powered CEOs
and public officials, as though equal opportunity to pay workers
starvation wages and jail immigrants at the border is the true aim of
gender justice. Likewise, [singular attention to **race** can produce
the same effects]{.underline}, [leading to]{.underline} the phenomenon
Keeanga-Yamahtta Taylor describes as "**[Black faces in high
places]{.underline}**," [providing a patina of diversity while
neoliberal capitalism continues to **wreak havoc** on people and the
planet]{.underline}.\[34\] Meanwhile, understanding capitalism, and the
role of capitalists in perpetuating inequity, is central, but [a
**class-only** analysis risks **missing** the myriad ways
racism]{.underline}, [patriarchy]{.underline}, [xenophobia]{.underline},
[and ableism shape people's reality]{.underline}. [A **careless** or
**reductive refusal** to acknowledge the complexity of people's lives
**hampers** our ability to bring them into the movement and creates a
**weakened** and **impoverished** Us]{.underline}.

The task of tackling multiple, interlocking sources of oppression at
once is daunting, but it is the only worthwhile way to proceed. As our
understanding of the causes of injustice deepens, taking into account
the impacts of racism, sexism, heteronormativity, economic exploitation,
climate degradation, and more, [we are in danger of having **so many
opponents** that potential allies come to us **tarnished** by their
association with some form of oppression]{.underline}. [But turning on
each other is a **dead end**]{.underline}. [We must face these
complications]{.underline}, [and the reality of our own complicity with
oppressive systems]{.underline}, [with humility]{.underline}, [and
without losing sight of our larger aims]{.underline}. [We must
remember]{.underline} Alinsky's maxim of "**[no permanent
enemies]{.underline}**," [as well as our goal]{.underline}: [not
opposition for its **own sake**]{.underline}, [but the building of the
**largest** and **most powerful** Us possible]{.underline}. This is why
we need solidarity. [Our real adversaries are those who **purposely
engage** in tactics of **divide** and **conquer** for personal and
political gain]{.underline}. [This exploitation]{.underline}, [and the
underlying systems that enable it]{.underline}, [must be
opposed]{.underline}. To do that, the rest of us need to get
organized--- which is much easier said than done.

### AT: No Internal Link

#### It's not about individual action but systemic dynamics.

**Thomas, 25**---Professor of Sociology at the State University of New
York at Oneonta (Alexander, "Against Sociology," *The Sociological
Predicament: Academia and the Contradictions of Working Class
Consciousness*, Chapter 2, pp. 44, dml)

The self-anointing by sociologists to leadership of the Left was, to
again borrow the phrase from Olúfẹ́mi Táíwò, an unacknowledged version of
elite capture.46 Paradoxically, [elite capture does not require
**individual** academics to **consciously attempt** to support the
dominant system]{.underline}---[elite capture is a **system
effect**]{.underline}. As Táíwò notes:

Elite capture is not a conspiracy. [It's **bigger** than cynical
appropriations]{.underline}, [opportunism]{.underline}, [or the moral
successes or failures of any **individual**]{.underline} or group. [It
is a]{.underline} kind of **[system behavior]{.underline}**---a
phenomenon articulated at the population level, [an
**observable**]{.underline} (**[predictable]{.underline}**) [**pattern**
of actions involving individuals]{.underline}, [groups]{.underline},
[and subgroups]{.underline}, [**each** of whom may be pursuing **any
number of different goals** from their own narrow point of
view]{.underline}. [Elite capture is **not limited to the scope of their
interactions**]{.underline}. [The **constant dynamic** of individual and
group interactions makes up a **social system**]{.underline}, [and elite
capture emerges out of that dynamic]{.underline}.47

[The **systemic effect** of an individual wanting to do **one**
thing]{.underline} (fight!) [and the system]{.underline} itself
[**imposing** its **own logic**]{.underline} (protect the interests of
the top 20 percent) [is at the **heart** of the]{.underline}
sociological [predicament]{.underline}.

The Predicament

The sociological predicament is that [any action or inaction will
**reaffirm**]{.underline}, [even **reify**]{.underline}, [the underlying
social structure]{.underline}. This fact emerges from the dynamics
underlying social evolution. [A society based on the **extraction** of
surplus from one population for the benefit of another
will]{.underline}, [even with the **reshuffling** of power
relations]{.underline}, [**regress** over time to unequal
political-economic relations]{.underline}.48 This deep structure imposes
itself on the larger society, negating utopian goals of building a great
society. This is true even if the lion's share of exchange is through
equitable trade as the presence of unequal trade eventually tilts back
to growing inequality. [The uncovering of these dynamics results in a
desire]{.underline}, even a desperation, [to attempt to change the

### AT: No Theory Of X Violence

#### This is the link, [not]{.underline} an alt answer. If we win the alt's [materially]{.underline} effective, then rejecting it on [theoretical]{.underline} grounds perpetuates [anticommunist dogma]{.underline}. 

**Ford, 24**---Associate Professor of Education Studies, DePauw
University (Derek, "What's so Marxist about Marxist Educational
Theory?," Policy Futures in Education, 22(8), 1570-1587, dml)

In my reading, Dumas explains [the **endless spiral** of]{.underline}
the [debates]{.underline}: [they're **asking the wrong
questions**!]{.underline} If this is the case, then Dumas is more of a
Marxist than the (neo)Marxists. First, Dumas\' methodology is the same
that Marx used to develop his unfinished critique of capitalism. Second,
Dumas\' [refusal to tussle over **what theory is superior** aligns with
Marx's organizing and theorizing]{.underline}, in which he embraced
united fronts with various and even antagonistic political tendencies.
Third, Dumas, like Marx, views theories as dynamic and [in]{.underline}
motion, [**not even suggesting** a theory can have the]{.underline}
"**[final word]{.underline}**" [forever and for all time]{.underline}.
My research works from a void Dumas helps me spot in the race-class
debates: any engagement with the actual and ongoing world-historic
transformations fighting to overthrow racist oppression and imperialist
exploitation. The roots of this absence boil down to [the **real
dogmatism** holding activist scholarship back]{.underline}:
**[anticommunism]{.underline}**. I hold that [this]{.underline} dogma
[keeps potentially radical work **circling** within a **spiral**
of]{.underline}, in Gabriel Rockhill's (2021: 133) terms, critical

amount to [theory that is **overwhelmingly critical** of the praxis of

legacies on which we build, for all their tragedies and defeats, for

and produced with certain intentions. [The very real racial divisions

factors**]{.underline}, [but the global revolutionary era led in large

Simply put, [we **can't overcome** the class-race divide
without]{.underline} simultaneously, or beforehand, [**breaching** the
theory-reality divide]{.underline}. [As]{.underline} Fred
**[Hampton]{.underline}** (2014: 139) flawlessly [phrased
it]{.underline} while speaking on the Black Panther Party's free
breakfast programs, "we sayin" something like this---we saying that
[theory's **cool**]{.underline}, [but theory with no practice **ain't
shit**]{.underline}. Speaking academically relative to the race-class
literature, the problem I'm identifying is similar but even more
elementary in that [it isn't even a matter of **practice**]{.underline};
[it's a need to take **material reality** and **history** into
account]{.underline}. [Without the latter]{.underline}, **[even theory
ain't shit]{.underline}**. [Rather than **what theory**]{.underline} or
theorist [is **superior**]{.underline}, [my project is guided by the
objectives to **be accomplished** and the objectives we **have
accomplished**]{.underline} (Ford 2023b). [The]{.underline} class-race
[divide **can't be bridged** in the realm of **thought** because it is
the **product** of academic thought]{.underline}. To be sure, all of us
engaged in liberatory work can undertheorize or inaccurately relay the
sources on which we base our critiques, build our theories, organize

Yet, [the **fundamental problem** is the idea that we can **test the
effectivity of any political theory on paper**]{.underline} or in the

is]{.underline}, above all else, [a theoretical weapon used to
**understand** and **transform** the world that **varies** depending on

The fact that this foundational tenet of Marxism is rarely addressed or
even asked in educational research raised the question with which I
titled this paper: "What's so Marxist about Marxist educational theory?"
"This is a kind of rebuttal to Antonia Darder and Rodolfo Torres" (2011)

conjuncture**]{.underline}. The question posed in the title is sincere
nonetheless, and I start pursuing it by highlighting some contemporary
CRT scholarship, showing that it's fertile ground for advancing
pedagogical and political praxis against capitalist exploitation, white
supremacy, and other forms of oppression.

### AT: Perm

### AT: What Does The Alt Do/State Bad/Communism Fails

#### Alt generates a [policy feedback loop]{.underline} by forming a [solidarity state]{.underline} based in [democratic input]{.underline}, [public accountability]{.underline}, [reparative justice]{.underline}, and [collective ownership]{.underline}.

**Taylor and Hunt-Hendrix, 24**---co-founder of The Debt Collective;
senior advisor at the American Economic Liberties Project, founder of
Way to Win and Solidaire (Astra and Leah, "A Solidarity State,"
*Solidarity: The Past, Present, and Future of a World-Changing Idea*,
Chapter 6, pp. 213-215, dml)

[By emphasizing **democratic input** and
**accountability**]{.underline}, [a solidarity state poses an **equal
challenge** to actually existing capitalist and communist economic
paradigms]{.underline}, [both of which]{.underline}, despite their
immense and important differences, [**embolden** elite administrators
and profiteers]{.underline}. As Rosa Luxemburg warned in The Russian
Revolution, "[Public control is **indispensably
necessary**]{.underline}," [or]{.underline} else "[corruption becomes
**inevitable**]{.underline}."\[99\] In his famous study of the German
Social Democratic Party (the same party that Luxemburg battled with),
sociologist Robert Michels dubbed this tendency the "iron law of
oligarchy."\[100\] Michels argued that while individual socialists may
have won elections and influence--- gaining control over the party's
finances, press, and key positions---socialism lost.

Today in the U.S., the corruption Luxemburg warned of manifests whenever
elites monopolize access to ostensibly public resources or profit from
their absence: for example, by harnessing public banking to protect the
interests of the fossil fuel industry, as in the case of the Bank of
North Dakota, or reaping the rewards of the public housing voucher
system, as many corporate developers and landlords now do. [Cultivating
the **countervailing power** of solidarity is an **important tool** to
resist this oligarchic disposition]{.underline}. [A solidarity state
would ensure that anything]{.underline} "**[public]{.underline}**"
[involves a commitment to advancing **parity**]{.underline}, both in
terms of [promoting economic equity and power-sharing]{.underline},
[decentralizing capital]{.underline}, [and democratizing
control]{.underline}. [One]{.underline} inspiring [example]{.underline}
of this [is Costa Rica's]{.underline} Banco Popular y de Desarrollo
Comunal (**[BPDC]{.underline}**), which has been called
[the]{.underline} "[most democratic bank in the
world]{.underline}."\[101\] [A non-state but public
institution]{.underline}, [fully owned by]{.underline} the
[workers]{.underline} of Costa Rica, [it has a mandate to]{.underline}
"[serve the social and sustainable welfare of Costa Rican
inhabitants]{.underline}" [and is governed by a Workers'
Assembly]{.underline} made up of 290 individuals representing 10
different social and economic sectors. In line with the demands of the
solidarists and socialists of the late nineteenth century, [the Banco
Popular provides credit to]{.underline} workers, artisans, small
producers, communal development associations, municipalities,
cooperatives, unions, and development projects that
[benefit]{.underline} "**[collective welfare]{.underline}**," including
the welfare of the planet.\[102\]

Adding to their appeal, [more solidaristic and participatory governance
models not only help ensure **shared prosperity** and
**power**]{.underline}, [they also function as a **bulwark against elite
capture**]{.underline}. Paradoxically, pure state ownership can
sometimes make it easier for public goods to be destroyed for private
benefit by those who want to eliminate or sell them off (think of Bill
Clinton's destruction of tens of thousands of units of public
housing).\[103\] [We can see this in **Denmark**]{.underline}, [where
the majority of **utilities**]{.underline} (water, electricity, and
heat) [and]{.underline} a substantial portion of social [**housing** are
cooperatively owned]{.underline}, thanks to the efforts of workers'
movements past. According to former member of Danish Parliament Pelle
Dragsted, "[even if a neoliberal government **really wanted** to
commodify and privatise it]{.underline}, [they
**couldn't**]{.underline}, [because they didn't **own**
it]{.underline}."\[104\] Instead, these essential resources are in truly
public hands.

[The creation of public goods that are **accessible to everyone** has
the added benefit of]{.underline} what scholars call [a]{.underline}
"**[policy feedback loop]{.underline}**," a term for the ways [policy
**shapes consciousness** and **creates commitments** that can be
**difficult to change**]{.underline}, in ways both good and ill.
[Universal programs]{.underline}, like Social Security, [create a more
**solidaristic feedback loop**]{.underline}, while fragmented or
means-tested programs, like those to which welfare rights organizers
objected decades ago, reach narrowly defined groups and often create
confusion and resentment. [Broad and inclusive programs give people
**common stakes**]{.underline}, [creating constituencies that are
**invested** in **defending gains** from being rolled
back]{.underline}.\[105\] (They are also typically more cost-effective:
means-tested benefits are usually more expensive than universal ones
because of the costs associated with administrative systems that vet
applicants.)\[106\]

However, it is not enough for [policies and public goods]{.underline} to
be universal---as anthropologist Hannah Appel argues, they [also need to
be **reparative**]{.underline}.\[107\] To be truly equitable, [policies
and public goods must be structured in ways that **account for** and
seek to **redress** historical injustices and ongoing
disparities]{.underline}, which means they must explicitly acknowledge
the negative material consequences of racism, sexism, and ableism on
millions of people's lives. For example, in order to be reparative, [a
**job guarantee** might prioritize funding care work generally done by
women or cleaning up sites poisoned by environmental
racism]{.underline}, all based on the recognition that women and Black
people were left out of the New Deal. [A reparative approach to
**finance** would invest in communities and households that have long
been victims of predatory lending or denied access to
credit]{.underline}; [it could create social housing in neighborhoods
devastated by redlining or open public banks in neighborhoods currently
taken advantage of by payday lenders and check-cashing
companies]{.underline}. [A reparative resolution to **drug policy**
could take inspiration from **the 2022 Rhode Island** cannabis
legalization legislation that sets aside a substantial portion of
limited retail licenses for worker-owned businesses]{.underline}. [This
is the largest expansion of state support for cooperative enterprises in
recent history and a way of **explicitly ensuring** that income and
opportunities will flow to the low-income communities most harmed by the
War on Drugs]{.underline}.\[108\] [A reparative approach to **democracy
reform** might prioritize representational parity by instituting quotas
to ensure that]{.underline} women and people from [marginalized or
colonized communities have a political voice]{.underline}, as other
countries around the world do.

# Case

## Presumption

### Presumption---V1---1NC

#### Presumption:

#### Vote neg, they haven't met their burden of proof. \<[The 1AC already performed their method; their ev is about groups doing good stuff in the status quo; insert other examples from their speeches/cross-x/evidence as appropriate]{.mark}\>

#### This is [offense]{.underline}. Symbolically affirming their method despite a lack of institutional and material commitments [weakens]{.underline} radical praxis.

**Rosenberg, 22**---Associate Professor of Gender, Sexuality, and
Feminist Studies and History, Duke University (Gabriel, "The Lazy
Bear\'s Guide to Effective Anti-Imperialism,"
<https://bearistotle.substack.com/p/the-lazy-bears-guide-to-effective>,
dml)

[Because anti-war [rhetoric]{.mark} is **so easily abused** it [must be
**matched** by **institutional** and **material
commitments**]{.mark}]{.underline} you simply won't find in either
political party as they are currently constituted. Of course, [the
question of when military force is warranted is **not**]{.underline} a
**[simple]{.underline}** one. It's a moral question, entangled in
interpretation, speculation, and clouded by selfish interests. I tend to
answer it with strong skepticism of any and all military action, but I
wouldn't defend categorical pacifism. I think, for example, that US
entry into World War II was justified, as was decisive federal military
action against the treasonous slave power during the Civil War.
[Pacifism is a fine way to evaluate military action]{.underline}...
[right up until there's a **good** and **justified reason** for the use
of force]{.underline}. When pushed, many pacifists will admit that there
probably are conditions under which they would endorse a military
response (Nazis! Invading xenocidal lizard people! All of the above!).
[Practical pacifists and warmongers are **not always rhetorically
distinguishable**]{.underline}: [both may well describe military action
as a lamentable final course of action prompted by exceptional
danger]{.underline}. Given this, how can we ensure that military force
is, in fact, the exceptional course of action?

[My recommendation is to [**concretize** skepticism]{.mark} of
militarism in **institutional arrangements**]{.underline}, [[to **bend
institutional defaults** away from militarism]{.mark} such that
war-making capacities must be fabricated before they can be deployed and
are starkly limited in duration]{.underline}. In simpler terms, [[it
should be **politically**]{.underline}, **[socially]{.underline}**, [and
**logistically difficult**]{.underline}]{.mark} [for politicians [to
prosecute a war and we should **organize**]{.mark} **government
[agencies]{.mark}**]{.underline}[, [including **budgets**]{.underline},
[to reflect this]{.underline}]{.mark} [fact]{.underline}. That's a good
reason not to deploy troops to Ukraine, but it's also a good reason not
to fund the military as we do.

This is, to a great extent, a practical lesson I take from the history
of the two military engagements I named above as justifiable. Both the
Civil War and World War II were watershed moments in the construction of
American state capacity. In both cases, the Federal government was faced
with such a pressing military danger that it had to whip up popular
support for military action and constitute a fighting force. That
entailed dramatic and unpopular disruptions of daily life in which
average citizens were expected to subordinate their own interests to
those of the state and war effort. And it required the creation of vast
new state bureaucracies and unprecedented interventions in the national
economy. And it also involved important and persistent constitutional
reorderings that fundamentally altered the shape of how lawmaking,
courts, and ordered liberty functioned. In the case of the Civil War,
many of those constitutional and governmental reorganizations were ended
in a premature fashion, leading to the failure of Reconstruction. But in
the case of World War II, the United States never really wound down what
historian James T. Sparrow terms "the warfare state," instead rolling it
into, first, the Cold War State and, now, the National Security State. I
think that was a grievous error precisely because it has allowed 80
years of conniving and opportunistic politicians to free-ride on the
necessary national mobilization executed by Roosevelt. We no longer need
to reorganize American society to prosecute a war; we often need to
reorganize it just to prevent a war. [We have made it **easy** for
politicians and generals to start wars because the **unchecked
institutional default** is to lavish improbable sums of money
on]{.underline} the military and defense contractors and to stock their
bases with missiles and jets just waiting to be used. (Not that the jets
always work.) Even when we don't have a hot conflict going, [our war
machine]{.underline} is the Chekov's gun of American political theater.

[[This]{.mark} institutional context [makes]{.mark} so much anti-war
[rhetoric]{.mark}]{.underline}, my own included, **[[fine words scolding
the storm]{.underline}]{.mark}**. [This rhetoric is **too [easily
appropriated]{.mark}** [by warmongers]{.mark} and deployed [as a
**partisan cudgel**]{.mark} to criticize the party in
power]{.underline}, [only to see the ascendant party make use of the
same military apparatus]{.underline} when it has taken control. This was
(and remains) a regular criticism of the Obama Administration in some
quarters. If you'll recall, Obama had campaigned as a credible critic of
the disastrous invasion of Iraq. Along with a majority of the Democratic
Senate caucus, Hilary Clinton had voted in favor of the Authorization of
Military Force Resolution (2002), while Obama, still a state senator at
the time, had not. I wouldn't exactly call Obama's rhetoric dovish, but
it inspired some (imho unwarranted) optimism among Democratic voters as
well as some vitriolic criticism from the Right. Depending on how much
hope one invested in Obama's foreign policy, the succeeding eight years
were possibly disappointing, with the Obama Administration sustaining an
open-ended military presence in Afghanistan and asserting the right to
engage in drone strikes, bombings, and targeted assassinations whenever
they felt national security required it. If one believes the Glenn
Greenwalds of the world, Obama's foreign policy and commitment to the
national security state was a seamless extension of Bush II's and most
Democratic media types were all too willing to forget about US
militarism once their own guy was in the White House. Greenwald now also
makes the same criticism of the Biden Administration and the current
Democratic party. One needn't agree entirely with Greenwald's
assessment---you may justifiably prefer Obama's foreign policy to Bush's
and Biden's to Trump's on the particulars---to, nevertheless, recognize
that, however Obama and Biden deploy the American war machine, both are
unflinching about maintaining America's capacity to unleash massive
amounts of violence on the rest of the world.

Neither Obama nor Biden are unusual in this regard. Senator Bernie
Sanders has long been sneeringly dismissed in national media as a loony
leftist, despite his persistent national popularity, because he has been
a stalwart critic of defense spending. He has been politically isolated
and marginalized for his longstanding commitment to reducing America's
capacity to make war. But maintaining such a capacity, at extraordinary
expense in lives, liberties, and lucre, has been a near constant in
American political life since World War II. Recent military actions as
part of the "War on Terror" have cost the US \$9 trillion and killed
more than 900,000 people in the last two decades alone. But the National
Security State remains the ideological and institutional container
within which partisan disagreements play out and that has been so, at
least within the narrow band of respectable national politics, for the
past 80 years without serious interruption. People may disagree with how
to deploy the American war machine. Some even believe---earnestly and
resolutely---that America should rarely deploy it and is or has been
badly misguided in deploying it in the past. The arguments in polite
political society, however, remain focused on the when of
deployment---"now? no!"---and rarely pose the more fundamental question:
should the United States maintain a war machine at all? That's a shame.
Just as the most effective deterrent to driving a Lamborghini is to not
have a Lamborghini in your garage, the most effective deterrent to US
militarism would be for the United States not to have a war machine.

And what a war machine it is! The amount of money the US government has
invested in its military is truly mind-boggling. It is beyond counting.
No really. Pentagon spending has never been audited. Current Department
of Defense annual discretionary expenditures are \$770 billion, but a
fuller tabulation, including things like the Homeland Security, Veterans
Affairs, and the Department of Energy's contributions to the nuclear
arsenal, could put the total annual tab at nearly \$1.2 trillion
conservatively estimated. It is true, as hawks are fond to note, that
current spending on defense is lower than it has been for most of the
past century when taken as a percentage of GDP. And it's also true that
the US spends a smaller portion of its GDP on its military than do Saudi
Arabia, Israel, and Russia. Yes, that's all true... and an unpersuasive
rationale for current spending levels! The US has the largest economy in
the world. The US economy is 14 times larger than Russia's and almost 50
times the size of Israel's. You don't fight wars as a percentage of GDP.
It's the absolute size of your military that matters and the US war
machine towers over everyone else. When we move past the percentage of
GDP smudge, you'll notice that Russia spends only about \$60 billion
annually on its military, a mere 5% (!!!) of the \$1.2 trillion total US
tab I mentioned above. And even that understates the relative difference
in size since decades of capital investments in military infrastructure
and equipment compose the unrivaled total assets of the US military.
Sure, the military is a huge sinkhole of waste and fraud, but it also
owns a bunch of stuff! The (unaudited) Department of Defense's own
(probably low-balled) accounting suggests it holds \$3.2 trillion in
total assets, a sum that is only about 50 times as large as Russia's
annual military expenditures.

Given this context, my own sense is that [[critiques]{.mark} of US
military actions [are too concerned with]{.mark} the **[ideological
rationales]{.mark}** for particular actions]{.underline}, [[and
not]{.mark} concerned [enough with **institutional design** and
**material interests**]{.mark} that mobilize them]{.underline}[. [I
**doubt**]{.underline}]{.mark} [**very much** that [we are likely
to]{.mark} **radically [alter the]{.mark} guiding [ideology]{.mark}**
[of]{.mark} America's political
[leadership]{.mark}]{.underline}---there's a longer post on this yet to
come but even Trumpkins are the weird, spoiled fruit of
liberalism---[but [even if we did]{.mark}]{.underline},
[**[institutional inertia]{.mark} alone** [could]{.mark} still
[overdetermine a]{.mark} **casually [fatal
militarism]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark} [[Transaction costs
are]{.mark} an **important deterrent** to action]{.underline}---[perhaps
[the **most important** deterrent]{.mark} from an institutional
perspective]{.underline}---and 80 years of defense spending has helped
to radically reduce the transaction costs of military action. Indeed, in
the case of the invasion of Iraq, some portion of our political
leadership was captured by a sunk cost fallacy: We'd already spent
trillions on the war machine, so why not put it to some use and, in
Jonah Goldberg's shameful and abhorrent quote of Michael Ledeen, "pick
up some small crappy little country and throw it against the wall, just
to show the world we mean business." Because, why not? What's the worst
that could happen? The institutional gravity of a \$3.2 trillion dollar
war machine is such that if you keep one in your garage, the particular
reasons to use it will find you.

[Knowing that Iraq and the global War on Terror were very bad wars
**only gets us so far** since it seems **inevitable** that]{.underline},
[[at some point]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[politicians and voters]{.mark}
both [will be enthusiastic about **other bad
wars**]{.mark}]{.underline}. And [[**how will we stop
those**?]{.underline}]{.mark} As I have already stipulated, [the
**fine-tuned adjudication** of the rights and wrongs of future military
actions requires a level of expertise that is **currently unavailable**
to most American citizens]{.underline}. I agree with folks such as
Daniel Bessner and Stephen Wertheim that a huge part of the problem is
that average American citizens are excluded from influencing American
foreign policy and that to turn away from endless war we need to
effectively "democratize" that debate. We can lament the fact that the
average American is both ignorant about world affairs and, at once,
profoundly alienated from participating in [**[debate]{.mark}s** about
foreign policy]{.underline}. But it remains the reality, the good work
of the Quincy Institute notwithstanding. In the meantime, that
adjudication, rarely fine-tuned in national media discourse, [[is run
through with **bad faith actors**]{.mark} [who]{.mark} are interested in
advancing their partisan interests]{.underline}, [[lack a **sincere
desire** to reduce]{.mark} US [military action]{.mark}
abroad]{.underline}, [[and may convert]{.mark} **[political
capital]{.mark} accrued [through such criticisms]{.mark}** into
**advancing [the interests]{.mark}** [of that military]{.mark} apparatus
in the **near future**]{.underline}. Similarly, my gloss is that the
removal of voters and citizens from foreign policy decision making is
part and parcel of the redesign of the American state during World War
II. In this sense, allowing citizens to shape foreign policy would
require not just a change in attitudes by both elites and average
citizens, but, in a deeper sense, it would require a broader
reorganization of the American state, its boundaries and objectives, and
the interests and constituencies it serves. There's some reason to be
optimistic that such a reorganization would be broadly popular with
American voters. But, as of yet, there is simply little institutional
support for dramatic military spending cuts from within either major
political party. The centrist and wonkish wings of the Democratic party
don't yet seem to recognize anti-militarism as a potential component of
a "popularist" agenda, despite the popularity of cutting defense
spending. But absent an effort to meaningfully reduce that spending,
arguments about military action---which ones to support and which ones
to denounce---are destined to be chafe in America's own spiraling
Forever Culture War, based less on a serious reflection on the
catastrophic touch of American power and more on a gestural affinity for
your team's current imperial enthusiasms.

[[Effective anti-imperialism will **radically reduce** the size of
the]{.mark} American [military or it will do
**nothing**]{.mark}]{.underline}. And [[anyone who fails to endorse such
an approach is **unserious** about]{.mark} how to temper the [military
violence]{.mark} American unleashes on the world]{.underline}. Those
statements lays out [the monumental nature of the task]{.underline} in
no uncertain terms, and I hope their clarity offers you something of
value. At the very least, it [gives you a ready-at-hand instrument to
distinguish]{.underline} the **[opportunistic criticisms]{.underline}**
of the Hawleys, Carlsons, and Vances, on the one hand, [from the
**difficult political commitments** that]{.underline}, on the other
hand, [would effectively reduce militarism in the longer
term]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} is a [distinction]{.underline}
that seems increasingly lost on Glenn Greenwald, but it [remains
**critical** to articulating an effective anti-imperial
politics]{.underline}.

### Presumption---V2---1NC

#### Presumption:

#### Vote neg, they haven't met their burden of proof. \<[The 1AC already performed their method; their ev is about groups doing good stuff in the status quo; insert other examples from their speeches/cross-x/evidence as appropriate]{.mark}\>

#### This is [offense]{.underline}. Symbolically affirming their method despite a lack of material outcomes [weakens]{.underline} radical praxis.

**James, 97**---Ebenezer Fitch Professor of Humanities at Williams
College (Joy, "Radicalism and Black Intellectual Life," *Transcending

Conclusion, 185-187, dml)

Today, identity [[claims to radicalism are made **irrespective**

**[sober [capacity]{.mark}]{.underline}**\" [[to analyze the
