## Generic/Topic Ks Toolbox

### Action Good---2AC

#### Acting to prevent extinction is [good]{.underline}---reject Ks of instrumental action.

**Levine, 21**---David and Kathleen Ryan Professor of the Humanities at
Cornell University (Caroline, "The Long Lure of Anti-Instrumentality:
Politics, Aesthetics, and Sustainability," Modern Fiction Studies,
Volume 67, Number 2, Summer 2021, dml)

This [[critique]{.underline}]{.mark} has been crucial and persuasive.
But the argument against instrumentality in literary studies
[has]{.underline} gone a step further than Weber's Zweckrationalität.
That is, the most influential theorists in the field have [[warned
against **all instrumentality**]{.underline}]{.mark}---[not just
Western-style instrumental rationality]{.underline}, [but **all
means-ends thinking**]{.underline}. [All plans and programs are
dangerous]{.underline}, they say. [Even the most utopian visions of a
revolutionary future]{.underline}, for example, [only **re-entrench**
existing dominations]{.underline}. As Michel Foucault puts it: "to
imagine another system is to extend our participation in the current
system" (230). We are so stuck inside our own moment, the argument goes,
that we cannot imagine any genuine alternative to it. Adorno, though
deeply unlike Foucault in many ways, shares this position. He writes,
"One may not cast a picture of utopia in a positive manner" (qtd. in
Bloch 10). [What is productive instead]{.underline}, Adorno says, [is to
draw attention to what's **missing**]{.underline}: "the determined
negation of that which merely is . . . always points at the same time to
what should be" (12). [According to [this logic]{.mark}]{.underline},
[we do our best political work when we [dwell in]{.mark} **restless
[negativity]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[using **imperfections** to
point]{.mark} beyond themselves [to **something undefined to
come**]{.mark}]{.underline}. This argument has lasted into our own time,
with numerous critics insisting on the political importance of refusing
to sketch out or plan for the future. "Revolution will come in a form we
cannot yet imagine" (11), Jack Halberstam writes: "We cannot say what
new structures will replace the ones we live with yet, because once we
have torn shit down, we will inevitably see more and see differently and
feel a new sense of wanting and being and becoming" (6).

For these thinkers, the resistance to instrumentality is political. But,
of course, anti-instrumentality also underpins major theories of
aesthetics. Since Kant, a range of theorists have defined the aesthetic
precisely by its disturbance of means-ends thinking. Art cannot be
reduced to exchange and profit or to the communication of moral \[End
Page 226\] values or information. Art's strangeness and difficulty halt
the reflexive rush to calculability, efficiency, and utility. Strangely,
then, while anti-instrumentality grounds a range of political arguments,
from feminism to postcolonial theory, it also provides the basis for
antipolitical arguments in the aesthetic disciplines. According to
Langdon Hammer, for example, literary critics should stay away from
fantasies of having any kind of political impact because art should not
serve ends other than itself. Jonathan Kramnick argues that the
discipline of literary studies rests on "the norm of the open question";
it is the job of the critic "to state and explore problems rather than
provide solutions to them" (27). This disciplinary obligation emerges
out of our focus on the aesthetic, "if only because considering artworks
as significant in their own right often means spelling out the
open-ended or unresolved." And so, at the same time that we find
anti-instrumentality at the heart of the work of radical Black studies
thinker Moten, we also find it central to the work of Derek Attridge,
perhaps the field's most influential champion of aesthetic autonomy.

[Anti-instrumentality also underlies]{.underline} specifically ethical
arguments in literary studies---calls to humility in the face of a
complex world no one should try to master. Environmental humanists have
been especially committed to replacing teleological accounts of heroic
human action---which is also typically conquest and exploitation---with
[an embrace of]{.underline} what Anna Tsing calls "[open-ended
assemblages of **entangled ways of life**]{.underline}" (viii). Although
Haraway argues that instrumentality is inevitable because species must
use each other for survival, she, like Tsing, invites readers to learn
"to be truly present, not as a vanishing pivot between awful or edenic
pasts and apocalyptic or salvific futures, but as moral critters
entwined in myriad unfinished configurations of places, times, matters,
meanings" (1). Both embrace the pause, the need to stop to notice and
reflect on the now, interrupting the usual rush to profit and progress.
Both insist, too, on open-endedness, the refusal to give a definite
shape to future worlds. In this respect, the environmental humanities
reinforces the long tradition of opening ourselves to the unknown to
come.

In short, from Marxist critique to decolonial resistance and from
aesthetic autonomy to deep ecology, the common logic of
anti-instrumentality subtends otherwise conflicting schools of thought.
But what is it exactly that they share? [There is one common
urge]{.underline}: **[negation]{.underline}**, [the desire to **crack
the world open** to alternatives to the status quo]{.underline}, to
beckon to unmapped and unmappable possibility. And so, if you look at
almost any essay or book in literary or cultural studies, [its
conclusion will be a **deliberate open-endedness**]{.underline}, a
soaring \[End Page 227\] refusal to spell out specific paths forward.
Catherine Gallagher and Greenblatt, for example, write: "we sincerely
hope that you will not be able to say what it all adds up to; if you
could, we would have failed" (19). Alexander [**Weheliye** ends with
artists who]{.underline} "**[explode]{.underline}** both [the
individuated]{.underline} person and couple as [subject]{.underline} . .
. thus underscoring the messiness of these categories" (197). Fredric
Jameson marks "the now open space for something else" (296). Sara
Ahmed's The Cultural Politics of Emotion closes this way: "Justice
involves feelings . . . . Where we go, with these feelings, remains an
open question" (202). Christina [**Sharpe** urges readers]{.underline}
"[to stay in this **wake**]{.underline} time that would inhabit a
blackened consciousness that would rupture the structural silences
produced and facilitated by, and that produce and facilitate, Black
social and physical death" (22). And Robert **[McRuer]{.underline}**
[ends]{.underline} his book on queerness and disability [with
the]{.underline} "[promise that we will always **comprehend disability
otherwise** and]{.underline} that we will, collectively, [somehow access
other worlds and other futures]{.underline}" (208). Critics of all
stripes issue the same call: refuse the status quo in favor of an
unknowable world to come.

The Politics of Anti-Instrumentality

It is strange that anti-instrumentality can work as a distinctively
apolitical aesthetic value as well as a deliberately, explicitly
political one, and it is this particular convergence that draws my
attention in this essay. What is it, exactly, that allows
anti-instrumentality to cross back and forth so easily between artistic
autonomy and political purpose? The answer, I think, is this: when the
art work is understood as an end in itself, when it is cast as an object
that resists dominant institutions and refuses to serve powerful agents,
then in this precise capacity, art is doing political work. That is, as
long as art is free from ends, it can yield pleasures and thoughts and
possibilities that are not subject to dominant pressures and purposes.
And emancipation from dominant ends is nothing if not a political value.

In practice, this means that aesthetic autonomy can be readily
politicized. One famous story from the middle of the twentieth century
shows this paradox in action. In the 1940s and 1950s, the Soviets were
charging that the US was a shallow and materialist nation, obsessed only
with wealth and military might. In response, the US State Department
sought to showcase the many ways that the US allowed artists to thrive
in an atmosphere of unparalleled freedom. But it was hard to prove that
the democratic West fostered freedom and openness when the US government
was ready to shut down artists \[End Page 228\] with the wrong political
sympathies. After all, the House Committee on Un-American Activities was
publicly targeting writers like Langston Hughes, Dashiell Hammett, and
Bertolt Brecht at the very same moment, demanding that they testify to
their own noncommunist political loyalties and inform on others.

An especially embarrassing episode happened in 1947. The State
Department had sponsored a traveling exhibition called Advancing
American Art, which featured a range of innovative modern American
painters, including Georgia O'Keefe and Jacob Lawrence. At first it was
a huge success. Reports suggested that these bold and unexpected
paintings had actually begun to persuade audiences in Eastern Europe and
Latin America that the US was capable of experimental, original art. The
State Department planned to take the show to Guatemala, Iran, and
Cambodia. But when the US Congress and the press found out about the
exhibit, there was a huge public stink. The paintings were called
subversive and alien. One congressman from Mississippi said, "No
American drew these crazy pictures!" (qtd. in Littleton and Sykes 41).
Public pressure forced the State Department to cancel the show.

It was in this context that CIA strategists launched a covert arts
program. The goal was to prove that the West was home to exciting,
highly original art that did not cater to specific political programs.
Against the Soviets' insistence that art serve the regime, the CIA would
showcase aesthetic autonomy and anti-instrumentality. Jackson Pollock,
as Frances Stonor Saunders explains, seemed like a perfect vehicle to
prove the Soviets wrong. Defiant and anticonventional, Pollock
exemplified an avant-garde American creativity that refused mass culture
and Hollywood glitz. He had been widely mocked and condemned by the
press, but this was all to the good, from the CIA's point of view. While
the Russians forced their artists to work in the style of Socialist
Realism, the CIA secretly supported mass media coverage of "Jack the
Dripper" to prove that the US let even the strangest and most unpopular
artists flourish.

The CIA's role in cultural life in the middle of the twentieth century
is now widely known, but at the time they largely kept their operations
secret through fake foundations and front organizations. The CIA
covertly funneled resources to the literary magazine Encounter and to
the Chekhov Publishing House, which printed the works of Vladimir
Nabokov and other Russian emigres. It supported the Nigerian little
magazine, Black Orpheus, which published some of the most influential
négritude writers, including Aimé Césaire and Léopold Senghor.1 Deborah
Cohn explains that El Mundo Nuevo, the \[End Page 229\] Paris quarterly
of Latin American writing, was exposed as a CIA venture early on, though
it earns a place in literary history for publishing the first chapters
of Gabriel García Márquez's Cien años de soledad in 1966, as well as
works by Octavio Paz and Mario Vargas Llosa. The Cold War Program for
Cultural Freedom was a major source of support not only for artists and
writers but also for the humanities in US universities. The CIA provided
funding for academic programs in Asian and Latin American studies,
foreign languages, and American literature. It was Cold Warrior Richard
Nixon who poured money into the National Endowment for the Arts (NEA)
and the National Endowment for the Humanities (NEH). Cuts to the NEA and
NEH began in 1990, shortly after the Berlin Wall fell. If we look back
nostalgically to a time when there was ample support for the arts and
humanities, we should not overlook the importance of Cold War ends to
this flourishing.2

What matters here is that [the CIA figured out how to
**instrumentalize**]{.underline}---we might [even]{.underline} say,
**[weaponize]{.underline}**---the [anti-instrumentality]{.underline} of
the aesthetic. [They saw that difficult]{.underline}, [experimental art
could stand for freedom from political ends]{.underline}, [and then they
put that freedom to political ends]{.underline}. And [this was **not a
misuse** or **misunderstanding**]{.underline} of artistic
anti-instrumentality. As Peter Kalliney argues, African writers in the
period also embraced aesthetic autonomy for both political and
antipolitical ends, including "emancipation from colonialism;
independence from the postcolonial nation-state; avoidance of politics
in order to foster collaboration among multiple constituencies; freedom
from politics altogether as a professional disposition; and ideological
neutrality in the Cold War" (337).

Again, we might well ask what position could possibly underpin this
complex and conflicting list, uniting thinkers as radically different as
Nixon and Halberstam, Paz and Césaire. But, again, I think there is an
answer: [it is a **specifically indiscriminate** version of political
negation]{.underline}. That is, [anti-instrumentality
can]{.underline}---and does---[set itself against **any
end**]{.underline}. [It can challenge]{.underline},
[unsettle]{.underline}, [and negate]{.underline} any constraint, any
rule, and **[any plan]{.underline}**. The worldview underpinning all of
these many versions of anti-instrumentality is a dream of freedom from
all doctrines, dogmas, and programs.

This matters today because [it is precisely this account of
anti-instrumentality that has come to **pervade**]{.underline} literary
studies. Susan Stanford Friedman refers to it as the "gadfly position"
of the humanities (3). [As dominant institutions lumber
along]{.underline}, [imposing their oppressive norms]{.underline}, [it
is the job of artists and humanists to **annoy** and
**negate**]{.underline}, [to act as **constant irritants**]{.underline}.
But [this means that its refusals of ends can]{.underline} \[End Page
230\] [go in **any direction**]{.underline}---the goal is to resist,
unsettle, or puncture any program at all.

What seems crucial to stress, then, is that
[[anti-instrumentality]{.mark} **does not necessarily align** with the
Left]{.underline}. [It [can motivate both **Cold War spies** and those
keen to **avoid politics altogether**]{.mark}]{.underline}. And [what
is]{.underline} perhaps [most disturbing right now is the **eerie
similarity** between the language of anti-instrumentality]{.underline}
in literary studies [and the language of the political
**Right**]{.underline}. Leaders like Donald [**Trump** and]{.underline}
Jair [**Bolsonaro** use anti-establishment rhetoric in the
interest]{.underline}, they claim, [of freeing people from oppressive
norms and state regulations]{.underline}. In an unsettling echo of
writers and critics on the Left, these right-wing authoritarian leaders
embrace a disruption of rules and a shattering of institutions in order
to resist what they cast as the oppressive norms of the status quo.

Sustainability, or Collective Continuance

I argue here that it is particularly urgent for the Left to reconsider
our longstanding attachment to anti-instrumentality at this moment of
rapid and destabilizing climate change. [[Ecocritics]{.mark} have argued
especially strenuously against **all** instrumentalizing
programs]{.underline}, [[insist]{.mark}ing that [**any** teleological
thinking is **in cahoots** with capitalism and
colonialism]{.mark}]{.underline}, and only re-entrenches the destructive
ideologies that caused the climate crisis in the first place. [Refusing
to coerce others into exploitative use or service]{.underline}, [they
take a principled stand in favor of **passivity** and **deliberate
political withdrawal**]{.underline}. Anne-Lise François, for example,
turns toward "recessive action" (xvii)---the "nonappropriative" (xxiv),
minor gesture that rejects any illusion of human mastery or conquest.
And Anahid Nersessian argues, "For better or worse, works of art resist
practical application or problem-solving" (2), which means that the task
of the aesthetic disciplines is to face the "traumatic knowledge that
climate catastrophe is certain and unfathomable."

[There is something **attractively pure** about refusing all complicity
with capitalism and colonialism]{.underline}. [But [there is]{.mark}
also a [strange logic to the arg]{.mark}ument [that]{.mark} any attempt
to slow or stop global warming would be **so much in keeping [with
instrumental reason]{.mark}** []{.mark}that [we have **no choice but
to**]{.mark} **surrender** to the climate catastrophe]{.underline} to
come. [Can it be true that our only ethical option is to **[accede
to]{.mark} our own [extinction]{.mark}**]{.underline}---[[not to
mention]{.mark} the extinction of **many human** and **nonhuman others**
in the process?]{.underline} Or, to put this another way,
[**acquiescing** to forces of
**[massive]{.mark}**]{.underline}[,]{.mark} [**global
[destruction]{.mark}** [that will intensify the suffering
of]{.mark}]{.underline} \[End Page 231\] [[the world's poorest]{.mark}
is **not a recognizably leftist position**]{.underline}, if by the Left
we mean a political commitment to collective, egalitarian flourishing.

Thus, it is my central argument here that our own historical moment
demands a reconsideration of aesthetic and political
anti-instrumentality on the Left. As neoliberal economics undoes hopes
of secure work and as fossil fuels radically disrupt longstanding
ecosystems, [the most urgent threat facing people around the world is
not **oppressive stasis** but **radical
instability**]{.underline}---[intensifying poverty and food
insecurity]{.underline}, [flooding]{.underline}, [forest
fires]{.underline}, [violent conflicts over water]{.underline}, [and the
rapid extinction of species]{.underline}. In literary studies, [we have
been so focused on refusing to serve ends that we have not often
developed resources for working for reliable **food**]{.underline}, for
example, [or safe and stable **shelter**]{.underline}. [With the rapid
onrush of **climate catastrophes**]{.underline}, [these basic conditions
are **increasingly under threat**]{.underline} for the world's poor.
[[Open-endedness is **not**]{.mark} primarily a source of [pleasure and
excitement for those who are anxious about **finding their next meal**
or a **safe place to sleep**]{.mark}]{.underline}. I claim that justice
demands a focus on the mundane work of sustaining living bodies over
time, which requires adequate nutrition, breathable air, clean water,
and stable shelter. [Anti-instrumentality turns our attention **away**
from solving these problems to focus instead on
**withdrawal**]{.underline}, **[inaction]{.underline}**, [and
**mourning**]{.underline}. The usual logic of literary studies is, in
short, a logic of antisustainability.

So, what is the alternative? I propose an affirmative instrumentality in
literary studies. While humanists have been fully justified in resisting
pressures to put bodies and ideas to work in the service of violent and
inequitable systems, I do not believe that it follows that we should
avoid all ends. [Not only has this insistence lent itself to an
**indiscriminate [politics of undoing]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [but it
has also [left]{.mark} the tasks of designing and [building social
life]{.mark} [to]{.mark}]{.underline} the social and natural sciences,
[**[government]{.underline}**, [and **business**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
As long as literary studies imagines its role as [refusing and negating
all practical plans and projects]{.underline}, it [will continue to
**yield** the power to shape the world to other
disciplines]{.underline}. What if, instead, we saw it as the central
political task of literary studies to figure out how to sustain
collective life over time with some degree of justice and mutual care?

To be sure, a number of scholars, including Leerom Medovoi and Timothy
Morton, have roundly critiqued the term sustainability. As often
embraced by businesses and environmental activists, sustainability
implies the continuation of life as we know it, which for many in the
business world includes expectations of ongoing economic growth,
competition, and accumulation. It is certainly true that if we work to
sustain current systems---including global markets and \[End Page 232\]
extractive industries---we become complicit with the most rapacious
forces on earth. And yet, our dominant systems are in fact dramatically
unsustainable. The current pace of extraction and emission is making the
planet uninhabitable for humans and vast numbers of other species.

From this perspective, sustainability is in fact a neutral term. It
refers to the capacity to keep any state of affairs going over time. It
can include both just and unjust conditions. What climate change has
made suddenly clear is that sustaining must be a goal on the Left as
well as the Right. We are now faced with a struggle to keep collective
life going at all. A more appropriate focus than rupture and
open-endedness, then, would be the goal of a genuine
sustainability---the vast and urgent project of sustaining collective
life over generations.

A revaluing of sustainability will mean a turn to the ordinary work of
maintenance, a celebration of the work that keeps life going every day.
I draw inspiration here from feminist thinkers such as Mierle Laderman
Ukeles, Luce Giard, bell hooks, and Susan Fraiman, who have argued for
decades that the artistic emphasis on negations and revolutionary
ruptures have meant too little respect for the tasks and values that are
crucial to keeping bodies alive, including maintenance and caretaking.
And it is true that Leftists and artists have often ignored or devalued
certain necessary labor, such as the reproduction and maintenance of
daily survival, which is so often women's work---from literal
reproduction to the ongoing care of bodies through the preparation of
food, care for the sick, and the daily upkeep of environments. This kind
of labor is not necessarily creative, heroic, or radically emancipatory.
It is practical and repetitive. It has to happen in many of the same
ways every day. But no human sociality has ever taken shape without it.

The thinker who best captures the task before us is indigenous
philosopher Kyle Powys Whyte, who coins the term "collective
continuance" (345). Whyte argues against the common stereotype that
indigenous societies are static. All collectives, he points out, have to
develop strategies for adapting to new conditions. Food systems, for
example, are integral to the survival of both bodies and communities,
but they are also always subject to external forces, such as storms and
floods. "**[[Collective continuance]{.underline}]{.mark}**," he argues,
"is a society's overall adaptive capacity to maintain its members'
cultural integrity, health, economic vitality, and political order into
the future and avoid having its members experience preventable harms"
(353). Some adaptation, and some transformation, will always be
necessary. This approach does not set up an opposition between
conservation and innovation, \[End Page 233\] or between traditional and
modern societies. Rather, it [requires us to think]{.underline} justly
[about]{.underline} what to maintain in the face of inevitable change.
Integral to this approach is [[the **prevention of foreseeable future
harm**]{.underline}]{.mark}, a basic political responsibility [to ensure
the conditions for **intergenerational flourishing**]{.underline}.

Whyte's model of [collective continuance]{.underline} is especially
valuable because it [points to a kind of means-ends thinking that **does
not immediately fall back** into the trap of instrumental
rationality]{.underline}. [Collective continuance is an **end** that is
also an **ongoing means**]{.underline}. That is, [collective continuance
is the establishment of]{.underline} political, cultural, environmental,
and economic [conditions that allow collective life-worlds to **flourish
over time**]{.underline}: [it [is neither]{.mark} a **[top-down]{.mark}
conclusion** [nor a **superficial fix** but]{.mark} a set of **enabling
conditions**]{.underline}---an infrastructure for living. [To reject all
ends as constraining and oppressive is to **miss** the ways that some
fundamental conditions]{.underline}---clean water, fertile soil, and
breathable air---[are [the **preconditions** for **all other
activity**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Or, to put this another way, collective
continuance is a capacitating end, a crucial means of affording a range
of other ends.

Amartya Sen's influential capabilities approach to justice captures the
importance of understanding ends as means. Sen turns away from abstract
rights to the real constraints and possibilities people face when they
try to lead lives they value. In many places, women must depend on male
breadwinners for survival, for example, and transgender people cannot
move safely through the streets. These are injustices in Sen's terms
because they make it impossible for some groups to shape their lives
according to their values, while others can do as they wish. He is clear
that providing resources alone is not enough for justice: it does no
good to be given a food like pork if it violates one's religion to eat
it, or to be offered a job if one cannot travel to work. Although Sen
focuses most of his attention on fairly complex and contested values, he
assumes a broad consensus around "basic capabilities" (288): everyone
needs minimal standards of health, food, education, and shelter as a
precondition for achieving other ends. These basic capabilities are not
particularly interesting to many theorists of justice, but climate
change is threatening all of them right now---including air to breathe
and water to drink. Or, to put this another way, **[any acceptance of
the onrushing consequences of global warming is intolerably
unjust]{.underline}** in Sen's terms.

And so, I make the affirmative case here for a set of ends that are also
a means, a set of just and sustainable conditions that are themselves a
means to allow a rich variety of lives to continue into the future.
[[Anti-instrumentality]{.mark} is not the best approach to this
task]{.underline}, \[End Page 234\] especially [since]{.underline}, as
we have seen, [it [can]{.mark} also **[serve]{.mark} the interests of
[the Right]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [In the name of **negating oppressive
norms** and **institutions**]{.underline}, for example, as Nadja
Popovich and others have shown, the **[Trump]{.underline}**
administration [rolled back over]{.underline} 95 [environmental
regulations]{.underline}, including prohibitions on fracking on Native
lands, drilling in wildlife preserves, and dumping toxins in waterways.
From this perspective, we can see how an [excitement about resisting the
state can actually **hasten** the worst effects of climate
change]{.underline}.

This matters because the most important difference between Left and
Right lies not in our relationship to norms and institutions, but in the
way we understand enabling conditions---the infrastructures of
collective life. While the Right often justifies some amount of
starvation and homelessness as a necessary spur to economic
productivity, which they see as beneficial for everyone, the entire span
of the Left, from radicals to progressives, argues that it is unjust to
deny the most basic necessities of survival to anyone. Thus, across the
spectrum of the Left, radicals and progressives alike might begin not
from a resolute anti-instrumentality, but from a shared basic version of
justice that is both an end and a means: the urgent work of guaranteeing
basic capabilities for all. With this work as our horizon, we do not
have to choose between brutal exploitation and principled withdrawal, or
between acquiescence to the status quo and change so radical that it is
literally unimaginable. We can start the hard work of trying to build a
lasting infrastructure for multiple collective life-worlds to flourish
over time.

Practical Politics

The shift to an affirmative instrumentality in literary studies will not
be easy. It will mean a turn away from a politics of relentless negation
toward practical action, and turn away from an aesthetics of
experimentation, opacity, and difficulty toward a whole new canon of art
objects.

Pragmatism has often been a term of opprobrium in literary studies,
charged with confining us ever further within the brutal and corrupt
systems of the present. Anything short of pulling this whole rotten
society up by the roots is the same as quietism and complacency,
wishy-washy liberalism, or worse, sinister neoliberalism. Critics draw
on a long history of revolutionary thinkers who have opposed pragmatic
measures like reform in favor of the shattering work of revolution. At
least as far back as Marx in 1850, radicals have worried that social
welfare programs like healthcare and social security \[End Page 235\]
provide just enough in the way of comfort and security to prevent
workers from rising up as an angry mass, but without changing
fundamental economic structures.3 Accelerationists like Slavoj Žižek go
so far as to argue that we should hasten the worsening of conditions
because desperation is the necessary precondition for revolutionary
change. From this perspective, we should welcome mass starvation and
climate catastrophe because only these will spur the building of a just
society. The more moderate---and more pervasive---version of this logic,
which we can see in such thinkers different as Halberstam and Giorgio
Agamben, is that we should not work for small changes or half-measures
because these will prolong our acceptance of a fundamentally violent and
exploitative system.

But what if this refusal of pragmatic action is wrong? What if
institutional changes, [**[techno-fixes]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} ]{.mark}[**piecemeal [reforms]{.mark}** **[do
not]{.mark} necessarily [get in the way]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
large-scale structural change and can]{.underline}, in fact, **[serve
[revolutionary ends]{.mark}]{.underline}**? One strand of revolutionary
thinkers has understood organizing for achievable ends as important,
even necessary, steps in a larger revolutionary struggle. For Rosa
Luxemburg, famously, [the opposition between revolution and reform was a
**false dilemma**]{.underline}: "The struggle for reforms is its means;
the social revolution, its goal" (129). Women's suffrage was, for
Luxemburg, a crucial example. Raymond Williams, too, for many decades
understood reformist tactics as more effective at mobilizing
working-class people to act than the demand for an immediate smashing of
capitalism. In our own time, Chantal Mouffe advocates a "radical
reformism" (46) as a crucial tool for building a powerful populism of
the left. And against passionate arguments from fellow radicals, Angela
Davis has defended the legal reforms of Civil Rights and the election of
Barack Obama as important pieces of the Black radical struggle, not an
obstacle to it. Similarly, Sherry Wolf, a socialist organizer for LGBTQ
rights, has argued for the importance of gay marriage not as a
concession to an assimilationist pressure but as part of a larger fight
for Civil Rights for all. And here, surprisingly, is Žižek himself,
arguing that the [insistence on revolution]{.underline} in wealthy
countries actually [turns into]{.underline} the opposite---[a kind of
**perfectionism** that gets **stuck** because it does not have
**sufficient mobilizing power**]{.underline}. In this scenario,
revolution itself gets in the way of revolution:

In principle, the choice facing leftist politics is between
social-democratic reformism and radical revolution, but the latter
choice, although abstractly correct and true, is self-defeating and gets
stuck in Beautiful Soul immobility: in the developed Western societies,
calls for a radical revolution have no mobilising power. Only [[a
modest]{.underline}]{.mark} \[End Page 236\]
'[**[wrong]{.underline}**']{.mark} [[choice]{.underline}]{.mark} can
create the subjective conditions for an actual communist perspective:
[[whether it **fails** or **succeeds**]{.underline}]{.mark}, it [[sets
in motion]{.mark} a **series** of [further demands]{.mark}]{.underline}
('in order to really have universal healthcare, we also need . . .')
[which will lead to the **right** choice]{.underline}. There is no
shortcut here, [the need for a radical universal change has to emerge by
way of **mediation** with **particular demands**]{.underline}. [[To
begin straightaway with the right choice is]{.underline}]{.mark}
therefore [[**even worse** than making a wrong
choice]{.underline}]{.mark}, as it amounts to saying 'I am right and the
misery of the world which got it wrong just confirms how right I am.'

\(lxxvii\)

Despite their many differences, these thinkers agree that [**vast
masses** of people are most inclined to mobilize around **immediate
causes** of suffering and **concrete demands**]{.underline}. And
[because revolutions take shape through the collective energy and
organization of **large numbers**]{.underline}, [the practical struggle
to transform existing conditions and institutions]{.underline}, such as
the fight for labor protections, voting rights, and marriage equality,
[is **necessary**]{.underline} work. From this perspective, it is a
mistake not to recognize the revolutionary potential in any leftist
campaign that draws and mobilizes large numbers, such as Black Lives
Matter or the LGBTQ Human Rights Campaign.

The crucial question here is a strategic one: how do social, economic,
political, and cultural transformation come about? Against a Marxism
that gives up on politics, which has been paradoxically seductive in
recent years, as Bruce Robbins argues in his 2013 essay "Balibarism!"
[this]{.underline} robust revolutionary tradition [invites us all to
**get our hands dirty** in imperfect politics]{.underline}, to focus on
mobilizing, organizing, and planning, [and to engage in]{.underline} the
unromantic, demanding work of [social transformation through **all
existing channels** for political struggle]{.underline},
[including]{.underline} elections, [battles for **legal
rights**]{.underline}, [and **institutions** like]{.underline} the
university and [the **state**]{.underline}. [Practical politics emerges
here as **crucial** to building skills]{.underline},
[organizations]{.underline}, [and collective power on the
Left]{.underline}, [all **preconditions** for radical structural
change]{.underline}.

[If **no stark decision** has to be made between revolution and
reform]{.underline}, [if movements]{.underline} across the Left [grow
powerful by joining forces in **messy**]{.underline}, **[impure
coalitions]{.underline}**, [and if short-term]{.underline}, [practical
struggles have the potential to serve **long-term radical
ends**]{.underline}, [then the wholesale refusal of pragmatism is
troubling indeed]{.underline}. [The argument]{.underline} dominant
across literary studies---[that we have an obligation to refuse **all**
arduous and imperfect struggles to **change conditions for the better**
in the here and now]{.underline}---[seems like **exactly the wrong way**
to work for justice]{.underline}.

How, then, might literary scholars participate in the struggle for
collective continuance? When it comes to climate change, both leftist
activists and humanities [[scholars]{.mark} have been quick to spot
problems]{.underline} \[End Page 237\] [in **every** [propos]{.mark}al
for environmental action]{.underline}. [We should not hope for
**techno-fixes**]{.underline}, for example---whether wind power,
geoengineering, or wildlife reclamation
projects---[because]{.underline}, as Jordan Kinder argues, [an ongoing
reliance on [technical knowledge]{.mark} **only [re-entrenches]{.mark}**
the assumption that [human subjects]{.mark} can dominate and manipulate
nature [and]{.mark} **deepens** [inequitable]{.mark} social
[relations]{.mark}]{.underline}. Nor does it make sense to seek change
through electoral politics, if Timothy Mitchell is right, because our
government is what he calls a carbon democracy, where the dominant
"politics of economic calculation" (252) has itself developed out of our
dependence on fossil fuels. [We should not fight for **carbon
pricing**]{.underline}, even if it will bring down emissions globally
and quickly, [because]{.underline}, David Bush argues, [it continues the
**logic of capitalism**]{.underline}. Max Ajl makes the case that the
Green New Deal sustains the long history of Euro-American colonialism
and the exploitation of the Global South.

[[Since each movement is]{.mark} **imperfect** or
**[impure]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[many **give up hope
altogether**]{.underline}]{.mark}. We need [a radical [revolution in
consciousness]{.mark}]{.underline}, we say, and by then it [[will be
**too late**]{.underline}.]{.mark} And yet, [even as the critiques of
each separate political project might be fair enough]{.underline},
[**together** they have **noticeably strengthened**]{.underline},
**[sustained]{.underline}**, [and **transformed** each
other]{.underline}, [providing **momentum** for larger and larger scales
of change]{.underline}. In recent years, as Kai Heron and Jodi Dean
argue, a strong new coalition for environmental justice has taken shape
that joins "scientists, indigenous rights activists, and proponents of
social justice," despite differences in epistemic positions and methods.
This coalition has not only successfully shifted mainstream audiences
away from climate denialism, but it has also strengthened the claims of
each group by connecting them to the others. "Allied with science,
environmentalists shed their eco-hippy personae to become
representatives of a fact-based critique of mass consumption"; "the
leadership of indigenous people \[grew\] to national and international
prominence as they forged collective opposition to pipelines and
fracking"; and "Attention to sacrifice zones, slow death, and the
persistent deprivations of environmental racism helped environmentalists
move beyond the elitist image long associated with conservationism."
Increasing numbers of university scientists have found their work
transformed for the better by alliances with indigenous communities, and
racial justice organizations, from the NAACP to Black Lives Matter, have
incorporated the fight against climate change and pollution into their
daily work. Each part of the struggle can draw strength from the others,
and all can work together to advance the work of climate justice. [An
[alternative to pessimistic inaction]{.mark}]{.underline}, then, [[might
be]{.underline}]{.mark} "**[[anything that moves the
ball]{.underline}]{.mark}** \[End Page 238\]
**[[forward]{.underline}]{.mark}**," as Democratic Representative Salud
Carbajal of California puts it (qtd. in Worland).

[Left pessimism]{.underline}, according to Heron and Dean,
[is]{.underline} not only wrong but also **[dangerous]{.underline}**.
[Leftists have managed to **displace** climate denial from fossil fuel
interests]{.underline}---where it began---"[into their own
arguments]{.underline}, [**shielding** themselves from the overwhelming
burden of action]{.underline}." [The]{.underline} "**[burden of
action]{.underline}**" [is **overwhelming**]{.underline}, indeed, [if we
assume that **no work is worth doing** apart from **immediate** and
**total revolution**]{.underline}, demolishing every constraint and
every institution, [or if we must conduct pure and virtuous campaigns
that avoid all impress of ourselves on the world]{.underline}. [But the
burden **lifts**]{.underline}, [if we imagine ourselves as working with
the materials we have to build]{.underline} a larger movement for
**[collective continuance]{.underline}**.

### Anti-Anti-State---2AC

#### We're [anti-anti-state]{.underline}.

**Gordon, 24**---Associate Professor of Political Science and Africana
Studies at University of Connecticut (Jane Anna, "Creolising the
State?," Theoria, Volume 71: Issue 181 (Dec 2024), dml)

In Statelessness and Contemporary Enslavement (2020), I offered a brief
defence of the state in what I preliminarily termed an
**[anti-anti-statism]{.underline}**. I formulated it this way to
[acknowledge that]{.underline}, [given the **active complicity** of
states in the production of **statelessness** and]{.underline}
conditions that foster [contemporary **enslavement**]{.underline}, [it
is **not hard** to recognise the appeal of **anarchistic**
approaches]{.underline} amongst some of the most politically committed.
As was true in the first quarter of the twentieth century, contemporary
[states appear as primarily **predatory** and
**kleptocratic**]{.underline}, [either **unable** or **unwilling** to
counteract]{.underline} the [precariousness]{.underline} created by
current political economic developments and their violently xenophobic
expressions.

Still, I argued, as indispensable as [these insights]{.underline} are,
they [must be mobilised to formulate a **viable
anti-anti-statism**]{.underline}. After all, [conceiving global
responses to developments that have made vulnerability highly lucrative
**must involve governments** and **political
institutions**]{.underline}. What is more, in most versions
[anti-statism rests on a **logical circularity** in which political
institutions that are **not liked** are synonymous with **states** while
responsive]{.underline}, [legitimate ones are called **something
else**]{.underline}. [Rather than letting the exploitative
instantiations of the state or instances of state capture **monopolise**
and **exhaust** the term]{.underline}, [the state must also include the
deeply imperfect **New Deal** government]{.underline} of Franklin D.
Roosevelt [and experiments in **Caribbean socialism** that
produced]{.underline}, amongst other outcomes, the free medical training
offered by [the **Cuban healthcare system**]{.underline}. Surely
'**[states]{.underline}**' [include neo-fascist varieties]{.underline},
[political institutions that expand human freedom]{.underline}, [and
everything in between]{.underline}. If so, [the question is **not**
whether we are **for** or **against** states but about the **kinds** of
political institutions we need and **how** they are
constructed]{.underline}. As Alix Olson and Alex Zamalin write: 'If
democracy is intended to be enacted by the people, then the state itself
is ours to reimagine and those desires are to be collectively acted
upon' (2024: 151).

This observation informs a second pragmatic one. [We **cannot cede**

**established channels**]{.underline}. [It also entails engaging in

### Arctic Policy Advocacy Good---2AC

31.1 Against Neocolonialism

[We have become **increasingly attuned** to the **necessity of asserting
our rights** when dealing with]{.underline} transnational corporations
and [powerful state actors,]{.underline} and such [**actors** have in
turn become **increasingly aware** of the **necessity** of taking our
rights into account]{.underline} (Wilson 2016; Papillon and Rodon 2017).
[**[Foreign capital]{.mark}** and **[expertise]{.mark}** is
**[not]{.mark} in [itself]{.mark} [bad]{.mark}**]{.underline},
[[but]{.underline}]{.mark} it is [**[vital]{.mark}** to [ensure]{.mark}
that [foreign-led]{.mark} and financed economic [activities]{.mark} in
the Arctic **[serve the needs of Arctic peoples]{.mark}** as well in
order to prevent becoming caught up in neocolonial
processe]{.underline}s. From mining companies and fishing interests to
government authorities based in distant seats of power, [[we]{.mark}
**[must]{.mark} seek** to simultaneously be open to the world and
**[avoid]{.mark} [becoming ]{.mark}**a **[passive]{.mark}** cog in the
great machine of global capital and realpolitik.]{.underline}

The globalisation of corporate power is accompanied by a globalisation
of risk and of concern for Indigenous peoples and the future of the
Arctic. [As the world awakens to the dangers of climate
change]{.underline}, increasing attention is being paid to scientific
research into the Arctic, [[research]{.underline}]{.mark} that -- though
**[[often well-meaning]{.underline}]{.mark}** -- is also often
[[embedded in metropolitan]{.mark} [mindsets]{.mark}]{.underline}, which
ultimately [[reinforce]{.underline}]{.mark} existing power
[[structures]{.underline}]{.mark}, priorities, and dependencies. Each

challenges for Arctic peoples while advancing ever-more technocratic
discourses and globalist solutions. People the world over are at last
becoming aware of how important the Arctic is to them, yet this [growing
recognition]{.underline} is [not guaranteed]{.underline} to [result in
greater influence for Arctic peoples]{.underline}; it might just as

the region to suit state or globalist interests -- perhaps at the

focus on climate change may]{.underline} in some cases [be politically
convenient for powerful actors]{.underline} rather than ideally suited

[[We **require** **knowledge**, **research**, and

[outside]{.underline}]{.mark} [the region]{.underline} if we are
[[to]{.mark} undertake]{.underline} sustainable
[development]{.underline} and **[[take advantage]{.underline}]{.mark}**

The peoples of the Arctic are not responsible for having produced global

(Farbotko 2010).

#### Arctic policymaking is key. Collective Arctic action that engages with state actors allows for a ruptural break from neocolonialism and guides the stolen capital of the West back into Arctic control, especially over contingent issues like climate crisis.

31.2 A New Era of Arctic Cooperation

The Arctic is home to many peoples, who possess diverse sets of needs.

constructing **[strong]{.mark}** institutional foundations for **[Arctic
policymaking]{.mark}** that reflects shared values and
priorities]{.underline}. As we work to define our own ethnic and
national identities, [we should seek to locate these within a wider
Arctic identity]{.underline}, one that can [enrich]{.underline} --
rather than overshadow -- our [localised values]{.underline}. Such a
multifaceted, [**[pan-Arctic]{.underline}**
**[policymaking]{.underline}**]{.mark} platform could
[[strengthen]{.mark} our [ability]{.mark} to [engage with]{.mark}
non-Arctic actors]{.underline} ([including]{.underline} metropolitan
interests within **[[states]{.underline}]{.mark}** [that possess Arctic
territory]{.underline}), which are both [affecting and affected by
changes taking place within the Arctic]{.underline}. This contrasts with
current regional bodies and institutions, which often take a broad
state-centric perspective (for example, the Arctic Council and the
Barents Euro-Arctic Council) or a narrow focus on Indigenous interests
(for example, the Inuit Circumpolar Council and the Indigenous Peoples'
Secretariat): Both such perspectives are necessary and important, but
the former places agency in the hands of the metropole while the latter
risks creating false divisions between Arctic communities, erroneously
grouping the interests of Arctic non-Indigenous peoples with those of
the metropole.

Arctic economic, societal, cultural, and political development must be
led by Arctic peoples themselves, working to develop peaceful and
constructive relationships at the community, state, and other levels. A
sense of a shared stake in the Arctic can even help foster better
relationships between Indigenous peoples and former colonisers,
supporting a decolonial Arctic politics that regards all Arctic peoples
as equal -- understanding the consequences of historic injustice without
perpetuating ethnic and political animosity. Understanding today's
Arctic and envisioning a future Arctic requires acknowledgment that,
across much of the North, "the institutional and ideological
interference of the colonial period directly or indirectly, subtly or
overtly, continues to influence political thought and economic agendas"
(Nadarajah and Grydehøj 2016: 442; see also Grydehøj 2016).

[[These **must not**]{.mark}, however, [be **decisions made by a
local**]{.mark} **[elite]{.mark}**]{.underline}. It is
[important]{.underline} that the [entire]{.underline}
[population]{.underline} of the Arctic be [involved in the move toward
sustainable]{.underline} regional [development]{.underline} and come to
[understand the benefits to **collective action** and **collective

31.3 Arctic Research and Education

This kind of [regional awareness and bottom-up
[policymaking]{.mark}]{.underline} [[requires]{.underline}]{.mark}
enhanced knowledge on the part of Arctic peoples and an **[[improved
connection between science and policymaking]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
[Education]{.underline} is [key]{.underline} to this, with actors such
as UArctic working to [build local capacity through
education]{.underline}. Yet it is important to be conscious of
neocolonial forces within education systems and to prevent the
development of an Arctic cultural elite that is distanced from knowledge
of ordinary communities across the region. Development planning is
increasingly driven by systemitisation, data collection, and
technocratic discourses, which often combine to privilege metropolitan
interests and expertise, leading to local suspicion of development and
planning in general (Pugh 2013, 2016).

Without denying the importance of 'evidence-based policymaking', it is
necessary to recognise that, in today's world, not all kinds of evidence
are treated equally. In particular, the experiences of Arctic Indigenous
peoples, who have centuries or millennia of experience living in close
interaction with nature, can contribute lessons in flexible livelihoods
and adaptability to harsh and changing environments (e.g. Pearce et al.
2015). [**[Climate change]{.mark}**, for example, will have
[serious]{.mark} and possibly even [disastrous repercussions]{.mark} in
some parts of the world, but this [change]{.mark} will [need to be
**dealt with**]{.mark}, and its effects on Arctic environments and
peoples may not be straightforwardly negative]{.underline}. It is vital
that Arctic policy breaks free from the crippling strictures of
neoliberal risk, vulnerability, and resilience discourse (Evans and Reid
2014). [**Scientific [research]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **[evidence-based
policymaking]{.mark}** that is [sensitive]{.mark} [to Arctic
conditions]{.mark} may [reach]{.mark} quite [different]{.mark}
[conclusions]{.mark} [than]{.mark} research and [**policymaking** with a
metropolitan orientation]{.mark}]{.underline}. The scientifically and
technocratically motivated drive toward 'modernisation' asks Arctic
Indigenous peoples to submit to a developmental model that has proved
environmentally calamitous on the global scale and that has been applied
elsewhere with such poor results in earlier periods of decolonisation.
Involvement of Arctic peoples in Arctic research is a necessary but
insufficient condition for truly locally oriented research: The
framework, expectations, and design of the research must itself be
decolonised.

[[Arctic peoples]{.mark} [require]{.mark} an emancipatory and decolonial
[system]{.mark} of research and education]{.underline}, which
[[neither]{.mark} [turns its back on]{.mark} knowledge and cutting-edge
[scholarship]{.mark} [nor]{.mark} find itself [trapped]{.mark}
[in]{.mark} preconceived [notions]{.mark} [regarding]{.mark} the

[[subjects]{.underline}]{.mark}. **[[Scientific]{.underline}]{.mark}**
**[[research]{.underline}]{.mark}** itself -- from the humanities,

[**[Greater investment]{.mark}** [in]{.mark} Arctic [science]{.mark} is
**[critical for achieving Arctic sustainability]{.mark}** -- but [only

[communities.]{.mark}]{.underline}

cultural distinctions from one another, which produces a rich seam of

31.4 Towards an Arctic Awakening

Global change is producing new challenges for peoples of the Arctic, but
it is also bringing us closer together. We cannot simply draw the line
at the individual community, ethnic group, nation, or even state: It is
vital for us all to get engaged with the shared Arctic project. By
grasping the necessity of change, we can enter a new era of regional
stability, shared purpose, and political power.

**[Change is coming.]{.underline}** We may find it daunting, yet [it is
up to us, the peoples of the Arctic, to say:]{.underline} [**We will
no** **longer be passive observers** of our development but **will take
charge of our futures.**]{.underline}
