### AT: Empathy/Solidarity/Universal Values/Etc Bad

#### The [premise]{.underline} that it is [possible]{.underline} and [desirable]{.underline} to formulate universal standards of right and wrong is a [prerequisite]{.underline} to both [coherent ethics]{.underline} and [effective politics]{.underline}.

**Ruti, 17**---Distinguished Professor of critical theory and of gender
and sexuality studies at the University of Toronto (Mari, "The Ethics of
Precarity: Judith Butler's Reluctant Universalism," *Remains of the
Social: Desiring the Postapartheid*, Chapter 4, pp. 106-113, dml)

I agree that self-assertion can take place at the expense of others. And
I agree that the fantasy of sovereignty can promote contempt not only
for others but also for alternative, more relational modalities of
being. But [I am **not convinced** that the subject who seeks to
recentre itself is **automatically sadistic** and
**cruel**]{.underline}, [driven to **annihilate** the
other]{.underline}. Indeed, looking back, [[one might ask whether
feminism]{.underline}]{.mark}, [the [civil rights]{.mark}
movement]{.underline}, [[anticolonial struggles and resistance to
apartheid would have been **possible**]{.mark} if those involved had
decided that autonomy and self-determination are **inherently
malevolent**]{.underline}. Moreover, [I wonder whether such initiatives
would have been possible [without **a priori values**]{.mark} such as
**freedom** and **equality**]{.underline} -- values that arguably hark
back to the very Enlightenment that Butler tries so hard to bury.

Interestingly, Butler's resistance to a priori values, even ones derived
from the Enlightenment, dissipates in the context of her critique of
Israeli state violence against Palestinians, for she argues that
Palestinians have the right to basic rights, such as not to be
dispossessed of land, due to their membership in a global human
community. Regarding Palestine's claim 'to the lands that rightfully are
its own', Butler writes: 'One could formulate the right in light of
international law or on the basis of moral and political arguments that
may or may not be framed within a specific version of the nation-state'
(Parting Ways 205). I agree with this stance: I also think that
Palestinians should have basic human rights regardless of whether or not
they belong to a nation-state. Yet there is nothing about Butler's
Levinasian vision that supports her sudden turn to the kind of liberal
rights-based cosmopolitanism that can be traced, through Hannah Arendt,
all the way back to Immanuel Kant. If Butler stayed faithful to her
Levinasian approach, she would not be calling for equal rights for
Palestinians but rather saying that self-preservation should not be a
priority for them, that, indeed, there might be something profoundly
unethical about their quest for sovereignty and self-determination.
Butler makes a valiant effort to show that her cosmopolitanism is not
the same as that of Kant by arguing that it is precarity rather than the
integrity of the autonomous self that is the foundation for equal
rights. But this does not change the fact that, in this instance, she
falls back on the very system of Enlightenment morality that she has
spent much of her career criticising, so that all of a sudden equal
rights are all the rage. One could of course argue that the
Enlightenment does not own the ideal of equal rights, that it is
possible to think about equal rights beyond their humanistic context, as
Jacques Derrida does when he claims that 'what remains irreducible to
any deconstruction' is 'an idea of justice -- which we distinguish from
law or right and even from human rights -- and an idea of democracy --
which we distinguish from its current concept and from its determined
predicates today' (74). But Derrida's elusive definition of justice and
democracy is not what Butler is working with when she, in a Kantian
vein, calls for international laws that recognise the rights of
individuals not on the basis of their attachment to nation-states but
rather on the basis of their humanity.

It seems to me that Butler cannot have it both ways. If she is going to
recoil from any mention of the Enlightenment, as she consistently does,
then she cannot use its values whenever these happen to suit her
political purposes. Nor is it true that we can simply just discard a
priori normative limits. [There are situations where]{.underline} all of
our **[talk]{.underline}** about human precariousness **[will not get us
very far]{.underline}**, [where normative judgements are
**indispensable**]{.underline}. One of the limitations of posthumanist
[theory]{.underline} is that it [places so much emphasis on
deconstructing normativity that it tends to **forget** that while
there]{.underline} certainly [are normative limits we need to criticise
and transgress]{.underline}, [there are others we need to **endorse**
and **treasure**]{.underline}. As Dominick LaCapra explains with his
characteristic levelheadedness, [there are some]{.underline} normative
limits '[you might want to place in **question**]{.underline}, [some you
may want to **reform**]{.underline}, [and others you may want to **test
critically** and perhaps **validate**]{.underline}' (154). That is,
[[not all]{.mark} normative limits [are
**diabolical**]{.mark}]{.underline}: [there are [some]{.mark} that
[are]{.mark} **by far** the **best**]{.underline} ([or even [the
**only**]{.mark}]{.underline}) [[way to **check the abuses of
power**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [There are times when we need [to **make
decisions** about right and wrong]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} to
**[act accordingly]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Whether we are talking about
a man aiming his gun at Norwegian youth, or about a dictator aiming his
genocidal rage at segments of his own population, [[we need
**universal**]{.mark}**ly applicable [principles]{.mark}**]{.underline}
-- normative limits -- [and the fact that we might never be able to
agree fully on their parameters **does not diminish** the urgency of our
desperate need for them]{.underline}.

[Nor is there any reason to reject a priori norms]{.underline}, as many
poststructuralist thinkers seem to do, [on the basis that they are
metaphysically founded]{.underline}, [for it is **perfectly possible**
to derive a set of a priori principles from provisional social
agreements]{.underline} -- agreements [[that remain open to]{.mark}
**constant re-evaluation** and **[revision]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As
the feminist philosopher Amy Allen has argued persuasively, [a priori
norms are always historically specific in the sense that they arise in
particular social contexts]{.underline}. [This]{.underline}, however,
[does not mean that they are **invariably worthless**]{.underline}; that
is, [the loss of metaphysical foundations for our normative systems does
not **automatically invalidate** them but merely reveals their
historicity]{.underline}. Any a priori set of principles, Allen
explains, is by definition 'our historical a priori', yet [rejecting
such principles wholesale]{.underline} '[would mean **surrendering
intelligibility**]{.underline}' (35).

VII Butler's resistance to normative limits explains, in part at least,
her conflicted attitude towards the universal implications of her ethics
of precarity. As I have emphasised, one of the strengths of this ethics
is that it offers a universally applicable ethical paradigm that is
grounded not in Enlightenment notions of sovereignty but rather in a
social ontology that foregrounds human relationality and vulnerability.
As we have seen, Butler explicitly acknowledges the universal reach of
her paradigm: [[the]{.underline}]{.mark} fact that precariousness is a
generalisable condition of human life. Yet her [[relationship]{.mark}
to]{.underline} this [universality [is
**ambivalent**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [even
**[reluctant]{.mark}**]{.underline}, no doubt [[because]{.mark} it is
**difficult** to talk about universality in the context [of]{.mark}
ethics without raising [the]{.mark}]{.underline} very [spectres of
**Enlightenment rationality** and **Western imperialism**]{.underline}
that she is trying to outwit. This ambivalence is particularly manifest
in Parting Ways, where Butler performs an awkward retraction of her
earlier rhetoric of generalisability by claiming that her emphasis on
shared human precariousness is a matter of pluralisation rather than
universalisation. Under pluralisation, she writes, 'Equal protection or,
indeed, equality is not a principle that homogenizes those to whom it
applies; rather, the commitment to equality is a commitment to the
process of differentiation itself ' (126). Speaking of suffering
specifically, Butler adds that, unlike universalisation, pluralisation
recognises that even though all of us are defenceless against suffering,
any given experience of suffering is so unique that the attempt to
compare various forms of suffering is bound to founder. If, as I noted
at the beginning of this chapter, Butler's earlier work included a
sustained effort to navigate the tension between universality and
singularity, she now presents this tension as more or less
insurmountable: 'If we start with the presumption that one group's
suffering is like another group's, we have not only assembled the groups
into provisional monoliths -- and so falsified them -- but we have
launched into a form of analogy building that will invariably fail '
(128, emphases added).

Butler's ambivalence about using the trope of universalism in Parting
Ways is understandable, for in this text -- which includes the
aforementioned critique of Israeli state violence against Palestinians
-- she is walking a tightrope between arguing, on the one hand, that the
Jewish history of exile, violation and dispossession should yield
insight into the experiences of exile, violation and dispossession of
others, including the Palestinians, and insisting, on the other, that we
should not conflate these two experiences. In other words, however
critical Butler is of Israel's policies toward the Palestinians (and she
is very critical), she wants to make absolutely sure that she cannot be
accused of claiming that these policies are akin to Hitler's National
Socialism; she obviously does not want to imply that 'Zionism is like
Nazism or is its unconscious repetition with Palestinians standing in
for the Jews' (29). One can certainly understand why the context of her
discussion calls for repeated disclaimers about strict analogies, and
why it therefore complicates the discourse of generalisability that she
has used more freely in her earlier books. The rhetorical challenges of
Butler's analysis in this text are formidable because she does not want
to downplay the specificity of the Nazi genocide, yet she seeks to build
a model of political responsibility that would recognise convergent
modes of dispossession; she wishes to acknowledge that 'there are
historically specific modalities of catastrophe that cannot be measured
or compared by any common or neutral standard' (29), yet she also
strives to leap from one history of oppression to another. As she
maintains: 'In thinking about the history of the oppressed, it seems
imperative to recognize that such a history can and does apply to any
number of people in ways that are never strictly parallel and tend to
disrupt easy analogies' (100). Butler, in short, wishes to translate
divergent experiences of suffering into claims about justice without
thereby implying that the experiences in question are equivalent.

I understand why Butler insists that histories of dispossession can be
convergent without being equivalent. I agree with her resistance to the
kind of universalisation that erases important distinctions. And I would
never endorse an ethical model where one history of suffering would
negate another, where the specificity of suffering would be lost. Yet
[there is]{.underline} also [something]{.underline} [**unconvincing**
about Butler's]{.underline} sudden [attempt to **replace**
universalisation with pluralisation]{.underline}, [and]{.underline}
particularly about [her [claim that]{.mark}]{.underline}, [when it comes
to experiences of suffering]{.underline}, [[analogies]{.mark}
will]{.underline} '**[invariably [fail]{.mark}]{.underline}**[']{.mark}.
[[Given that the ability to draw analogies]{.mark} between different
forms of suffering [constitutes the]{.mark} **very [crux
of]{.mark}**]{.underline} her **[[ethics]{.underline}]{.mark}** of
precarity, [it is **difficult** to see how this ethics can **survive**
the collapse of this ability]{.underline}. If anything, it seems that
[[this]{.mark} collapse [would]{.mark} **instantly [undermine]{.mark}**
[the **most radical potential**]{.mark}]{.underline} of Butler's ethics,
namely [[its ability to **compete** with other universalising
paradigms]{.underline}]{.mark}, [such as transcendental Enlightenment
ones]{.underline}. [Nor does]{.underline} Butler's [hesitation about
universalism seem **theoretically necessary**]{.underline}, [for drawing
an analogy **does not cancel out** the distinctiveness of the entities
being compared]{.underline}. As Butler herself postulates in Precarious
Life: 'When analogies are offered, they presuppose the separability of
the terms that are compared. But any analogy also assumes a common
ground for comparability, and in this case the analogy functions to a
certain degree by functioning metonymically' (72). Exactly. If I draw an
analogy by saying that you and I both have two eyes, this does not mean
that our eyes are therefore identical: my eyes will still be blue while
yours will still be brown. But what is important is the understanding
that if someone throws acid in our eyes, we will both scream. Likewise,
[if I say that bodily vulnerability is something that you and I
**share**]{.underline}, [I do not mean to suggest that we experience
this vulnerability in the **same way**]{.underline}.

[A universalist ethics]{.underline} of precarity [does not demand a
**similarity** of experiences but merely that we are able]{.underline}
to recognise [**points of contact** between different
experiences]{.underline}. Let us recall that [even the most banal forms
of universalism]{.underline}, such as the liberal rhetoric of 'different
but equal', [do not ask that we all have the same experiences but merely
that]{.underline} -- as human beings -- [we possess the capacity to
**recognise the correspondences**]{.underline}, the often quite abstract
resemblances, [between different experiences]{.underline}. When it comes
to suffering, for instance, [universalism does not presume that my
suffering is like yours but merely that I am able to **draw a
parallel**]{.underline} between your suffering and mine. In
[this]{.underline} sense, Butler's [fear that universalisation is
**intrinsically homogenising** seems]{.underline} somewhat
**[misplaced]{.underline}**, and in fact directly contradicts her own
statements elsewhere in Parting Ways, such as the following: 'It is only
possible to struggle to alleviate the suffering of others if I am both
motivated and dispossessed by my own suffering' (127). Likewise, Butler
explicitly posits that one history of suffering provides 'the conditions
of attunement to another such history', so that 'one finds the condition
of one's own life in the life of another where there is dependency and
differentiation, proximity and violence' (130). I have already expressed
my reservations about the idea that ethics should be based on something
as unreliable as my capacity to be moved by the suffering of others. But
[if]{.underline} such an [ethics is going to **work at
all**]{.underline}, [[we must **presuppose**]{.underline}]{.mark} that
the common experience of precariousness provides [[a grounds
for]{.underline}]{.mark} translating from one experience to another in
ways that open to a degree of
**[[universalization]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [that though each human
life is unique]{.underline}, [there is a kernel of sameness [that
makes]{.mark} **identification**]{.underline} ([and therefore
**[ethical]{.mark} indignation**]{.underline}, [[**outrage** and
**action**]{.underline}]{.mark}) [[possible]{.underline}]{.mark}.

VIII\. This attempt to sort out the relationship between the universal
and the particular, the generalisable and the singular, the potentially
equivalent and the unique, surfaces strongly in the context of the
concept of global apartheid that the editors of this volume foreground
in their introduction. Like the relationship between Israel and
Palestine on which Butler focuses, South African apartheid is one of the
sorest spots in recent world history, frequently discussed with an
emphasis on its exceptionality. Can it, then, be raised to an emblem of
racism, inequality, suffering and poverty outside its original context?
Can analogies be drawn between its oppressions and more global
oppressions without losing the distinctiveness of the South African
experience? Achille Mbembe seems to believe so when he remarks that the
student protests that have recently rocked South African campuses 'can
be felt afar, in a different idiom, in those territories of abandonment
where the violence of poverty and demoralization have become the norm'.
Michael Hardt and Antonio Negri suggest the same when they assert, in
Multitude, that in 'the global Empire today, as it was before in South
Africa, apartheid is a productive system of hierarchical inclusion that
perpetuates the wealth of the few through the labor and poverty of the
many' (166--167).

The volume editors find much that is productive about such attempts to
draw comparisons, not least because they recognise that [insisting
on]{.underline} the **[[specificity]{.underline}]{.mark}** of South
African apartheid [[makes it]{.mark} **all [too easy]{.mark}** for
Western powers [to **disavow oppression**]{.mark} on their own
doorstep]{.underline}, [to insist that systematic segregation is **not a
problem**]{.underline} '**[over here]{.underline}**' -- say, [in
the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates --
[in the same way as it is]{.underline} '**[over there]{.underline}**'.
[[The]{.mark} emphasis on]{.underline} **[[global]{.underline}]{.mark}**
apartheid [[is **effective** in rendering this]{.mark} type of disavowal
**[impossible]{.mark}**]{.underline}. At the same time, the editors
point to an important pitfall in drawing the analogy between South
Africa and global apartheid too carelessly, namely that this can enable
the global West to use South Africa as a screen upon which to project
its optimistic fantasy that since apartheid was dismantled in South
Africa, it can also be easily dismantled globally. The editors note that
such a fantasy is particularly pernicious in light of the fact that the
end of South African apartheid coincided 'with South Africa's entry into
the neoliberal order of global capitalism' (15). Even though this state
of affairs merely rearticulates some of the very pillars of inequality
upon which apartheid was built, it is expedient for supporters of
neoliberalism who wish to promote global capitalism as an antidote to
the world's problems, including the problems of 'global apartheid'. This
is why the editors reject the kind of facile globalisation of apartheid
that allows the West to engage in hypocritical gestures of self-critique
without actually doing anything to alleviate global inequalities.
Instead, the editors advance a concept of global apartheid that
acknowledges that apartheid 'stretched back to the dawn of modernity and
to the beginning of European colonialism'. In other words, if it is
convenient for Western powers to think of apartheid as a malaise that
started in South Africa and has now somehow suddenly spread to the rest
of the world, the editors ask us to admit that global apartheid reaches
back at least as far as René Descartes and 'the division, the
separateness, the apartness, of mind and body, and the subjection of
body to mind that he inaugurates' (18).

### AT: Nation-States Obsolete

#### Nation-states [inevitable]{.underline}.

**Malešević, 23**---Full Professor and Chair of Sociology at the
University College, Dublin (Siniša, "The Many Faces of Nationalism,"
Nationalities Papers, First View, doi:10.1017/nps.2022.114, dml)

Only [**twenty years** ago]{.underline}, many scholars interpreted
nationalism as an ideology experiencing gradual and inevitable decline.
Social and political [theorists]{.underline} from Ulrich Beck and
Anthony Giddens to David Held and Zygmunt Bauman [were **adamant** that
globalization had **dissolved** the borders of nation-states and
that]{.underline} "old style [nationalism]{.underline}" [had become
**obsolete**]{.underline} (Giddens Reference Giddens2002, 18). Even as
an astute thinker as Hartmut Rosa (Reference Rosa2013, 203--4) could not
escape this type of reasoning, as he wrote about the ever increasing
"denationalization" of societies and the "loss of the national state's
function in the age of globalization." Today, the focus has shifted in
the opposite direction and many scholars speak about the resurgence,
revival, return, renewal, or re-emergence of nationalism (Tamir
Reference Tamir2019; Hazony Reference Hazony2018; Judis Reference
Judis2018). For example, Tamir (Reference Tamir2019, 3) writes how "the
reemergence of nationalism has taken the world by surprise" while Hazony
(Reference Hazony2018) points to the "return to nationalism" that is
exemplified by "the British vote for independence and a nationalist
revival in the United States." The 2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine has
further intensified this view of nationalism's "re-emergence."

However, there is nothing new to these types of pronouncements, as
[intellectuals have been **writing off** nationalism since its inception
and have]{.underline} then [just as often been]{.underline} [**regularly
surprised** by its]{.underline} "sudden" and "unexpected"
[periodic]{.underline} "**[revival]{.underline}**." From Rosa Luxemburg
and Karl Popper to Friedrich Hayek, political [[theorists]{.mark} have
**habitually [dismissed]{.mark}** [nationalism as]{.mark} a temporary
anomaly [bound to disappear]{.mark} soon]{.underline} (Malešević
Reference Malešević2019, 1--5). As [this **does not
happen**]{.underline}, many intellectuals express astonishment every
time nationalism becomes highly visible in public discourses. Hence the
focus usually shifts towards finding the immediate causes of something
that is in fact a longue durée phenomenon and, as such, requires
structural and historical explanations.

The classical scholarship on nationalism demonstrated a long time ago
that [[such]{.mark} dismissive proclamations [have **no foundation in
historical reality**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Gellner Reference
Gellner1983; Anderson Reference Anderson1983; Smith Reference
Smith1986). Nationalism developed quite late as a sociological
phenomenon, but [once it took hold of state power its growth was
**largely continuous**]{.underline}. [[Nationalist doctrine]{.mark} has
managed to [**replace** competing ideological discourses]{.mark} of
state legitimacy]{.underline}, [including the divine origins of
monarchs]{.underline}, [imperial creeds]{.underline}, [and
the]{.underline} notion of a [civilizing mission]{.underline}, among
many others. From the end of WWII, [nationalism has gradually attained
many **hegemonic features** [and has become]{.mark} the
**[dominant]{.mark} form** of political legitimacy]{.underline} in the
contemporary world. [In addition to **acquiring state power** and
**enveloping official institutions** and **non-state
organizations**]{.underline}, [this ideology and social practice has
**penetrated civil society** and]{.underline} the inter-personal
networks of **[everyday life]{.underline}**. The
[scholarship]{.underline} on everyday nationalism [clearly indicates how
**embedded** nation-centric thinking is]{.underline} our daily practices
(Fox and Iddriss, Reference Fox and Miller-Idriss2008, Skey and
Antonsich Reference Skey and Antonsich2018, Knott Reference Knott2016).
Hence [[nationalism **never** experienced a significant
decline]{.underline}]{.mark}. Instead, [over [the last **two hundred
years**]{.mark} one could [witness an **incessant
proliferation**]{.mark} of nationalism across the globe and within
different social strata]{.underline} of individual societies. As I have
argued before, [the historical trajectory of nationalism has been
[shaped by **nearly continuous**]{.mark}]{.underline} organizational,
ideological, and micro-interactional [[grounding]{.mark} **across** time
and space]{.underline} (Malešević Reference Malešević, Höhne and
Meireis2020, Reference Malešević2019, Reference Malešević2013). [The
**relative [invisibility]{.mark}** of this grounding]{.underline} and
the lack of aggressive language in the public discourse [have
[prevented]{.mark} many]{.underline} commentators [from **adequately
[assessing]{.mark}** [the actual strength]{.mark} of nationalist
penetration in everyday life]{.underline}. Thus, as [nationalism **never
went away**]{.underline}, [**nor** it was in **decline**]{.underline},
it could not experience a "re-emergence" or "return." [The recent
proliferation of aggressive nationalist discourses does not indicate
its]{.underline} "**[revival]{.underline}**," [it only makes ongoing
nationalist practices **more visible**]{.underline} in the public space.
In this sense, nationalism resembles the proverbial iceberg: many pay
attention to its tip when there is a threat of direct collision, even
though the iceberg has been there before and much of it remains
invisible to the human eye.

One of the reasons why intellectuals tend to regularly downplay the
significance of [[nationalism]{.underline}]{.mark} is that this
phenomenon [has **many faces**]{.underline}. Nationalism has protean
features [that]{.underline}, like a chameleon, [[can]{.mark} **easily
[adapt]{.mark}** to its surroundings]{.underline}. Scholars have
traditionally invoked the image of the Roman god Janus to pinpoint the
two contradictory faces of nationalism - as a force of collective
solidarity, but also as a mechanism of group aggression against those
that do not belong to the nation (Nairn Reference Nairn2005). However
nationalism has many faces -- [[it is]{.mark} a
**[multifaceted]{.mark}** political ideology]{.underline}, [a **habitual
form** of everyday practice]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} a **very
plastic** type of modern subjectivity]{.underline}.

[Nationalism is]{.underline} a **[flexible]{.underline}** discourse
[and]{.underline} social practice that [can coalesce with far right and
far left ideologies but [can]{.mark} also [accommodate]{.mark} moderate
[positions **across the**]{.mark} **political
[spectrum]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Nationalist ideas are the cornerstone
of such ideologically diverse movements as the far right Reconquete! in
France and Finns Party in Finland, and far-left groups such as the
Catalan Candidatura d'Unitat Popular or the United Socialist Party of
Venezuela. Similarly nationalist principles feature prominently in
center right parties such as the Conservatives in the UK, Forza Italia,
or the Croatian Democratic Union, and in the center left parties, such
as Scottish National Party, the Bulgarian Socialist Party, or the Danish
Social Democrats.

Even more importantly, [nationalism is not just a **political
ideology**]{.underline}, [[it is]{.mark} also [a **social
practice**]{.mark} that is [**integral** to]{.mark} many activities and
processes present in a **variety** of social organizations and outlets
of everyday life]{.underline}. For example, [nationalist practices are
often promoted by **religious organizations**]{.underline} (i.e. Serbian
Orthodox Church, evangelical Christian movements in the US, or the
Jewish Home organization in Israel.) Nationalist ideas can also underpin
**[civil society activities]{.underline}** (from ethnic minority NGOs
and cultural heritage associations, to national sport societies such as
the GAA in Ireland and Basque pelota clubs), **[private
corporations]{.underline}** (from selling distinct national products
such as BMW and Guinness, to promoting national tourist destinations
such as the Taj Mahal or Hagia Sophia), [and different **social
institutions**]{.underline} (such as masonic lodges, veterans'
associations, or even nursing homes.)

[Nation-centric practices are also **integral** to [everyday life and
habitual interactions]{.mark}]{.underline} between friends, family
members, neighbors, peers, and clan and kinship-based groups. For
example, wedding celebrations in the Balkans are often accompanied by
patriotic songs and the waving of national flags, while in Denmark
birthday parties regularly feature Danish national symbols, such as the
Danneborg. The intimacy of friendship is also often interwoven with the
shared experience of fervent cheering for one's national teams in
various sporting competitions. In this sense, [nationalism has become a
meta-ideological doctrine and social practice that
**infuses**]{.underline} many aspects of [everyday life]{.underline} in
the contemporary world. Hence to better understand this complex
phenomenon, it is necessary to explore these many faces of nationalism.

## Embodiment, Identity, In-Round Microaggressions, Etc Toolbox 

### Contingency---2AC

#### Racism's [contingent]{.underline}, not [structural]{.underline}.

**al-Gharbi, 24**---assistant professor in the School of Communication
and Journalism at Stony Brook University (Musa, "Mystification of Social
Processes," *We Have Never Been Woke: The Cultural Contradictions of a
New Elite*, Chapter 6, pp. 284-286, dml)

Moreover, [the]{.underline} largely [abstract [focus on]{.mark} social
[structure]{.mark} **conveniently [elides]{.mark}** considerations of
individual [agency]{.mark}]{.underline}. In truth, structure and agency
are deeply intertwined: as a result of social structure, [we all make
decisions under constraint]{.underline}. [Some have many more
constraints than others]{.underline}---particularly those who have
little financial or symbolic capital. The flip side, however, is that
[others have far more agency than everyone else]{.underline}, and their
decisions or behaviors often exert a disproportionate impact on the
possibilities available to others. Symbolic capitalists, again, must be
counted among this latter group. We are in a much stronger position than
most to influence the prevailing order. We often exercise our agency in
pernicious ways when we could do other wise. [Evoking]{.underline} "[the
system]{.underline}" [is often a way of **obscuring** this
fact]{.underline}. [By claiming to be **mere cogs** in the
machine]{.underline}, [**helplessly bound** by the prevailing
order]{.underline} just like everyone else, contemporary [elites
**implicitly absolve** themselves of any unique responsibility for
social problems]{.underline} ([or any unique obligation to make
sacrifices or changes to address those problems]{.underline}).

In a similar fashion, [[many]{.underline}]{.mark} contemporary symbolic
capitalists [[evoke]{.underline}]{.mark}
"**[[history]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[as]{.mark} a chief [cause]{.mark}
of contemporary injustices]{.underline}. [However]{.underline},
"**[[history]{.underline}]{.mark}**" **[[doesn't do
anything]{.underline}]{.mark}**. [The tendency]{.underline} of many
symbolic capitalists [to analyze contemporary injustices in historical
terms]{.underline} often [**obscures** how and why certain elements of
the past continue into the present]{.underline}. Discussing the
persistence of race ideology, historian Barbara Fields explained,
"[[Nothing]{.mark} handed down [from the past could keep race alive if
we did not **constantly reinvent**]{.mark} and **re-ritualize**
[it]{.mark} to fit our own terrain]{.underline}. If race lives on today,
it can do so only because we continue to create and re-create it in our
social life, continue to verify it, and thus continue to need a social
vocabulary that will allow us to make sense, not of what our ancestors
did then, but of what we ourselves choose to do now."49

[[Present-day]{.mark} racial [inequalities are **not some inevitable
by-product** of]{.mark}]{.underline} America's history of
[[slavery]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[Jim Crow]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and
so on]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[They are]{.underline}]{.mark} instead
[[reflections of people **today**]{.underline},]{.mark} here and now,
[taking actions that systematically favor certain people over
others]{.underline}. [Consider racialized differences in **home
appraisals**]{.underline}: studies consistently find that real estate
agents value the same home much differently depending on the race of the
seller.50 [This tendency is not a mere product of]{.underline}
"**[history]{.underline}**." In fact, far from being a holdover of the
past, [racial gaps in property appraisal have **grown
dramatically**]{.underline} since the 1980s.51 Large cities across the
United States were more segregated in 2019 than they were in 1990.52
Obviously, [these recent trends are **not**]{.underline} going to be
[**well explained** by appeals to slavery]{.underline}, [Jim
Crow]{.underline}, [historical redlining]{.underline}, [and so
forth]{.underline}. [The growing gaps are instead **overwhelmingly** a
function of **contemporary practices**]{.underline}. [Although appeals
to]{.underline} America's racist and sexist [history are often portrayed
as some kind of **critique** of the social order]{.underline}, instead
[they often serve as an **alibi**]{.underline}: it's not we who are to
blame, but those terrible people in the past (who are all conve niently
dead and therefore unable to be held to account).

"Historical," "[structural]{.underline}," "systemic," or "institutional"
[narratives]{.underline} also tend to [present an **overly mechanical**
and **deterministic** picture of the world]{.underline}. In truth,
[[events are]{.mark} often **[quite contingent]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
[[Societies and institutions are]{.mark} **highly
[dynamic]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As sociologist Andrew Abbott
emphasized, it is actually a much harder puzzle to explain continuity
than change.53 [[Change]{.underline}]{.mark} happens all the time. It
[[is **literally inevitable**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[People cycle in
and out of institutions]{.underline}]{.mark}, [geographic
[areas]{.mark}]{.underline}, [relationships]{.underline}, [[and
life]{.mark} altogether]{.underline}. [[Individuals learn and
grow]{.underline}]{.mark}. [The physical environment is constantly
evolving]{.underline}. [New [ideas]{.mark}]{.underline},
[practices]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} [tech]{.mark}nologies [are
constantly]{.mark} being [developed]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[Resources
are]{.mark} constantly being extracted]{.underline},
[transformed]{.underline}, [and [shifted around]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[Money is constantly changing hands]{.underline}. [[Actors
external]{.mark} to a particular society]{.underline} or institution
[are constantly attempting to [shape the world]{.mark} in the service of
their own ends]{.underline}, [influencing the possibilities and
incentives for others]{.underline}. [Structural]{.underline} or cultural
[persistence]{.underline} in the face of this constant churn and,
especially, [across generations and cultural contexts doesn't
just]{.underline} "**[happen]{.underline}**." It's an accomplishment.

[When we analyze how systems and institutions operate in **concrete**
terms]{.underline} rather than just waving our hands at "the system,"
"history," or related abstractions, [we can see that inequalities within
institutions tend to be produced and sustained by **everyday practices**
in **local contexts** rather than by forces outside one's
control]{.underline}.54 Likewise, [it is in discrete behaviors and
interactions among actual people in concrete places at particular points
in time that abstractions]{.underline} like "race" or "gender" or
"class" or "sexuality" [express themselves]{.underline} "in the world."
[To the extent that the [macro forces]{.mark} and abstract
notions]{.underline} that sociologists focus on **[[exist]{.mark} at
all]{.underline}**, [it is [**only** through people enacting them in
**local contexts**]{.mark}]{.underline}.55 And [agents typically have
**many degrees of freedom** with]{.underline} respect to [if and how
they enact a social order]{.underline}. However, [[**cosmetically
radical** narratives about]{.underline}]{.mark} "systems,"
"**[[structures]{.underline}]{.mark}**," and "history" [are often
mobilized by symbolic capitalists to **[absolve]{.mark}** us of
[responsibility]{.mark} for the choices we make]{.underline}---
[[through]{.mark} **hyperbolically [deterministic]{.mark}**
[narratives]{.mark}]{.underline} on the one hand, [[and]{.mark} **unduly
[pessimistic]{.mark}** [analyses]{.mark}]{.underline} on the other.

[According to some]{.underline} prominent accounts, [America is
**fundamentally racist**]{.underline}, [and it **always has
been**]{.underline}. "**[Progress]{.underline}**" [has been]{.underline}
largely **[mythological]{.underline}**. [Injustices are **so deeply
entrenched** and **pervasive** that nothing short of **revolution** can
rectify the situation]{.underline}. [Smaller ameliorative measures may
even be **counterproductive**]{.underline} insofar as they make a bad
situation more tolerable, thereby forestalling the dramatic change that
needs to happen.56 However, revolution does not seem to be on the
horizon in the foreseeable future, nor is there an obvious path from the
current status quo to a (leftist) revolution. Hence, it seems as though
the only thing that the clear-eyed can do is carry on as usual (albeit
with occasional pangs of guilt) and regularly condemn the system we
profit from even as we continue to actively exploit it. [Arguments like
[these are **superficially radical** yet **functionally
conservative**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [They [allow adherents to **feel

[present]{.mark} themselves [as]{.mark} deeply [committed to]{.mark}

[[reforms]{.underline}]{.mark}, whom they often mock), [[while
**neutralizing any**]{.mark} **apparent [obligation to do
anything]{.mark}**]{.underline} (beyond saying, thinking, or feeling the
"right" things). [Although]{.underline} the people advancing these
[arguments]{.underline} often [evoke]{.underline}
"**[systems]{.underline}**" [and]{.underline}

[thinking]{.mark}** [about concrete changes]{.mark} that could be made
to the system in order [to **ameliorate**]{.mark} social
[problems]{.mark}]{.underline}---[[let alone working through **specific
processes**]{.underline},]{.mark} mechanisms, [and]{.underline}
