and [Indigenous people in emerging fields such as green

[Policies]{.underline} must [ensure a significant portion of
Arctic-based projects are locally owned, **supported by tax incentives
f**]{.underline}or companies that prioritize local shareholders and
**[reinvest profits into communities.]{.underline}** Additionally,
**[grants and low-interest loans]{.underline}** [should be provided to

**[artisanal crafts]{.underline}**, and **[renewable

and ensure that important positions are held by people who are a part of
the community.10)

[Legislation should mandate that all major Arctic development projects
**publish detailed impact assessments** outlining affected territories,
resources, and communities.]{.underline} **[Regional
councils]{.underline}** comprising local community representatives,
[Indigenous groups, and youth must review and approve these projects to
ensure diverse perspectives guide decisions]{.underline}. Digital
platforms offering real-time updates on project goals and progress will
further enhance public scrutiny and feedback.

A portion of [revenues from Arctic resource **extraction and
development**]{.underline} [projects]{.underline} must be
**[reinvested]{.underline}** into [community infrastructure, education,
and cultural preservation initiatives]{.underline}. Additionally, a
regional investment fund managed by Arctic community representatives
should be established to support long-term projects that align with the
region's sustainable development goals.

By focusing on these **[concrete initiatives]{.underline}**, the [Arctic
can transition to a sustainable economy rooted]{.underline} in
[innovation and inclusivity]{.underline}. Transparent governance, local
empowerment, and strategic investments will ensure the region's growth
benefits its people and preserves its cultural and natural heritage for
future generations.

Holistic Wellness

There is a [lack of accessible, culturally-relevant, and socially
cohesive mental health and medical]{.underline} care [services in the
Arctic, particularly for Indigenous and marginalized
groups]{.underline}.11) This includes critical areas like mental health
support, cultural programs,12) housing assistance, employment aid, and
rehabilitation services.13) To address these gaps, mobile health units
and telemedicine platforms should be deployed to bridge distances in
sparsely populated areas. [Community health initiatives, such as
culturally informed **mental health workshops** and **wellness centers**
led by local practitioners, must be prioritized]{.underline}.
Governments and stakeholders [should provide funding for these services
to ensure they are tailored to the specific needs of Arctic
populations.]{.underline}

The [quality of and access to education (including **cultural ways of
education**]{.underline}) in the Arctic is [limited]{.underline}. This
[leads to people leaving the region to pursue their own
education]{.underline}, or the education of their children.14) To
improve educational outcomes and retention, [Arctic-specific training
programs for educators should be]{.underline} established, [focusing on
cultural safety, anti-racism, ethics, technology,]{.underline} and
[innovative teaching methods.]{.underline} Additionally, creating
partnerships with local communities to develop bilingual and culturally
relevant curricula can help [preserve Indigenous languages and
knowledge]{.underline}. Building satellite schools or offering hybrid
learning options can expand access to education in remote areas.

[**Climate change** poses **severe challenges** to **living sustainably
in the Arctic**,]{.underline} affecting health, safety, and cultural
practices.15) To combat these effects, [**substantial investments**
should be directed toward **community-led adaptation
projects**]{.underline} such as **[sustainable housing
design]{.underline}**s that [withstand harsh climates]{.underline} as
for example has been suggested in Qaanaaq in Greenland and [renewable
energy installations in remote]{.underline} [villages]{.underline}.16)
Establishing [Arctic-led research facilities on **emission reduction**
**and climate resilience**]{.underline} can [position the region as a
global leader in green technology]{.underline}. For example, wind and
solar energy projects tailored for Arctic conditions could reduce
dependency on imported fossil fuels, [while promoting local expertise in
green energy solutions.]{.underline}

The [**development of the Arctic** must honor its natural and cultural
values while creating new opportunities.]{.underline} Community
ownership in **[development projects,]{.underline}** such as [co-managed
renewable energy farms or cultural tourism ventures, can ensure that
benefits remain within the region]{.underline}. Young leaders must play
a central role in these efforts by participating in policy discussions
and contributing to knowledge production. [Initiatives like **leadership
fellowships** and **Indigenous youth councils** should be
**established** to **empower** the **next** **generation** to guide

### Cede the Political---2AC

#### The 1AC's constructivist framing of Indigenous peoples as pacifist and cooperative reinscribes colonial erasure. Embracing political engagement is key for effective Arctic de-colonialism.

**Reid 23** (Julian Reid, a Professor of International Relations at the
University of Lapland, Finland. Previously he taught at King's College
and SOAS (both University of London), as well as Sussex University.
9-18-2023, \"Deconstructing "Decolonization" in International

https://gjia.georgetown.edu/2023/09/18/martial-indigeneity-deconstructing-decolonization-in-international-relations/,
DOA: 7-15-2025//St. Marks Adam)

Constructivists [often criticize mainstream International Relations (IR)
theories for their colonial approach]{.underline} to the cultures,

[[constructivist]{.underline}]{.mark} [[approaches]{.underline}
[fail]{.underline}]{.mark} [[to]{.mark} [decolonize

[construction]{.underline} [of Indigenous peoples as embodiments of

[Constructivists]{.underline} [wrongly [portray]{.mark} the [Indigenous
as ultrasocial]{.mark} people]{.underline}

[[Many claim]{.underline}]{.mark} that [decolonizing]{.underline}

[Constructivists argue that "Indigenous politics is driving a

Indigenous peoples are adept at geopolitics and warfare

In his recent book, Indigenous Continent, historian Pekka Hämälainen
provides a detailed account of the military and geopolitical supremacy
that Indigenous peoples achieved across North America during their
17th-century conflicts with European settlers. With the advent of
settler colonialism, Indigenous peoples, such as the Iroquois and the
Sioux, outfought and outmaneuvered colonial forces from Spain, Britain,
France, and the Netherlands, adeptly using geopolitics to their
advantage.

[Indigenous]{.underline} [peoples waged war]{.underline}, however, [not
only against colonizers]{.underline}. The [[Inuit]{.underline} [of
Alaska]{.underline}]{.mark} [[waged]{.mark} vicious [wars on]{.mark}
their southern [neighbors]{.mark}]{.underline}, the Cree, for hundreds
of years until Cree acquisition of firearms from settlers ended the
series of conflicts. There has been strikingly little 'ultrasociality,'
to use Crawford's essentializing term, in Inuit-Cree relations. The
realities of such violent conflicts between Indigenous peoples undercut
constructivist representations of Indigeneity, which misrepresent the
complexities of Indigenous cultures.

[Western academics may avoid researching Indigenous warfare]{.underline}
[due to the fear that representing Indigenous peoples as violent or
warlike legitimizes colonial narratives. But as the anthropologists
Richard Chacon and Ruben Mendoza highlight, warfare was fundamental to
Indigenous cultures long before contact with Europeans. Indeed, they
claim that denying the prevalence of Indigenous warfare is itself "a
vestige of colonial repression--depriving native warriors of their
history of armed resistance."]{.underline} Indigenous [peoples should be
recognized for their courage and their prowess in fighting to defend
themselves. Contemporary racism denies these capacities.]{.underline}

Indigenous[\]." [Ignoring]{.mark} or denying the [existence of

[the [Indigenous damage]{.mark} [their interests]{.mark}]{.underline}

### Engagement=Possible---2AC

#### The Arctic is a site of collaboration, not exclusion, with indigenous people. examples of "arctic exceptionalism" redefines IR. 

**Fabbi 12** (Nadine C. Fabbi, Associate Director of the Canadian
Studies Center in the Henry M. Jackson School of International Studies
at the University of Washington. 11-26-2012, \"Inuit Political
Engagement in the Arctic\" ,Arctic Yearbook,
https://arcticyearbook.com/images/yearbook/2012/Scholarly_Papers/9.Fabbi.pdf,
DOA: 7-14-2025//Adam)

As a result of global warming, the Arctic is now a key focus for the
eight Arctic nation-states -- Canada, Russia, Denmark (Greenland), the
United States, Norway, Sweden, Finland and Iceland -- as well as many
non-Arctic states. In fact[, some scholars would argue that since the
Cold War the Arctic has become "the center of world politics" (Heininen
& Southcott, 2010: 4). By 2011, each of the Arctic nations had released
an Arctic or northern dimensions of its foreign policy clearly
illustrating the surge in geopolitical interest in the
region]{.underline}.1 Even the European Union and China are planning to
draft Arctic policies.2 [Although the Arctic was a key geopolitical
focus during the Cold War, the current situation differs in two
important ways. First, the [Arctic nations are]{.mark}, for the most
part, [committed to **collaboration** on]{.mark} issue-resolution and
[governance]{.mark}]{.underline} (Brosnan, Leschine & Miles, 2011;
Heininen, 2011; Heininen & Southcott, 2010; Keskitalo, 2004 & 2007;
Young, 2009 & 2011) [[including **active** **participation** in the
Arctic Council,]{.mark} a high-level intergovernmental forum, created in
1996 to foster Arctic cooperation. Second[, Arctic indigenous peoples
have **mobilized** **politically** and]{.mark} [**effectively** in the
last 30 to 40 years and now play a significant role in]{.mark} [Arctic
policy development]{.mark} [and decision-making at]{.mark} both the
[domestic and international levels]{.mark}]{.underline} (Abele & Rodon,
2007; Griffith, 2011; Koivurova, 2010; Shadian, 2010; Plaut, 2011;
Wilson, 2007; Wilson & Smith, 2011). The **[Arctic has become the
meeting ground for traditional state geopolitics and indigenous
diplomacies]{.underline}**. Heininen & Nicol (2007) call the
geopolitical reality in the Arctic today "some sort of renaissance of
regional co-operation by circumpolar indigenous peoples and civil
societies" (Heininen & Nicol, 2007: 161). [The [combination of a
collaborative approach to geopolitics]{.mark} [combined with the
participation of new actors]{.mark} on the world stage -- actors who
have distinct values and goals that are not nation-state-centered --
[may be contributing to a new approach to international
relations]{.mark} in the Arctic region]{.underline}. [In international
relations theory, [the nation-state has traditionally]{.mark} [been used
as the]{.mark} primary [unit for political analysis]{.mark}. Therefore,
traditional foreign policies reflect the interests of the nation-state
and prioritize national interests over community or individual security
or capacity. The [northern dimensions of foreign policy for the]{.mark}
eight [Arctic nations]{.mark} begin to [diverge from this tradition
**placing**]{.mark} **greater [emphasis on state
collaboration]{.mark}**]{.underline} (Heininen, 2011) [[including a
commitment to working closely with Arctic indigenous peoples to
address]{.mark} current and future [challenges to
communities]{.mark}]{.underline}. At the same time, Arctic indigenous
organizations, in particular [[the]{.mark} international Inuit
organization, the Inuit Circumpolar Council [(ICC)]{.mark}3 , are
[**challenging** nation-**state dominance**]{.mark} in international
relations [by reframing the Arctic as a region that transcends]{.mark}
[nation-state borders]{.mark} [and by asserting]{.mark} [their rights as
a people]{.mark}. Even mainstream media have noted the growing influence
of the Inuit]{.underline}. For example, in March 2010 The [Economist
published an article about how [the Inuit are influencing]{.mark}
natural resource [development]{.mark} in the Arctic noting, "although
they are only a small minority -- an estimated 160,000 of them are
spread across the Arctic -- [they have achieved a degree of
power]{.mark}"]{.underline} (para. 4). Parallel efforts are also found
at the national level particularly evident in recent efforts by the
national Inuit association in Canada, the Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami (ITK),
to redraw the map of Canada to ensure all Inuit benefit from domestic
northern policies. Both domestically and internationally Arctic
indigenous peoples are challenging the conventional concepts of
territory in favor of a regional understanding of the Arctic in an
effort to enhance their voice and influence in political affairs.

#### Despite small population sizes, the Inuit have achieved [effective]{.underline} political engagement .

**Duguid 20** (Danielle Duguid ,MA in Political Science and Anthropology
at the University of Albany 5-20, \"\"How Political Engagement Helps
Indigenous Communities in their Fight for Rights\" University at Albany,
https://scholarsarchive.library.albany.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1034&context=honorscollege_pos,
DOA: 7-14-2025//St. Marks Adam)

While there are difficulties for most minority groups in the political
realm, those that make up a small portion of the population have a much
more limited range of options for actions to take. For example, the
Mayans who make up a majority have engaged in international appeals in
the past with their advocate Rigoberta Menchú, along with ethnic voting
and local representation. This contrasts with the Inuit who have most of
their accomplishments through direct national or international appeals.
Following this trend, [it appears that smaller communities would be more
prone to choosing international appeals. While [work at the domestic
level]{.mark} [may be difficult]{.mark} for any indigenous group
regardless of population percentage because of discrimination, it is
virtually impossible [for smaller ethnic groups.]{.mark} This is not
because of formal barriers like restrictions of voting rights, but just
based on logistics.]{.underline} [However, **this [does not mean]{.mark}
that the [ways of political engagement]{.mark}** **[used by small
indigenous groups]{.mark} [are any less effective]{.mark}** than methods
of larger groups]{.underline}. While there is not a measure to quantify
effectiveness or success for these different methods in this paper, [the
international appeals that the Inuit favor seem to work
well]{.underline}. Partially [[because of]{.mark} work by [the Inuit,
Canada]{.mark} has [switched its position]{.mark} [from a non-signer of
the]{.mark} [U]{.mark}nited [N]{.mark}ations [Declaration on the
Rights]{.mark} [of Indigenous People to supporting the
declaration]{.mark}.]{.underline} The [Inuit have [also]{.mark}
successfully [managed to get seats on]{.mark} [international
organizations]{.mark} [and have]{.mark} been [a]{.mark} clear and
[ever-present voice at]{.mark} organizations like [the U]{.mark}nited
[N]{.mark}ations]{.underline}. In fact, it [could be argued that these
international measures are **more effective** than the domestic means.
Through creating a lot of noise, the Inuit have managed to [represent
themselves on]{.mark} an international panel, [the Arctic
Council]{.mark}]{.underline}. While there are still downfalls, such as
they have not secured voting rights in that organization, [the Inuit
still have succeeded in bringing their problems to an international
audience and manage to represent themselves while doing so]{.underline}.
The unfortunate situation is that often, forms of political engagement
only work when it is in the reigning government's interest to grant
rights to the indigenous population. This has often been a second
priority compared to economic or political development and is often used
as a PR stunt. While this reality creates doubt about the benefits of
attempting to fight back and become an advocate, it [still stands that
[any progress]{.mark} the group can make [would be beneficial]{.mark}.
With the use of domestic or international means of political engagement,
governments have a difficult time pushing groups to the side when their
voice is strong]{.underline}. The [benefits of working through domestic
efforts, like the Mayans and Quechua do, is that it is more difficult
for their governments to shut down these efforts without suppressing
democracy. While this has happened before, it calls into question human
rights abuses and creates another range of international problems for
the administrations.]{.underline} [[When groups make]{.mark}
[international appeals]{.mark}, it [brings attention into a
state]{.mark}, [which has resulted in them wavering and granting
requests]{.mark} (Coates & Holroyd, 2014). This has been the [case with
both Mayan and Inuit]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} [Through any of
these methods of political engagement, indigenous communities are
staring their governments down and demanding change]{.underline}. With
methods like local parties, voting, or international appeals, these
groups have succeeded in creating change to better their political
situation. [The **[best way to succeed is through]{.mark} legitimate,
[political means]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Even in situations where
oppression is widespread, or the percentage of population is very low,
these [cases prove that it is possible to use political engagement to
**create** **change**.]{.underline}

### AT: Arctic Governance Links

#### Writing off legal frameworks as products of colonialism prevents their usage to solve real world problems now. The EU can be used to protect the Sámi people. 

Anja MáRjá Nystø **Keskitalo** **and** Jacqueline **Wingens**
9-19-20**23**. Anja Márjá Nystø Keskitalo (she/her) is an Advisor at the
Saami Council's EU unit, and holds an MSc in Geography from the
Norwegian University of Science and Technology. Jacqueline Götze
(she/her) is a Political Scientist and Researcher in the Research
Programme „Inter- und transnational Cooperation" at the German Institute
of Development and Sustainability (IDOS). \"How to streamline Sámi
rights into Policy-Making in the European Union?,\" Arctic Institute -
Center for Circumpolar Security Studies,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/how-streamline-sami-rights-into-policy-making-european-union/.
苗

Potential of an EU internal Indigenous Peoples Policy

[Coloniality]{.underline} in an EU context is [often only understood as
a legacy of the EU Member States]{.underline} while the
**[EU]{.underline}** [itself is **framed** as a post-**colonial**
project]{.underline}. Following Hansen and Jonsson34) though, the EU
needs to address its own colonial legacy and present more critically.
[Although the authors focus on entanglements between the history and
present of EU integration and the African continent]{.underline}, they
still hold an [interesting angle for the case of the Sámi
People]{.underline}. An [EU internal Indigenous Peoples
Policy]{.underline} could [support]{.underline} in **[addressing
colonial legacies]{.underline}** [internally through strengthening
Indigenous Peoples' right to **participate in EU
policy-making**.]{.underline} Early in the policy cycle process, an EU
internal perspective featuring a rightsholder [concept in
consultation]{.underline} and [participation fora]{.underline} could
help to [counter current dynamics of "judge-made law and only applying
law]{.underline}"35) that often only in a last step decide on the
compliance of development projects with national and international law.
Furthermore, setting a rights-based standard and policy framework at the
EU level could [also assist in addressing the issue of capacity on the
side of Sámi organisations]{.underline}. By [including Indigenous
Peoples' representatives]{.underline}, in our case Sámi representatives,
**[early in policy-making processes]{.underline}**36) and by
[**facilitating** meaningful and **rights-based
participation**]{.underline}, policy [outcomes]{.underline} [run less
the risk of being perceived as unjust and can reduce the probability of
cases being brought to court]{.underline}. A relevant angle could be
[setting rights-based standards]{.underline} in an EU internal
Indigenous Peoples Policy on [permitting processes that could be
implemented at the national level]{.underline} to **[better
ensure]{.underline}** [Sámi rights to **self-determination** already at
a higher governance level]{.underline}. For instance, the [EU could
develop targeted guidelines on the implementation of
policies]{.underline} in the field of renewable energy and mining,
stronger safeguards for EU funding and decision-making in the European
Investment Bank and guidelines for Environmental Impact Assessments and
Strategic Environmental Assessments addressing Sámi rights. Moreover,
[the Commission could **establish dialogues** with the Member States to
**raise** **awareness** on **Sámi rights** to land and
resources.]{.underline}

Against the backdrop of insights and experiences from the local,
regional and national level regarding 'dialogues',37) these [dialogues
need to acknowledge Indigenous Peoples as rightsholders
different]{.underline} from [other actors to better protect Indigenous
Peoples' rights to self-determination]{.underline}. Although the EU
neither designs the processes in the national context nor permits
licences, it still has an [agenda-setting power]{.underline} [using
"moral discourses of climate change mitigation]{.underline}"38) through
developing overarching policy frameworks. Thus, applying a rightsholder
[concept]{.underline} in EU policy-making could also [**strengthen**
**Indigenous**]{.underline} [**Peoples'** **rights** throughout the
governance levels]{.underline}, [underlining]{.underline} the relevance
of an [EU internal Indigenous Peoples Policy as a
framework.]{.underline}

However, currently the EU policies concerning the Arctic and Indigenous
Peoples cannot achieve their full potential on both the goal of
decarbonisation and on respecting the rights of Indigenous Peoples.
[Through an EU internal policy, **rights cannot only be protected but
can thrive**.]{.underline} Such a [policy cannot only support
rightsholders but also stakeholders,]{.underline} Member States and the
EU in fulfilling the legal commitments and in actually acting on
Indigenous Peoples' rights and knowledge systems. [This also includes
acknowledging that consultations can lead to a decision not to proceed
with development]{.underline} [projects]{.underline}, securing space for
both Indigenous livelihoods and other developments. [EU decisions, even
if]{.underline} [applied nationally, regionally, or locally, strongly
impact the Sámi People]{.underline}. Due to the Sámi's rights, including
the right to self-determination, which need to be ensured by all actors
at different governance levels following inter alia ILO No. 169 and
UNDRIP, not only the nation-states but also the EU needs to consult with
the Sámi People on issues that affect their right to
self-determination[. Current examples from **Sápmi** demonstrate the
**significant impact of EU policies**]{.underline} on [Sámi livelihoods
and the ne**ed to properly protect Sámi rights** to self-determination
under the green transition]{.underline}. **[Sámi
rightsholders]{.underline}** all over Sápmi, whether in Fovsen, Giron or
elsewhere, [could be **better protected** by streamlining Indigenous
Peoples' rights into EU policy-making]{.underline}. However, such an
internal perspective should not be considered a panacea. We understand
it as [a **first** but relevant **step** because it could link existing
policies while stressing a rightsholder concept.]{.underline} An EU
internal Indigenous Peoples policy could also contribute to
**[addressing the EU's colonial legacies]{.underline}** [and design more
meaningful dialogues with Indigenous Peoples beyond the case of the Sámi
people.]{.underline}

Conclusion and Outlook

In this article, we presented the rightsholder concept as a potential
tool to streamline Indigenous Peoples' rights into EU policy-making.
[Court cases and insights from research with Sámi representatives who
participated in consultation processes at lower governance level
s]{.underline}how that the [stakeholder concept applied to participation
does not, in practice, protect the rights of Indigenous
Peoples]{.underline}. Sámi representatives do not assess "dialogue"
processes as meaningful, which in many examples leads to so-called
judge-made law when rightsholders go to court. Dialogues remain
superficial, one-sided and often do not take into account Sámi
Indigenous knowledge.

[For nation-states and the EU as a whole, these legal uncertainties can
hamper the implementation of the European Green Deal.]{.underline} For
the Sámi People, [these legal means are linked to long processes that
decide if and how Sámi rightsholders can continue with their traditional
livelihoods]{.underline}. We identify an **[EU internal Indigenous
Peoples Policy]{.underline}** as [relevant for applying a rightsholder
concept in consultations]{.underline} and other forms of participation
at the EU level. It [would help the EU to **address** its **colonial**
**legacies** and could even **influence** through its framework setting
power other governance levels]{.underline} by establishing rights-based
standards at the EU level. [Ultimately, it **is in all actors'
interests** to ensure such legal certainty and follow laws protecting
Indigenous Peoples' rights to self-determination]{.underline}. An [EU
internal Indigenous Peoples Policy could **facilitate more just** and
sustainable **policy outcomes.**]{.underline}
